The RED PAGE
The mystery of Williani Sharp, who was
also “ Fiona Macleod.;” may be partly cleared
up in the “Life,” by his widow, to be
published at the end of the year. Meanwhile
she has issued a selection of his Songs and
Poems, Old and New (Elliot Stock), written
while he was William Sharp between the years
1879 and 1905. The poetical part of Fiona
Macleod's work has already been published in
From the Hills of Dream. The Sharpend of the
dual personality was by far the bluntest,
as the poems in the above selection are
mostly tame and uninteresting. The speci-
mens here printed are the best of those
which are of quotable length. In justice to
the widow it must be mentioned that she
has not reprinted any of the dull stuff
written while he was on a visit here, and
selected by Sladen for his two Australian
anthologies. The widow mentions in a bibli-
ographical note that three of the poems she
has included appeared originally in the Fagan
Remew (1892), the first and only number
of a projected monthly review edited by
“W. H, Brooks ” —of which William Sharp
wrote every word from cover to cover under
the pseudonyms of the Editor and seven of
contributors. His work as “ Fiona Macleod ”
did not begin until 1893, the first publication
being Pharais, 1894 ; so the Pagan Review
may have been a preliminary effort of the
other self or selves to find expression.
Out of the valley of the Shadow of Death
Who cometh, through the haunted Hollow Land?
On those tired lips of mine whose quickening
breath,
In this long yearning clasp whose tremulous hand ?
O, is it death or dream, madness, or what
Fantastic torture of the chemic brain,
That brings thee here, as thus, when all forgot,
Thy body sleeps, as mine doth, free from pain?
What is the breeding word upon thy lips
O beautiful image of my heart’s desire ?
What is the ominous shadow of eclipse
That dusks those veiled eyes’ redeeming fire?
O soul whom I from life to life have sought,
What menace haunteth joy so dearly bought ?
This menace of remembrance that must come :
This menace : —of the waking that must be.
O soul, let the rhythm of life itself grow dumb
And be the song of dbath our litany :
Let the world perish as a perishing fire,
For us be less than ashes without flame,
So that we twain our last breath here suspire.
Here where none uttereth word, none calleth
name.
For in the Hollow Land is utter peace
The magic spell which hath no first or last:
But all that never ceaseth here doth cease '
And what would know no death is long since past:
Only one thing endures where all expire—
The inviolate rapture of fulfilled desire.
Australian Verso.
What is Australian Verse, anyhow ? An
English writer said some time ago that,
strictly speaking, the term Australian litera-
ture should be restricted to the literature of
the aboriginals, if there was any, for all the
rest was English literature produced in Aus-
tralia. Some of the alleged songs of the
aboriginals have been turned into English.
There are a couple in Dr. Lang’s Aurora
Australis (Sydney, 1826). The humorless
Horne gave a corroboree chorus in his South
Sea Sisters (Melbourne, 1866) :
From creek of Worooboomi-boo !
And sheep-run Woolagoola-goo !
Come Dibble-Fellow dancing in fog 1
All over mount Wooloola-yah !
And earth-holes of Worondi-wah !
Till he vanish in the yellow Wog-wog !
Etc., etc.
This sounds like something out of a bad
gollywog book, but was actually selected by
Sladen as a specimen of Australian Poetry.
It is, however, a shade more intelligible than
the samples of original blackfellow poetry
printed in Paterson’s Old Bush Songs. The
first two lines will be enough :
Korindabria, korindabria, bogarona, bogarona,
Iwariniang »,
iwaringdo, iwariniang, iwaringdo, iwariniang,
iwaringdo.
A footnote states : “I could never find out
what the words meant, and I don’t think the
blacks themselves know.” Very likely not.
Anyhow, it is clear that our dusky brothers
who have vanished in the yellow Wog-wog
have left no footprints in literature. The
Maoris have made a mark of some kind, fora
number of their songs in Sir George Grey’s col-
lection have been paraphrased into readable
English.
It has been urged locally that the adjective
“Australian” should be reserved for that
literature in English which has been pro-
duced by the white natives of Australia, just
as American literature is the product
of autochthonous Yankees. There is
hardly justification for this at pre-
sent, as the work of the native born
is not sufficiently distinguished from that of
the immigrant. America was colonised cen-
turies ago, and did nob begin to have a
separate national literature until after the
War of Independence. Even when De
Tocqueville visited the United States in 1835
he could not find one poet of high order,
and Stedman’s Poets of America, after a pre-
liminary gallop over the “ colonial ” writers,
begins with Bryant, who died in 1878.
What Stedman said about America’s early
days—“ All the drags, the anchorage, the
limitations involved in the word ‘ colonial ’
retarded a new ideality. It made the western
lyre a mechanism to echo without fresh and
true feeling notes that came from over sea ”
—applies with force to Australia ; only that
we are shaking off that restriction earlier and
without a war.
* •* * ■*
Let us accept the superior English person’s
definition for the moment. It would give us
the work of Gordon, Clarke, Boldrewood,
Brunton Stephens, Daley, Ogilvie and a good
many others. On the other hand, we lose Gil-
bert Murray, Haddon Chambers, Dr. Morri-
son, Frederic Manning and some others.
(Mrs. Humphrey Ward spent her first five years
in Tasmania—but we waive any claim to her.)
Artists, scientists, musicians, too, we lose.
The loss will increase until Australia is big
enough to provide a market for all the
artistic products of her children, for the
old hag on the Thames still sings with
all the compelling charm' of the Sirens.
The work of those Australians who have
left here has naturally been adapted to
the markets in which it was offered, and
little of it has any Australian character-
istics. And a good deal of work done here
by immigrants has no Australian flavor and
no intrinsic interest for Australians.
This definition, therefore, will not do un-
less it is qualified. And the necessary quali-
fication in its simplest form is—Australian
interest. Any artistic work done here by
Australians has that interest; so has the
work of anyone else which has been influenced
by life in Australia, even though, as in the
case of Daley, the influence is not obvious.
■* * * it
A collection of verse of this kind, believed
to be fairly representative, has been made in
The Golden Treasury of Australian Verse
(Angus and Robertson), which is really a
revised edition of An Anthology of Australian
Verse, published in 1906. The revision is
so extensive as to make it practically a new
book, and a new title was necessary. The
title does not imply any comparison with
Palgrave’s English or Knowles’ American
G.T.’s; and the word “golden” is dis-
tinctly modified by the word “ verse.” The
editor of this page does not feel called upon
to praise or abuse the selection made by
himself as editor of the book ; and, as he was
unable to see proofs, he does not hold him-
self responsible for two or three errors in the
text, nor for the presence of an “ index ”
which is merely a list of names.
Objection may be reasonably made to
stretching the term “Australian” so as to
cover Maoriland. But “Australasian ”is an
ugly mouthful ; and the people of the Com-
monwealth and the Dominion are so much
akin, the conditions of life so similar, that
the simpler word may be fairly applied to a
selection from the verse of both countries.
• * * *
The making of an anthology is a process
of stocktaking. The selection should enable
the reader to see at a glance, as it were, how
we stand in the poetry line, and what is the
value of our achievement. Over 20 years ago
Sladen started the business for Australia, and
put a pretty high estimate on the goods. That
valuation has not been sustained. Twenty
years hence a higher standard of values will
no doubt be applied, and a further reduction
made in what we may regard as assets to-day.
In the lower regions of verse interest dimin-
ishes with time. The preservative against
decay is style, and style is the vesture of the
imagination. An English critic who had
read some books of Australian verse com-
plained that what he read was lacking in
imaginativeness—there were no arresting
phrases, no haunting lines. That, you will
remember, was Matthew Arnold’s test for
great poetry. It is unreasonable to expect
great poetry from Australia yet. It is worth
while, however, trying to see whether there
are any lines of Australian verse which have
thp quality of memorableness. This would
mean taking stock in a much simpler way.
Will readers send in their selection of the best
lines by Australian or Maoriland writers—not
more than four lines in one quotation. The
name of the poem and poet to be attached to
each quotation. Contributions to reach here
before 18th November next. 10s. 6d. will be
forwarded to the sender of what is judged to
be the best quotation.
Francis Thompson
Francis Thompson has asked, in his essay
on Shelley (a most luminous piece of self-
revelation), why “ the poets who have
written for us the poetry richest in skiey
grain, are the very poets whose lives' are
among the saddest records in literature ? ”
We think of Thompson himself and echo
“Why?” There are few sentences in this
essay on Shelley, except those weakly ones
which attack Shelley’s atheism, that could
not be applied to Francis Thompson. He
reveals more of himself than he reveals of
the greater poet.
Thompson was born at Preston in 1863 or
1859, and was bred a Catholic at Ushaw
College. The son of a doctor, he was in-
tended to follow the paternal profession, but
he had a warmer love for dead languages
than he had of dead bodies, so he
took the unfilial course of becoming a poet.
This action appears not to have pleased his
father, and it preyed on his own mind and
body. For some part of his life he was a
starving derelict. He worked in a boot shop
and carried a bookseller’s collecting sack, till
verses he had sent to magazines brought him
friends. (So the fairies are not dead !) He
was looked after by these friends because his
health was not perfect, and he was taken
into the home of Mr. Wilfred Meynell,
whose children the poet honored with some
of his finest poetry, and whose wife is the
lady of “ Love in Dian’s Lap.” Mrs. Mey-
neil, a younger sister of the Lady Butler
who painted battles, is the subject of Thomp-
son’s most passionate and grief-stricken love-
poems, and though Mr. Wilfred Meynell
had beaten the poet for his girl, he has been
exceedingly generous to the lorn lover ever
since.
Thompson wrote for The Academy in its
best days, and, later, for TheAthenceum. He
published only three, and those small,
volumes of verse, namely, “Poems,” 1893;
“Sister Songs,” 1895 ; and “ New Poems,”
1897. His spirit, set in but a frail shell
from the first (this “ dear speck of flesh with
such daring wings ”) failed utterly on Nov-
ember 13, 1907. The world can expect no
other poet like him.
With Shelley he owes much to Shake-
speare—Shakespeare etherealised. Possibly
he is the last truly religious poet that we
can have, for the rare spirit that infuses his
work can seldom survive the tests of living
in these rushing days. He has said of
Shelley: “Any partial explanation will
break in our hands before it reaches the root
of such a power.” So it is only for the
present writer to indicate, and not to define,
the magic of this splendid poet, for he has
the gift of “abstraction vitalised” that is
the spring of the purest poetry. He is a
cowled ascetic, abasing self, and yet sun-
worship and the divinest Spring frenzies
uplift, and surge through his soul, while
there stirs in him “the babe of an unborn
calamity.”
... in all too late and frozen a day
I come in rearward of the throats of song
Unto the deaf sense of the aged year,
Singing with doom upon me.
His longer poems are regularly his best,
because his spacious images need room. The
magnificence of the “Orient Ode,” and the
“ Ode to the Setting Sun,” and the richness
of their language are not to be surpassed.
Haunting lines like
Spring is come home with her world-wandering
feet,
from “From the Night of Forebeing,” and
the fine abandonment of the last lines of
“ An Anthem of Earth ” :
Here I untrammel,
Here I pluck loose the body’s cerementing,
And break the tomb of life ; here I shake off
The bur o’ the world, man’s congregation shun,
And to the antique order of the dead
I take the tongueless vows : ray cell is set
Here in thy bosom ; my little trouble is ended
In a little peace.
—denote a deeper range of emotion and finer
command of language than any modern poet
possesses. And the tricky Celtic metre of
pieces like “ July Fugitive ”:
Can you tell me where has hid her
Pretty maid, July ?
I would swear one day ago
She passed by.
—show Thompson to be a true poet of fancy
as well as a poet of fine imagination. Song
and the consciousness of his song and of
the power of song is beside him always.
Wilfred Meynell, Thompson’s literary
executor, has issued a six-shilling selection of
the work which contains, first and foremost,
that most sublime, most undeniably-inspired
modern poem, “ The Hound of Heaven.” A
separate edition of “The Hound of Heaven ”
is sold at one and threepence. ‘ ‘ The Hound
of Heaven ” is Thompson’s climacteric poem,
because of the tremendous idea and the mag-
nificence of its statement. It is worth one
and threepence of any man’s money. It has
a sublime idea, and, for all the occasional
strangeness of Thompson’s vocabulary, a
sincere manner. It opens and closes splen-
didly. The language inevitably responds to
the idea and gives the thought its full value,
while the poem is just the right length.
“The Hound” might mean a lot of
things, but most of all it means the death-
lessness of the innate religious instinct of a
man. He sinned and ignored it, but it
would not entirely leave him ; it would not
let him go :
I fled Him, down the nights and down the days ;
I fled Him, through the arches of the years ;
I fled Him, down the labyrinthine ways
Of my own mind....
But with unhurrying chase,
And unperturbed pace,
Deliberate speed, majestic instancy,
They beat—and a Voice beat
More instant than the Feet—
“ All tilings betray thee, who betrayest Me.”
And it goes on for its two hundred lines or
so, with no faltering in the mood, and no
weakness in the word:
I triumphed and I saddened with all weather,
Heaven and I wept together,
And its sweet tears were salt with mortal mine;
Against the red throb of its sunset heart
I laid my own to beat
And share commingling heat:
But not by that, by that, was eased my human
smart.
And comes once more the urgent refrain:
Nigh arid nigh draws the chase,
With unperturbed pace,
Deliberate speed, majestic instancy,
And past those noised feet
A Voice comes yet more fleet—
“Lo, naught contents thee, who content's!; not
Me.”
And finally :
Halts bv me that footfall:
Is my gloom, after all,
Shade of His hand outstretched caressingly?
“Ah, fondest, blindest, weakest,
I am He Whom thou seekest !
Thou dravest love from thee, vvtio dravest Me.”
These quotations indicate the idea more
than the verbal beauty of the poem. There
are other good things in the selected poems,
but nothing so evenly great as “ The Hound
of Heaven.” For his richness of phrase
Thompson is a man to admire ; for his
courageous prosody, a man to respect. He
has the rare gift of being naive and man-
nered at the one time. This is from the
book of selections :
ENVOY.
Go, songs, for ended is our brief, sweet play;
Go, children of swift joy and tardy sorrow;
And some are sung, and that was yesterday,
And some unsung, and that may be to-morrow.
Go forth ; and if it be o’er stony way,
Old joy can lend what newer grief must borrow:
And it was sweet, and that was yesterday,
And sweet is sweet, though purchased with
sorrow.
Go, songs, and come not back from your far <Y ’
And if men a3k you why you smile ana par »
Tell them ye grieve, for your hearts know lo-
Tell them ye smile, for your eyes know
morrow.
Whether we believe in a man’s faith or 11 »
such a great belief as Thompson constant y
expresses with singular earnestness an
beauty of image and language, must com-
maad our admiration. The poems, in hi.>
last volume, grouped under the heading
“Sight and Insight,” and including such
fairy-like yet positive things as “ The Mas-
tress of Vision ” and “ Grace of the Way
I was translated and enskied
Into the heavenly-regioned She.
are the most delightful things that a man
can expect to get from another.
It is almost inconceivable how the frail
flesh shell of this man could contain the
trembling and forceful urgency that backs
these poems, and the man ye-t live for close
on 50 years and in circumstances not by any
means rosy. But what did it matter to him
if he sold matches or slept in the cold on
the Thames embankment ? He had on liis
tongue the magical word, and flaming in his
heart the eternal fire. He has the magic
that intoxicates and that draw's us with him
away from the world. His stately and deep-
thinking verse is caught with sudden lyric
bursts. “Give me song!” is his repeated
cry, for he feels that if he gets that he has
touched the secret of the cosmos and of man.
He has the pure vision and the ecstasy, but
his insight has made him mournful :
’Tis to have drunk too well
The drink that is divine,
* Maketh the kind earth waste
And breath intolerable.
He is without the modern superhuman cour-
age of George. Meredith, and his visions are :
Sweet with wild wings that pass, that pass away.
He is a mortal who needs the support of a
great belief into whose arms he falls half-
swooning, while his lips move with a
splendid utterance :
Meantime the silent lip.
Meantime the climbing feet !
via.
FURNLEY MAURICE.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Little Man.
Cherchez le petit homme.—New version of old proverb.
Climb down, ye six-foot braggarts,
Whose brains have run to brawn !
You had your day, and had your way—
Your day has come—and gone !
You still may be permitted
To live, the best you can,
As serfs and banner-bearers
For the Little Man.
He’s the cove to make things go,
He’s the Ruler of the Show,
He’s the sort of chap who smashed Afghanistan,
And the style of bloke forbye
Who turned Europe into pi
Is the bullet-headed, brainy Little Man.
While people are created
According to the Act,
If one is made a giant
He cannot help the fact;
But he should be contented
To know that all his clan
Were born to be the vassals
Of the Little Man.
When there is an axe to grind
You will nearly al ways find
He's the party ivith the axe since earth began ;
And since days of Goth and Vandal
The Big Man’s turned the handle —
It has always been his business—for the Little Man,
I heard a voice of thunder
Resounding in a bar :
It cussed and damned the British
About the Boer war.
“ If I were General Cronje,”
It said, “ their hides I’d tan ! ”
I recognised the speaker— .
’Twas the Little Man.
He's the Emperor of Skite ;
He's the Son of Dynamite ;
And his name is very chiefly Hooli-gan.
And his hair is often red — -
But when Hell has gone to bed
He is wide awake and lively, is the Little Man.
There never was Committee,
And never Council known,
Nor gathering, like Raad or Thing,
But he was its backbone.. .
' A fight was fought in Flanders,,
The Gallic army ran.
Who turned the fray ? Dumouriez —
The Little Man.
For he shamed the runaways,
As he piped the Marseillaise,
And they turned and charged like fiends Republican;
And before their fiery rage
Swept the foes of the new Age,
And Ca IRa the guns sang for the Little Man.
There are two little States that
Stand up like little men
Against God’s British Empire—
" “"One"man "have they to ten ; ~
And, though their doom is certain,
So also is the plan
Of Creedless Fate that, soon or late,
Helps the Little Man.
For, whatever Fatmen say,
He inbound to have his day,
And the dust is rising now before his van ;
And when his Army comes.
With its banners and its drums,
’Twill be wise to be a “ cobber ” of the Little Man.
CREEVE ROE.
The Elegant People.
Oh ! we are the Elegant People, well-tailored and
up-to-date
(But I hear a mysterious warder who ever parades
at the Gate !).
Aye, we are the Elegant People—the blood of the
kings in our veins
(But hark, there’s the scoff of the Warder, and
there is the clank of the chains !).
Oh ! we are the Elegant People (just lately re-
moved from the Ape),
And our pathway is littered with relics of revel
and ruin and rape ;
For the lust of the Ape’s in our nature, the Tiger
is loose in our blood,
Aye, we are Immaculate ever (who says we are
creatures of Mud ?).
We have planned—we have -pondered and pan-
dered ; we have travelled and triumphed—
how far ?
We have laughed at the Ape in derision, and
now we are—just what we are !
But our minds are the moulds of the Master’s,
our manners are many and mild
(Our instincts are warring within us—and yearn-
ing once more for the Wild !).
And can we be god-like and gentle ’—Depends on
the type of our god,
And the strength of our parts that are mental,
the length of the pathways we trod !
And can we be ape-like—untroubled by all the
pretences of Man ?
And though we be god-like or imp-like, I answer
this question, “ We can ! ”
Progression or grim Retrogression, it matters not
much in the end,
For the gods are our ardent advisers—the Devil
our trustiest friend !
But we are the Elegant People (the blood of the I
Beast in our veins !)
Lo ! I see the four walls of our prison—and I list
to the clank of the chains !
n.s.w:

RUBEROID
RED
ROOFING
IN A FIRE.
Mr* E, NEFF writes :
** A motor garage next
to my residence caught
fire. The house ad-
joining mine has a
shingle roof, and in a
few minutes it was a
mass of flame. My
house is roofed with
Red Ruberoid, and,
although burning
brands fell all over it
blazing merrily away,
the roof did not catch.
A close inspection
after the fire only
resulted in finding
a few places slightly
blistered.”

THE FREEDOM OF THE FUSED CONSCIENCE.
The Government has been holding a number of caucus meetings at which great efforts were made to compel the hostile minority to give its support to
the financial agreement arrived at with the States.
Deakin: “No man in this party is required to vote against his conscience; but if his conscience is against voting for my policy he can look out for trouble.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tim Bulletin would online the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods o f address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. 'To send them, to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin.” is the proper
superscription for “ copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 21, 1909.
THE PROBABLE REVULSION OF FEEL-
ING IN FAVOR OF PRIVATE ENTER-
PRISE RAILWAYS.
If there is anywhere in this world a blessing
which can’t be turned into a curse by villainous
administration the name and address of that
blessing ought be more widely advertised than
they are, for it must be a tough blessing and one
worth knowing. It isn’t more than once in 10
centuries that mankind drops on a grander idea
than that of State railway ownership, and yet even
that can be turned into a curse—in at least two
States of the Commonwealth (N.S.W. and Vic-
toria), and in a less degree in S.A., it has
already been so misused as to become a
very good semblance of one. This is no
reason why Australia should ever again dream
of private railway ownership, for private owner-
ship is apt to be a curse naturally and all
the time, while the State article has to be made
into a disaster by ferocious and misdirected
ingenuity ; but, all the same, there is danger that
a serious clamor for private ownership may arise
if the State doesn’t mend its ways. The State’s
right to make railways for the development of the
country necessarily includes its right to refuse to
make them, and its right to prevent other people
making them, and its right to make them so that
they will hinder rather than help the development
of the country. And when the management of
the State falls into the hands of a few narrow-
minded, pig-headed political savages, who regard
State railways solely as means towards the further
bloating of an overgrown State capital where
many of them have property, who deliberately
shut up rival ports lest they sjiould interfere
with that bloating process, who compel the
producer to pay freight on his goods for
twice or thrice the needful distance rather
than let him trade with any other place
than the bloated capital, ana who believe in
deliberately laying waste those parts of their own
State which, by reason of their remoteness, can't
trade with the bloated capital yet mustn’t trade
anywhere else—then a great political principle is
shamefully dragged through the gutter, and
brought into disrepute, ana made to smell like
last month’s fish.
■*■***
The case of Sydney is a notable example. In
order to concentrate there, as far as possible, all
the trade of over 310,000 square miles of country,
Tort Stephens, Jervis Bay and Twofold Bay have
been kept rigorously shut up. It was impossible to
shut up Newcastle, but then Newcastle is a poor,
accidental sort of harbor at the best, and even in
its case special railway rates have been devised to
attract Newcastle’s natural trade to Sydney.
Sydney is almost surrounded by water, so the
trade of 310,000 square miles has to approach it
by one bottle-neck entrance. The compression is
too great, and things have burst, yet everywhere
new railways are being built all pointing towards
Sydney, in order to increase the pressure and
make things burst still worse. There is talk of
millions of lambs, which were intended for export
this year, being left behind —possibly to perish in
the next drought—because they can’t get to Syd-
ney, and aren’t allowed to go anywhere else.
There are similar complaints about wheat
and wool being left to decay because they can’t
reach the only market which is allowed them.
The number of trucks allotted for the conveyance
of cattle to tire Sydney sales has been cut down
because of the congestion, and the person who has
cattle for sale might often just as well have no
cattle for all the chance he has of turning them
into money. The natural result is that the
price of meat keeps going up and up in Sydney,
and then foolish people say that Protection has
raised the cost of living. And meanwhile closer
settlement is being pushed on to increase the
block, and add seven more devils to the seven
devils of confusion that are already on the pre-
mises.
Under these circumstances the people of Finley
(N.S. VV.) and its neighborhood lately called on
Works Minister Lee. They represent
a hard case, but a very ordinary one.
Finley is 454 miles by railway from Syd-
nej% and is at the tail end of a branch
line. It is about 170 miles from Mel-
bourne, but it and the greitt extent of
country around it aren’t allowed to trade
with Melbourne because Melbourne is in
another State. The Victorian railway
comes up to the border —in fact, it comes
over the border to Tocumwal, on the
N.S.W. side of the Murray, and N.S. W. paid '
a share of the cost of building the railway bridge ;
but a gap of 12 miles, which could be filled in at
a cost of £30,000, is carefully preserved, in order
to prevent the trade of Finley, Berrigan, Jeril-
dene, Narrandera and other places thereabouts
taking the short, cheap road over that bridge
to Melbourne instead of the long, costly one
to Sydney. Again, these places are much
closer to Twofold Bay in N.S.W. than to
Sydney, but they aren’t allowed to trade with
Twofold Bay because it isn’t Sydney. Even Bom-
bala, when it gets its railway, will have to ex-
port and import via Sydney, 318 miles away, in-
stead of via Twofold Bay, 40 miles away. The
railway is being specially laid out in order to make
that compulsory, though Twofold Bay is just as
much N.S.W. as Port Jackson is. Finley and
Berrigan and derilderie and the rest of Western
Riverina didn’t complain much about all these
miles of needless distance to Sydney when they
were able to get their produce to Sydney on any
terms; but now they often can’t get it there for love
or money, because of the congestion which hasgro wn
up between. When the freight train starts from
Finley, at the far end of things, it plods along the
single line till it reaches Narrandera, where it gets
mixed up with the trains coming in from Hay.
At Junee the trains from Melbourne, Corowa,
Germanton and Lockhart (four railways) come
on to the same line. At Cootamundra the
branch trains from Wyalong and Tumut
(two more railways) come on to the line. At
Murrumburrah the Blayney and Grenfell branches
join, and their trains come in. At Goulburn the
Crookwell and Cooma railways empty their traffic
on to the same line. It is about here that the
freight-train begins to take 25 hours to do
100 miles. At Granville all this combined traffic
gets mixed up with all the trains from the west
and centre of the State, and at Strathfield it
tangles up again with all the trains from the
north and north-west. Later the Illawarra line
comes in. Progress becomes so slow through the
dragging of everything to Sydney that it takes
three or four or six trains to do the work that one
would do if the lines took a bee-track to the
nearest good port; and N.S.W. hasn’t the trains,
and there wouldn’t be room for them on the last
few miles of line if it had, for you can’t put all the
trains in creation on to one railway. So the far-
out portions of the railways are being practically
abandoned, so far as goods traffic is concerned,
during the busy wool and wheat and stock seasons.
Their business is crowded out by the growth of
new traffic between them and Sydney. In fact,
they are the small, lame devil at the back of Hell
—the little devil that is shoved against the wall
as • population increases between him and the
stove, and is scorned by everybody, and con-
sidered by nobody.
* • * *
So, as already mentioned, the people of Western
Riverina appealed to Solemn Lee, Minister for
Works, to build the 12 miles of cheap railway
which would connect them with Melbourne. The
Victorian line isn’t overcrowded like the N.S.W.
one, because it is shorter, and hasn’t half so many
tributaries running into it. Melbourne isn’t yet
congested like Sydney because it has less trade
and more space, and the railways run into it
from various directions instead of being all com-
pressed into one bottle-neck. Therefore Mel-
bourne can give Western Riverina the outlet
which Sydney can’t. The deputationists also
pointed out to Lee that the new arrange-
ment would be cheaper for them, and that
as N.S. W. citizens they had as much
right to be considered as anybody else; and that
it was almost a case of saving a large and import-
ant district of N.S. W. from ruin. Furthermore,
it wasn’t a case of diverting Sydney trade to Mel-
bourne ; it was rather a case of saving the N.S. W.
trade which Sydney couldn’t accommodate from
complete extinction. At present the Western
Riverina lines are worked at a dead loss, and part
of this loss arises from the fact that they can’t
gather up the trade that is offering, and carry it
north-easterly to Sydney, and they won’t con-
descend to make a heap of money by carrying the
available trade southward into Victoria. It
looked a convincing case, but solemn, narrow,
parish-minded Lee met it with flat refusal. His
attitude may be fairly set forth in these seven
propositions:—
(1) All N.S.W. lines were made lor the purpose of carry-
ing trade to Sydney and to no other N.S.W. port, nor to
any other port whatsoever.
(2) If this policy necessitates any district paying three
times as much freight as it need pay if it was allowed to
trade with its nearest port, that district must just stand
the loss.
(3) If, through the impossibility of crowding all the
commerce of 310,000 square miles into one city located
on a narrow peninsula, the system breaks down, it must
just break down.
(4) If it is impossible to convey the wool and wheat and
stock of outlying places along the congested lines, these
things must just rot or die where they are. It is better
for the goods of N.S.W. citizens to be destroyed flan to
be shipped from any port except Sydney, even if the
other port, as in the case of Twofold Bay or Port Stephens,
is also a N.S.W. port.
(5) If the people make ruinous losses—well, it is better
for good citizens of N.S.W. to be ruined than to trade
with any place save Sydney.
(6) If these ruined people have to abandon their hold-
ings, it is better for the outlying parts of N.S.W. to
become a desert than that they should prosper by having
commerce with any place save Sydney.
(7) It is also better for a N.S.W. railway to be a dead
loss than that it should be a paying proposition by carrying
traffic to some place that isn’t Sydney, and it is better for
it to run through an empty portion of N.S.W. than that
it should pass through a land densely peopled with
prosperous and contented N.S.W. citizens, if these
N.S.W. citizens have grown rich through dealing with any
place bar Sydney.
It is difficult to imagine that there should be on
the face of the earth a savage so unlettered as to
subscribe to these doctrines, but there certainly
AUSTRALIAN RAILWAY
BUILDING.
Portland is the finest harbor in Victoria. It is
splendidly placed to carry the trade of the
west of Victoria, and the south-east of S.A. As it
had to be killed in the interests of Melbourne and
Adelaide the only railway that is allowed to reach
it comes from the east. The other lines have been
carried round it at vast extra expense. And
there are many similar casts in Australia.
does seem to be such a savage—a good many of
him, in fact.
* * * *
It is reported that last year N.S.W. lost five
million sheep simply because the congested
railways couldn’t carry them to Sydney and
wouldn’t carry them anywhere else. Sheep
multiply fast; 11,000,000 lambs in a year is
quite a common thing. It only requires an-
other year or two of the present insanity, and
many areas will be compulsorily overstocked
to such a degree that the animals will perish
wholesale from hunger and thirst even in a fairly
good season. The bulk of the sheep, too, are on
the western slope of the mountains, on the western
plains, and in Riverina—the districts where, be-
cause of their distance from Sydney, the paralysis
is worst —and this makes the loss heavier. From '
Moree, in the far north (which is carefully refused
an outlet vid Brisbane in Queensland, or Fort
Stephens in N.S.W.) to Finley in the far south
(which is similarly refused an outlet to Melbourne)
and to Monaro (which is prohibited from using
Twofold Bay), the cry about congestion, loss and
ruin is the same. Yet the Federal capital is
being fixed as close as possible to Sydney so that
its trade may also get on to the crowded lines and
make things worse, and the Federal port at Jervis
Bay is to be restricted to two square miles, lest it
should do something to relieve tne pressure. And
there is already a demand that the transcon-
tinental railway from Port Darwin should crowd
into the same old Sydney.
Unless N.S.W. and Victoria, and in a less
degree S.A., alter their policy, or unless the Aus-
tralian Government takes over the whole railway
system and starts an entirely new policy, there
will one day be a great revulsion of feeling in
favor of private railway ownership. It would be
well for the Labor party—the only party which
has always been unanimously in favor of State
ownership—to make a note of this, and to write
the note large, for Labor, by carelessly lending
itself to the centralising policy of awful political
creatures like Wade, is helping to prepare the
way for the worst kind of trouble. Maoriland,
Queensland and Tasmania, which have built their
railways on fairly business-like principles, and
Westralia, which has sense enough to see that it
isn’t necessary to lay waste Albany and Genald-
ton in order that quite everything may go to Fre-
mantle, will escape; but the two south-eastern
States of the mainland are doing their worst to
create disaster. There is only one good thing
to be said for private railway enterprise : In its
desire to make money it is always ready to
take the shortest cut, and sometimes, m
spite of its shameful exactions, it becomes
cheaper than State enterprise for this one reason.
It may charge twice as much per mile,
but if the miles are 60 per cent, fewer
Private Enterprise supplies the cheaper service.
In all the far-back regions of N.S.W. there are
only two places which are blessed with a railway
which takes the ideal short track to the nearest
port. One is Broken Hill, which Private Enter-
prise connected with the S.A. railway system and
Adelaide when the State it belonged to said that
the place was too far off to be connected with
Sydney, and that N.S.W. would see it damned
before it would connect it with any other port.
The other shining example is Deniliquin, which
has been furnished by Private Enterprise with a
straight line to Melbourne. The result is that
Deniliquin, at the end of a private line, is only
200 miles from a port, while Finley, at the end of
a State line, almost alongside Deniliquin and
nearer to the sea, and also nearer to the big river,
is 454 miles by rail from the only port the State
allows it to use. Finley and Berrigan and the
adjacent towns on the end of the N.S.W. State
railway are not only refused access to the com-
paratively near N.S.W. port of Twofold Bay, and
to the still nearer port of Melbourne, but they are
even refused the few miles of railway which would
connect them with the Murray, lest they should
send their produce down-stream to a S.A.
market. It is the natural consequence of
this that Deniliquin has 2644 people, all
paying taxes to the N.S.W. Government,
while Finley, in a better situation, has only 294.
Under the system which has made Deniliquin—
a system which doesn’t care twopence about any-
thing save building the shortest, cheapest line
and getting all the trade that is possible—the
short cut to the sea would have been taken every
time—to Twofold Bay, Jervis Bay, Port Stephens
or any old place. And if that method had been
followed, N.S.W. would probably have a far more
efficient railway system at a third less cost, and
have a dozen fair-sized cities that it doesn’t now
possess. It is a disgrace that the splendid prin-
ciple of State railway ownership should be per-
verted into a device for the ruin and depopula-
tion of one-half the State, and a still greater
disgrace that hardly a voice should be raised
against the proceeding. Also it is a solemn
reflection that if accident had planted Australia’s
first settlement at Moreton Bay instead of Port
Jackson, Brisbane might now be the capital of a
State extending from the Mary to the Murrum-
bidgee, and the trains might run past a vacant
Sydney harbor carrying the produce of Goulburn
and Parramatta to be shipped away from the
Brisbane River. That would be no more foolish
than the things which State enterprise has done
in N.S.W. and Victoria, and is doing every day.
* * « «
Victoria has so far escaped the worst of the
trouble which has fallen on N.S.W., but it has
escaped through no merits of its own. It is a
smaller State, so the policy of dragging every-
thing to Melbourne involves less superfluous haul-
age than a similar policy does in N.S.W. Then
Melbourne covers more ground than Sydney, and
is less easily congested. It is not a bottle-necked
city like Sydney, and railways can enter it at
many points, which also tends to postpone the
worst of the trouble. And it only tries to crowd
into one bloated city all the trade of 88,000 square
miles—not all the trade of 310,000 square miles.
Nevertheless Victoria can show almost the prize
horror in the way of a vast expenditure of public
money incurred for the purpose of destroying one
of the finest ports in Australia. Portland is a
dozen times better than Melbourne as a seaport.
Though it is left almost in a state of nature it can
take in vessels of 11,000 tons without turn-
ing a hair. It is the natural outlet for
all western Victoria just as Twofold Bay
in N.S.W. is the natural outlet for much
of eastern Victoria. Portland is also the place
which Providence intended to carry the sea
trade of the rich south-east of S. Australia. So
Portland had to be killed twice over—once in the
interests of Melbourne, and once in those of Ade-
laide. It couldn’t be absolutely denied a railway,
but the accompanying diagram shows the fiendish
ingenuity with which it was arranged that hardly
any of the trade which naturally belongs to Port-
land should reach there without describing a vast
circle, which carries it so far towards Melbourne
that it is cheaper for it to go all the way and
finish its journey at Port Phillip. Victoria has
spent perhaps £5,000,000 in needless railway con-
struction, and it still spends perhaps £400,000
annually in needless haulage expenses in order to
kill its best seaport; and S.A. has spent a lot of
money for the same purpose. Which is the case
of Twofold Bay and Port Stephens, in N.S.W.,
repeated in an aggravated form.
A WELL-WATERED FEDERAL CAPITAL
WITH 11 INCHES OF RAIN.
State Premier Wade, of N.S.W., was moved the
other day, in his excited advocacy of his favorite
capital site at Canberra, to declare that the
streams thereabouts were “practically perma-
nent.” And his satellite, Senator Millkn, being
asked, while holding forth on the same subject, to
come down from the clouds and state exactly what
was the average rainfall at Quean bey an, the ob-
serving station just alongside Canberra, said that
he didn’t know—which was a sorrowful remark
seeing that he had just been pouring out a cataract
of eloquence on the subject—but he was sure in a
general way that the rain fell at Queanbeyan all
right. It seems a kind of a pity that Senator
Millen was so ignorant of his own subject as not
to know the average moisture at his own pet
capital site, especially seeing that he can discover
all the particulars by a glance at pages 731-732 of
the N.S.W. “Statistical Register” for 1907 (the
latest issue). That official publication dismisses
the matter briefly by saying that among the 64
observing stations in the coastal and tableland
country of N.S.W. there is only one that is drier,
on an average, than Queanbeyan, right alongside
the proposed Australian capital, and that one is
Cooma whence the alleged waters flow down to
the proposed capital. The only thing that is
wrong, in a meteorological sense, with Quean-
beyan, right alongside the proposed Australian
capital, is that, on an average, it hasn’t nearly
half the rainfall of Sydney, and that it and
Cooma, in the district where most of the alleged


Canberra waters have their source, are the two
least rained-upon places in eastern N.S.W.
As regards Premier Wade’s statement that the
streams around the proposed Canberra cap tal are
“practically permanent,” the same N.S.W.
“Statistical Register” for 1907 (pages 731-732)
contains some more interesting information. A
“ permanent ” stream is one which defies drought,
and a locality which defies drought is one where the
rain is fairly good even in bad years. The last bad
year in N.S.W.’s experience was 1902, and this
was the record during 1902 for all N.S.W., in-
cluding the furthest west: —

And these are official records, made by unbiassed
scientific men of N.S.W. at a time when there
was no question of a Federal capital in the case—
thej’ are not even suspected of being hurried fakes
or sham records got up to support a case.
• * ■* *
It may be, of course, that Wade and Millen,
in their frenzied desire to get the Australian
capital planted close to Sydney and within the
business radius of the Tory and Foreign-trade
morning papers of that city, have persuaded
themselves that Canberra is a miraculous place
where the rivers flow without rain and the creeks
run without moisture. But most thinking people
have-lost faith in miracles, and prefer to keep an
eye on the evidence. And the evidence is that,
in average years. Queanbeyan, right alongside the
proposed capital, is the driest place, bar one, in
eastern N.S.W. And in a dry year like
1902 it is the driest place in eastern N.S.W.;
it is also drier than a lot of places in
central N.S.W. ; and out of 95 observing
stations in the whole State, there are only 15,
most of them in the far and hopeless West, which
are drier. That sort of rainfall isn’t calculated to
supply a big city ; in a bad year the proposed
Federal capital district has as much rain as
Deniliquin, but the maddest person going doesn’t
propose; to build a great city at Deniliquin. Even
when Gregory Wade was inspecting his favorite
capital site —and that was in a good year—the
Queanbeyan river wasn’t flowing, and the habit
that creeks thereabouts have of not flowing is
something considerable. Of course it may be that
the anti-Federalists fixed their hopes on Can-
berra just because of its want of water, having a
desire that the capital of the hated Common-
wealth should be a mean and unlucky spot, but if
it is the want of water which recommends the
place to them they shouldn’t say so much about
the alleged abundance of water.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Line to Dugald’s Door,
When the Northern Territory Acceptance Bill
got into committee in the Reps, the first man to
leap into the breach with an amendment was
Dugald Thomson. Dugald proposed that the
Commonwealth .should only takeover the Territory
down to latitude 22, and that the balance of the
Territory—from latitude 22 to latitude 26—should
remain to S.A. or become a kind of No Man’s
Land. On the face of it the proposal—which,
at time of writing, is still undecided—looks
absurd, for it means the abandonment by the
Commonwealth of all the great mineral possibilities
of the McDonnell Ranges, but there are wheels
within wheels. The whole Territory is a lengthy
concern when measured from north to south,
and it is impossible to get away from the
fact that the kind of railway which would
do most in the way of opening up the land
would be one from Port Darwin to Port Augusta.
And if the McDonnell Ranges were Federal pro-
perty it would be absurd not to carry the line
that way and give their mineral riches a show.
But the proposed sawn-off or abbreviated Terri-
tory would be a stumpy, or almost square, propo-
sition. and a line running diagonally across it
from Port Darwin into Queensland and thence to
Sydney would open up about as much of it as a
line running southward towards Port Augusta.
And if the McDonnell Ranges were cut off from
the Federal area, then the argument coidd be
raised that the Commonwealth had no interest in
their mineral possibilities, and that it wasn’t
called upon to take the railway by the
short, straight, cheap route to Port Augusta
merely because of them—which would be
another excuse for shifting the line east-
ward into Queensland and finally to Sydney,
where Dugald lives. Of course, the great idea
of Dugald would represent a big loss to the
Commonwealth. The Commonwealth would have
to take over just about as much debt with the
northern bit of the Territory as with the whole
Territory, for the debt would go with the assets,
and these are practically all in the northern part.
It would sacrifice gratis and out of pure eussed-
ness a mineral field which may yet prove another
Kalgoorlie, and a vast amount of land which must
be of some value. The line to Sydney would be
much longer and more costly than the one to
Port Augusta, and it would finish at a city where
the rail way system is already congested to an extent
which almost means paralysis, and the deviation
would put an end to the prospeut of making the line
a Federal property from sea to sea with a Feder-
ally-owned port at each terminus, and it wouldn’t
even be a transcontinental line at all in any reason-
able sense of the word. But as a set-off to these
drawbacks there would be the joy of bringing the
line to Dugald’s door. The political crimes
that have been committed in the name of Liberty
are not a patch on those that have been committed
for the sake of the doors of people like Dugald
—some of them so very like Dugald that they are
almost more like Ddgald than he is himself.
,
The Deacook League’s Uncandid Opinion
of Its Leaders.
The N.S.W. “Federal Liberal League,” the
Deacook Fusion’s electioneering machine, is
issuing a series of what it calls “ educational leaf-
lets.” So far its chief effort is a tract entitled
“ Politics and Business Men a magnificent
piece of “ Awake ! Arise !” hifalutin. Commerce
is in danger—though the league apparently hasn’t
a ghost of a notion what it’s in dangef from.
Nevertheless, the league is solid on this—that to
avert universal smash, “the true functions and
the limitations of the State must be impressed
upon every man and woman in the com-
munity.” But, unfortunately, the tract doesn’t
ment.on what the true functions and proper limit-
ations are. If it doesn’t know what they
are it might get two of its members— Spkuce
Myth, Freetrader and colored labor advocate, and
Deakin, Protectionist and White Australian —to
define them. No doubt the job will be quite
simple. Still the league had better hurry them
up ; for until it does find out what the true func-
tions and the proper limitations of the State are,
it obviously can’t impress them on anybody,
wherefore its remarks on the point will continue
to be mere wind. And While it is waiting for
Spkuce Myth and Deakin to agree, the Deacook
League might reflect whether it isn’t either
making an ass of itself or trying to make an ass
of the public, in one or two other directions. It
calls on “business people” to awake and arise
with regard to the “ political mountebanks ” who
are getting into power and legislating for com-
merce :
This work can best be done by men who are themselves
masters of industry, who alone, because of their actual
knowledge and practical experience should be able to
deal with economic questions.
In fact, this is the one point that the Deacook
“Liberal League ” seems to have a definite opinion
about:—
This work must no longer be left to the theorists or to
the doctrinaires.
Why Deacook’s League makes such pointed refer-
ences to the utter unfitness and incapacity of
Deakin and Joe Cook, this paper doesn’t, of
course, know. Yet it plainly does point to
them. For Deakin is a doctrinaire barrister,
who never ran a business and wouldn’t know
what to do with one if he.had it; and Joe Cook
went straight from the mine to politics—he never
ran a business in his life either. Besides Deakin
and Cook, the Deacook Liberal League has evi-
dently got its eye on Fuller, the barrister; and
Glynn, the mere doctrinaire writer and barrister ;
and Groom, the ditto ; and Quick, the same ; and
Best, the lawyer; who also never ran any but the
six-and-eightpenny business. Also it has a
“derry” on Reid, the barrister; and Liddell,
the medico; and Bowden, the solicitor ; and
Bruce Smith, the barrister; and Irvine, the
ditto; and others. What the Deacook League
is going to do when it has fired this lot
out, The Bulletin can’t imagine. Yet if
it doesn’t intend to fire them out, what is
the good of the tract? For if Hughes
is to be tossed out because he is a mere lawyer,
Deakin must be tossed out because he is a mere
lawyer also; in fact, Hughes oughtn’t to be
tossed out quite as far as Deakin, because
Deakin never ran even an umbrella shop. And
if Fisher and Thomas are to be dropped because
they were lifted out of a mine into politics, Cook
must be dropped because he also came out of a
mine. In fact, Cook ought to be dropped
with a heavier thud tfiah Thomas, because
Thomas managed a mine, and Cook never
rose to be anything more than a check-
weigher. On the whole, the Deacook league
had better withdraw its unfortunate “educational
leaflet.” It hits its own crowd with too much
violence altogether. And, anyway, even if it
didn’t, the league can’t tell from the size a man
takes in tall hats the sort of success he is making
of his business. Uncle Abigail was a bank director
once, but where is that bank now? And it wasn’t
so long ago that William McMillan was a person
of tremendous financial consequence. Also Henry
Gyles Turner’s bank deposits can still be got for
about l/s. in the £. You ,chn’t size up a business
man till he has been dead for some time; and by
then he is liable to be too far gone to be of
much service to even a bogus “Liberal” league.

The “ Menace ” in Tassy.
“Apple-Islander” writes:—
Tasmania is apparently about to have a Labor Govern-
ment! After a Labor Government in the reactionary
little isle, the Deluge, Armageddon, the Millennium or any
other unexpected old thing may be looked for. This vviil
make the sixth Labor administration that the Common-
wealth has known. Anderson Dawson formed his Cabinet
in Queensland in 1899, and it lived just a week—from
Dec. Ito Dec. 7. The Prick Ministry in S.A. went strong
from July, 1905, till the death of its founder afew months
ago. VVestralia had the Laborite Daulish Ministry from
the end of April, 1904, till the end of August, 1905.
And there have been two Labor administrations in the
Australian Parliament—the Watson Ministry (April to
August, 1904), ahd the Fisher Ministry (Nov. 1908 to May
1909). By the present appearance of things the Earle
Ministry that is due in Hobart isn’t in for a much longer
lease of existence than the short-lived Dawson combination
in Queensland, but it is safe to say that considerable good
•will be wrought by it. It will be a revelation. Once the idea
of a Labor Premier, much less a Labor Prime Minister, was
inconceivable even to the majority of broad-minded
people in Australia. As to the reactionaries, their papers
had schooled them to the belief that such things could
only come to pass over dead bodies and ruined homes and
rifled banks and things. The first considerable object-
lesson all Australia got in the puerility of such views was
when J. C. Watson won to office in the Australian Par-
liament five years ago. It was quickly recognised that
the Commonwealth was being run by as sane and
level-headed a lot of administrators as it had
known in its history. Even Melbourne’s dreary
old Paunch was fain to go slow on its stock jests. The
Watson Ministry went out leaving an impressive record
behind it. That didn’t debar G. Reid and the anti-Labor
press from resurrecting the old slanders in the Federal
campaign, which followed 18 months later. Australia
was urged, with an appalling shriek, to vote “ against
Labor and Anarchy!" But by this time the
constituencies had learned things. They turned
down Reid’s anti-Social'ist candidates to such a
tune that by the end of last year Andrew Fisher
was able to form a Government composed of the elements
which the Tory dailies had classed with foreign regicides
and dynamitards. And again nothing untoward hap-
pened to the country. As a result of occurrences like
these in both State and Federal politics, the bulk of the
anti-Labor legends are wearing so thin as to be practically
useless. Every fresh opportunity Labor is accorded to
prove its mettie in an administrative capacity causes the
libels levelled against its political character to appear less
credible and more unscrupulous. That is the chief reason
why Labor’s present ascendancy in Tasmania is gratifying
to Democrats everywhere. The ascendancy may be of
the most temporary kind ; but it will signify, all the
same, that the old order is passing away in Dodery’s
verdant isle.
Dacey of the Mean Colonial Party.
The astounding upheaval of Labor-inember Dacey
in the N. S. Wales Assembly last week was one
of the things which is going to rise up and smite
his party next time it goes before the electors.
Among a mass of wild and mostly idiotic state-
ments. the most wild arid foolish of all was that
Organised Labor would live to curse the day
Federation was accomplished. Surely this poli-
tician is mad ! Federation gave Labor chances
that it gave no other party. The capitalistic,
Tory crowd stood entrenched in six different
citadels, and to win its way Labor had, under
the old regime, to attack and destroy six Legisla-
tive Councils after it had carried the outworks in
the shape of the six Legislative Assemblies.
Federation gave it the chance to dominate them
all without the half-century of dreary struggle that
must otherwise have eventuated before the Councils
were beaten down. Already the Labor party
has twice dominated the whole continent, and its
chances of doing so again look more rosy than ever.
There is something ludicrous in the idea of Labor
cursing the political and national evolution that
made possible an all-Australian Old Age Pensions
Act and an all-Australian graduated land-tax,
that will send the vast locked-up estates tumbling
on to the market; that gave Labor the oppor-
tunity to prove to the Imperial authorities and
the world generally that it is capable of ruling a
nation, and better able than any other party in
Australia to devise a scheme of efficient naval
and military defence : that made it possible
for a single Labor Government to safeguard
the interests of that roving army of toilers
that goes up and down the coasts in ships,
no matter to what State they may belong. Of
course, the real trouble is that before Federation
Dacey, M.L.A., might have ranked as a states-
man ; but since that disastrous event he knows
himself, no matter what others may think him,
for nothing better than a sort of glorified county
councillor, something a bit above the dignity of a
member of a Rabbit Board. For, with all its
limitations, the Federal power, in the hands of a
man who will do things whether he gets himself
disliked or not, is the dominant force in Austra-
lia. And one day there will arise a rude,
ungracious Prime Minister with a jaw like the
track-finder of a railway engine, who will proceed
to make himself unpleasant to small local per-
sonages of the type of Wade and his follower
Dacey, and will put them in their right place
once and for all.

The Lesson of the Dreadnought Fiasco.
The fate of the “Dreadnought movement” is a hor-
rible example of what happens to a press-created
“public opinion ” when that opinion gets a chance
to rise up and speak its mind. Six months ago
the two Sydney morning dailies were shrieking
six times a week into both ears of all their readers
that they (the readers) were fairly lusting to give
Britain a Dreadnought, wherewith to protect itself
in the North Sea. Joe Cook typified the sort of
politician who credited the foolish yarn. He
climbed, last April, on to a Stump at Marrickville
(N.S.W.), and with the froth of imperial de-
mentia streaming from his whiskers, said ;
Are we going to save our miserable skins here while
the Empire may be lost in the North Sea? (Cheers.)
The presentation of one Dreadnought would he a mere
fieahite. (Applause.) The situation called for scores
of Dreadnoughts !
And because the handful of old women present
hammered their gamps on the floor and said,
“ Hear, hear !” to this lunacy, Joseph and others
like him concluded that the dailies knew what
they were about in alleging that the great heart
of the people hungered to donate Dreadnoughts;
and in solemn form Cook gave the assurance to
the world: “Make no mistake. That Dread-
nought will go.” Of course the Dreadnought has
gone ;it has gone to the devil. Since those Jwild
days Joseph has changed. His Government is
addressing its mind as hard as possible to-day to
saving what six months ago he called “ our miser-
able skins”; and if the Empire is going to be
mislaid in the North Sea it will endure the catas-
trophe, so far as Cook is concerned, on its own.
When Dreadnausea in its original form fell dead,
publicly and incontrovertibly, a sort of substitute
arose. The papers which had howled loudest
about the clogged determination of Australia in
general and N.S.W. in particular to force a
battleship on a reluctant J. Bull, started
to admit coyly that there were points of
idiocy about the scheme. But, said they,
the public, though baulked of its chance to actu-
ally give Britain a battleship, is determined
to present the beleaguered old land with
a battleship’s equivalent. An equivalent would
be something worth about £2,000,000. That
idea also perished. Then it was insisted
that Australian patriots were madly enthusi-
astic to save the Empire by giving N.S.W.
a training farm and a naval college. Allen
Taylor, gaud-Mayor of Sydney, intimated that
his Dreadnought Fund—variously stated at from
£BO,OOO to £lOO,OO0 —would be applied to those
purposes. Once more-the public was represented
as concurring with frenzied eagerness. The Syd-
ney Telegraph averred that a “fresh waye of
imperial loyalty had passed over the land,” and
that donations from all sorts of unexpected
quarters were pouring in. Then money be-
gan to talk in a firm tone of voice, and
the burden of its remarks was that the
“Empire papers” were liars. The Dago fish-
monger whose name was down for £lOO
wrote requesting that his quota should be handed
to a hospital. A horde of other private Dread-
noughters followed suit. A meeting was called
of what the dailies termed “ largest contributors.”
One who had died, and whom Allen Taylor and
the press had got down as a promiser of £20,000
despite the fact that he had only pledged himself
to half that amount, naturally didn’t turn up.
His eldest son took on the original liability—• viz .,
£lO,OOO. Another, W. F. Buchanan, also stayed
away, and, till the end of last week, laid so
extremely low and silent as to his views on Dread-
nausea’s new development that the public might
be forgiven for concluding that his Empire frenzy
had evaporated. Next, various districts which
had been moved to promise lump sums started
to back out. For example :
Camden, Tuesda3'. —At a meeting last night of local
subscribers to the Dreadnought Fund, presided over by
Alderman G. M. Macarthur Onslow (Mayor), it was
unanimously resolved to ask the Lord Mayor of Sydney to
refund the £6OO remitted for the Dreadnought Fund. —
D.T. news item.
Also :
Tamworth, Sunday.—At a meeting of the Taniworth
subscribers to the Dreadnought Fund it was resolved
that the £2OO which was forwarded from Tamworth
should be returned. —Herald news item.
And so on. The Commonwealth won’t have
been made a world’s laughing-stock in this
matter wholly in vain if its Parliamentary
representatives learn from the sorry busi-
ness not to trust the diagnoses of party news-
papers as to the electors’ political frame of mind.
On all sorts of urgent questions—the capital
site, the route of the transcontinental railway
and the like—the bulk of the people is
continually being misrepresented as siding
wholeheartedly with the viewpoint of a
handful of N.S.W. Tories. In most cases it
is impracticable, unfortunately, to test such
allegations till it is too late. The lie concerning
the enthusiasm for the “Dreadnought move-
ment” differed from most of its kind, inasmuch as
it was capable of being tested on the spot. A
referendum is the most satisfactory test where
further wild statements are circulated by palpably
biassed newspapers as to the people’s eagerness
for any given measure, and in the light of the ex-
perience gained in connection with Dreadnausea
genuine Democrats in Parliament should see to it
that it is freely employed.
Transcontinental Railways.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He may be a simple moujik, quite inferior to Us,
He may be a hairy Cossack, with a beard to stuff
a ’bus —
But, when carefully considered, there are points
about the Russ,
And we haven’t any reason to expand our chests
with pride;
For he took the spade and hammer, and theodolite
and drill,
And he punched the steppes of Asia —punched his
Road along until
Vladivostock heard his engines rolling Gatewards,
whistling shrill,
And Port Arthur saw his locos. —where the
Bear went down and died !
Where the Irtish hastens seaward, where the Tobol
nor’ward flows—
Where the ice lies white on Baikal, ’neath Siberia’s
winter snows.
There the Builders pushed their Roadway, and
the White Man fought his foes—
Are ye building as he builded ? Nay , your Gates
are open wide!
Hear the songs the White Man chanted—battle
hymns his soldiers sang,
As his armies hastened forward—hear the cannon
roar and clang,
Lo, the blood of shattered heroes soaks the fields
of Liao-Yang,
And the dead to-day are sleeping on the Road
from Tsitsihar;
There are bones that bleach and whiten where the
Bear put up his fight—
There are Russian children crying for their fathers
in the night,
There’s a Flag that’s torn and humbled—do ye
understand it right?
Are ye Building, are ye toiling, driving on with
spade and bar ?
For the Russ, he did his darnedest in the few
Preparing Years —
Yea, he bridged the foaming Shilka with his steel
and engineers,
But to-day the Bear is Gate-less, and the Monkey
grins and leers—
Have ye barred Australia’s Gateway? See!
the portal stands ajar!
Far away the Bear is toiling—he is slowly “ mak-
ing good,”
He is grafting, he is marching as the White Man
surely should—
He is tramping towards the ocean ’neath his dis-
tant engine-hood,
Making ready, sure and ready for the conflict
yet to be;
He’s a plodder, he’s a sticker —stuck until Port
Arthur fell,
Beaten down with fists of iron, beaten down with
shot and shell—
He is working, he is waiting, then he’ll ring his
engine- bell —
Then he’ll roll his armies eastward to his Gate-
way by the sea !
Eighty millions spent a-building—still the Russ is
spending more.
“ Shall the Monkey hold my portal ? I will win
me back my Door ”
Says the Russian as he labors, as he plods by Dolo-
Nor,
Are ye plodding also seaward? Bah ! your Gate
is wide and free !
Lo, the Bear was White and willing, but the
foemen got him down—
’Twas St. Petersburg that beat him, not the
Monkey legions brown,
’Twas the crowd of slothful bosses far away in
Petek’s Town-
Fighting Peter, he who labored where the
Deutscher’s shipyards are ;
He whose fist propelled the hammer and the axe
and plane and saw—
He who left the pomps of Moscow, so that he with
earnest paw
Might instruct his Russian people, building ships
and framing law,
He whose days were days of vigor—cheers for
Russia’s Fighting Czar?
But the bones of Pete the Builder lie beside the
Neva’s stream,
And the man who holds his billet wanders sadly
in a dream—
So to-day the Monkey’s locos, lift their warning
toot and scream,
On the Bear-constructed Railway up in old
Manchuria !
There’s a Road that waits unfinished—well ye
know the Road I mean,
’Tis the Road from Port Augusta—there’s a con-
tinent to glean,
Time that ye were pushing nor’ward towards the
Gateway’s ocean-sheen —
Time that steel and steam were urging towards
the Arafura side ;
There’s a Track that needs preparing—One that
leads by Alice Springs.
Are ye bossed and chained and shackled that no
hammer skyward swings?
That the Track is but a pathway where the
“wire” vibrates and sings—
Shall they write upon your tombstone : “Here
a White Australia died ” ?
Lest the plain and simple moujik prove superior
to Us,
Let us Build our Road and yakker—let us push
Australia’s ’bus,
For the question rises bluntly: Though we scorn
the distant Russ ,
Have we tuppence worth of reason to inflate our
chests with pride !

A Tired Premier Collides ■with a Number of
Rich Landlords.
Victoria’s obstructive House of Rich Landlords
got in some more of its work the other night, in
consequence of which Premier Murray is more
energetically angry than usual. How long he will
stay that way r.obodv can say, and what will
happen to him when the Rich Landlords get hold
of his Land-tax Bill, Heaven only is aware. The
present difficulty concerns his Electoral Bill,
which provided, among other reforms, that the
police should enrol all Assembly electors by
canvass ; and that an elector should vote only in
the district where he or she resided. The Land-
lords had no sooner waddled to their seats
than J. M. Davies moved an amendment dis-
pensing with the new method of enrolling, and
reverting to the old ratepayers’ roll fraud. This
meant a return to the unsatisfactory, cumbersome
system under which some 58,000 persons are
perpetually disfranchised ; it meant a return to
the stupid, inconvenient electors’ rights nuisance,
with the consequentdisfranchisement of thousands
of women ; and it meant a dead loss to the State
of £lO,OOO or possibly of £20,000 a year. In fact,
it was a brazen attempt to disfranchise those
whom Parliament had already declared should
have votes. As the Rich Landlords carried
Davies’ amendment by 18 votes to seven,
and further hinted at their intention to
restore to their beloved property-owners the right
to vote in any electorate they pleased, Attorney-
General Brown wearily remarked that it was no
use going any further, and adjourned the House.
Murray now says be will give the Rich Land-
lords a chance to consider the error of their ways,
and if they persist in their iniquity he will
“ utilise all constitutional machinery provided to
meet such emergencies.” Which probably signifies
that he may hold a free conference, and if that is
resultless he may dissolve the Assembly and pass
the Bill once more ; and if the Landlords still prove
obstreperous, he may then dissolve both Houses.
How the dissolution of the Assembly is to penalise
the House of Landlords no one knows. There are
three possible remedies for that awful obstruction.
One is to send up a Bill providing for the aboli-
tion of the Upper House and a life pension of,
perhaps, £lOOO a year to each of the existing
Landlords “ in recognition of his valuable ser-
vices ” (which would be sarcasm). This scheme
would mean bribing the Landlords now
in power to abolish themselves and their
possible successors, and even at the price
it would be money well spent. The next
alternative is to send up the same Bill
and stop supplies till it is passed ; in other words,
to let the whole public service disband, the police
force go to pieces, the railways shut up, and the
State go under a sort of interdict until the in-
sanely inflated Landlords came to their senses.
I hat would cost a lot more than the bribery
device, but it would also be worth while. The
third remedy is for Murray and all his crowd to
push on the gospel of Unification and Reconstruc-
tion for all they are worth. Thereby the Victorian
people would, by referendum, hand over to the
Australian Parliament all their assets and liabili-
ties and powers of government, and the Australian
Parliament would hand back to them certain
powers of local government to be exercised by a
small State Legislature of one House, elected by
adult suffrage. And after that there would be no
more House of Landlords and no more silly old
veto, and no more trouble. Premier John Weary
says he is going to “utilise all constitutional
means ” to meet the obstruction of the Landlords.
Well, these three devices are constitutional
means, and if John Weary knuckles down to the
Landlords withouthaving first attempted all three
remedies, then the truth is not in John Weary.
