The N.S.W. Government’s Closer Settlement
Bill has practically collapsed before the storm of
opprobrium that blew out of the door of the Sydney
1 elegraph office. In its original form it provided
that any estate within 10 miles of a “centre of
population ” might be “proclaimed” and valued,
and if the owner didn’t cut it up and sell it within
a lengthy period, the State might annex it at the
value it bore when the valuation was made. It
was a clumsy method at the best when compared
with a progressive land-tax, for (1) it would yield
no revenue ; (2) its operation would be slow, and
it would only apply to a part of the State ; and
(3) it wouldn’t apply at all except when the
machinery was set in motion by hand, whereas a
progressive land-tax, if it is sufficiently heavy,
vvorks automatically, everywhere, all the
time—you just pass your law and then
go to bed. But the Telegraph fell on
Wade’s inefficient Bill, and now it is
a great deal worse than it was at first. Appa-
rently, it was a case of caving in or losing the
’Graph's support, and the Government caved
like .Jenolan. The first important change pro-
vided that an estate could only be proclaimed for
resumption purposes if it was within five miles
(not 10) of a “centre of population.” That re-
duced the area liable to resumption in
each case from over 310 square miles to
less thaa 80. Then came the question, “ What
is a centre of population?” It was decided
that it should mean a town which at last census
had a population of at least 600. Around any
smaller town the land is not liable to compulsory
resumption under the Act. The enormous dimen-
sions of this joke can be gathered from a glance at
the N.S. W. (“Australian Year Book”
for 1909). Ihe first page mentions 134 post towns,
and of these exactly 19 qualify by population
to have large estates compulsorily resumed in
their vicinity. These 19 include Alexandria,
Albury, Annandale, Ashfield, Auburn, Balmain
and Bankstown, which may as well be left out of
consideration, so that the final result is that out
of • 134 towns there are 12 around which some
resumptions may or may not be made. The
rest of the Gazetteer shows similar results.
For instance, in the first column of names
beginning with “R,” seven places out of 53
qualify by population to have big pastoral estates
cut up in their immediate vicinity, and four of
those seven are Randwick, Redfern, Rockdale
and Rookwood. That sort of thing is more
humorous than helpful. The general impression
is that the measure will die of laughing at itself.
“ Wild Cat ”: As the IO U business shows signs
of being played out, “ Wingen ” Abbott, of the
N.S.W. Squatters’ Union, proposes that the same
shall be continued with the I O Myself:—
In the last three or four years pastoralists and farmers
have lost very large sums of money through the inability
of our railways to carry produce to port. There can be
no remedy until the single lines within 200 miles of
Sydney have been duplicated. Within the next few
years oUr losses are certain to be much greater than in
the past, and the Government answer to every demand
is : “We have no money and cannot venture to borrow.”
At the present time, on deposit in the banks at 2 or 3 per
cent., and in many cases as floating balances earning no
interest, there are millions of money belonging to pas-
toralists, farmers and other producers. If the Govern-
ment will give the Railway Commissioners authority to
borrow, it will pay us to find the money, and expend it
in that way, rather than lose it, absolutely, in the next
few years. Let authority be given to borrow at 4 or, if
necessary, 4J per cent., locally, on five or ten years’de-
bentures, and the money to duplicate will be forthcoming
in a month.
It boils down to a simple business proposition.
Squatter Abbott says the work will be worth
millions to his crowd during the next few years.
Therefore his crowd would still be a lot to the
good if, instead of lending the necessary money
to the Government at 4£ per cent., it handed over
the coin for nothing—that is, in the shape of
taxes, etc. Abbott knows a trick worth two of
that, though. He wants the millions and the 4J
per cent. But if the State is not an utter
fool, it will reply that, as the money is to
be spent in putting millions into the squat-
ter’s pocket, it isn’t going to guarantee the
squatter any 4£ per cent. ; moreover, if
there is any guaranteeing to be done, the squatter
is obviously the right person to do it, since he is
the one who is going to reap the millions. If
“Wingen” Abbott’s friends don’t intend to work a
confidence trick, their course is quite easy. All
they have to do is to tell the Government that
they will guarantee the interest; further, as they
will make millions out of the work, that they will
undertake to repay the capital cost within the next
few years. Then, if they put up the money as a
guarantee of good faith, the Government isn’t
likely to waste much time considering the matter.
But so long as they merely want the Government
to borrow money from them at 4| per cent., and
spend it on something that will put millions in
their pockets, they are not likely to be taken
seriously—they are too much like the philan-
thropic gentleman with a gold brick to sell.
A few days ago one Geoffry Colquhoun, steward
of the Warrnambool (Vic.) Hospital and Benevo-
lent Asylum, disappeared from his billet. The
agitated committee promptly met to investigate,
with the usual result—“serious irregularities.”
As it happened, the steward was no further away
than Melbourne, where he had been gaily getting
married. He was brought back to Warrnambool.
and hauled up at the police court on an “ off day,”
on a charge of misappropriating £l3, money handed
in on behalf of a paying patient. On the Bench
were two local justices, and, as soon as everybody
was made comfortable, an equally comfortable
discussion took place regarding how the case was
to be dealt with. The steward’s solicitor asked
that the charge be altered to common larceny, so
that “the Bench could deal with it.” No one
objected, and altered it was. Then the
steward pleaded guilty, his solicitor made
a “moving appeal,” the Bench fined him
£lO, the steward paid it, the business was
over, and the lucky man left by train. This was
the end of the first act. Three days later, the
committee held another meeting, when a weird lot
of further “ irregularities ” were discovered. At
the time of writing, the auditors are busy making
a thorough overhaul of the accounts, and the com-
mittee is to hold another meeting. But, so far,
all that has really happened is the £lO fine already
mentioned. It is interesting, by way of com-
parison, to recollect that at this same Warrnam-
bool court a few months ago an unfortunate cycle
agent who was a few pounds short in his cash,
was suddenly told to go up for six months’ hard.
His trouble was that he had no influential
friends. The whole business is a scandalous and
odorous advertisement of the wretched .T. P.
business—so odorous that some day it must surely
cause some remark even in the apartments of the
authorities at Melbourne.
Some striking illustrations of fallacious reasoning
from figures have been published lately in the
Melbourne press re the question of the relative
viciousness and virtuousness of N.S.W. and Vic-
toria. Firstly, one of the pleaders for Bible
instruction in Victorian State schools quoted some
figures of mysterious origin to show that Sydney
police records during the past 20 years indicated a
greater improvement in public morals than Mel-
bourne can boast of. In reply to which a Mr.
Adam, of Melbourne, pointed proudly to the fact
that in Victoria the percentage of arrests per
1000 of population in 1905, as compared with 1890,
showed a reduction greater in some respects than
the other sophist had credited to N.S.W. But
neither of these two misleading citizens touched
the question of police efficiency, which is just as
pertinent to crime records as the figures thereof.
Nothing is proved by stating that “ offences
against the person ” and against “ property ” have
diminished from a certain percentage in 1890
to a much lower percentage in 1905, save the fact
that the police make fewer arrests than of yore.
In Victoria the public complaint is that the per-
centage of arrests for serious offences is disgrace-
fully low. Murderers, highway robbers, burglars
and sneak thieves go uncaptured wholesale. The
police force is alleged to be about equally inade-
quate, ill-officered and corrupt. Any number of
crimes are committed without causing any
addition to the number of arrests made ;
yet it is the question of arrests only
which counts in the comparisons from which
these rival statisticians draw their conclusions.
And it is a solemn fact that if law, police, gaols and
all the rest of the show were entirely abolished
arrests would stop altogether, and on this same
mode of reasoning there would be no crime what-
ever—and yet there might be a dead body lying
in each street, and a shooting scrap and a burglary
and an assault and a case of arson and a forgery
and two bigamists at each street corner.

“ The Belle of New York ” was revived at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) last Saturday night. Of
course it was an immense success. When, years
hence, the world’s last Freetrader shall stand
gazing drearily at the ruins of B. Smith’s mauso-
leum, he will raise his eyes from the sad spectacle
to encounter a hoarding intimating that that night
“ The Belle ” will be performed for the 8,000,000 th
time on earth, or words to that effect. This
is a piece in which the chorus is on the
stage nearly all the time. Has the length
of tenure of the stage by the chorus ever been
taken into consideration in estimating the reasons
for the success of a musical comedy? Where, as
in the entertainment under consideration, the
show-maidens are pleasing, it must be a powerful
factor. “The Belle” does not grow hackneyed
and pall on audiences because there is" so
much in it that is inimitable. Its bright
and original music, its insane humor and
equally insane sentimentality, its quaint
characters and the queer, delightful places wherein
they foregather, combine to render the piece
unique in its line. The combination may not be
good art, but it is sui generis , and that is a re-
freshing quality these plagiarising days. It is as
bizarre as a Wiertz picture, and as hard to imitate.
Miss Olive Godwin plays Violet Gray in
the present production. She sings the Sal-
vation girl’s music charmingly and acts well.
Frank Greene’s fine baritone voice is heard
to great effect as Bronson, jun. ; but he
doesn’t suggest the N’York youth-about-
town for a moment. Romano’s is this
spendthrift’s revelling place, not Sherry’s; and
tiiat fact, made patent by his every intonation, is
a defect in a part which is American or nothing.
E. Nable’s Ichabod Bronson is along conventional
lines, and the same remark applies to the Polite
Lunatic of W. S. Percy. Why these comedians
elected last week to introduce, in the candy store
scene, a series of dull, British-sounding gags the
writer failed to comprehend. The stock jests in
“The Belle,” which are racy of the U.S. soil
if nothing else, are numerous enough. Miss
Lottie Sargent is tuneful, sweet, pathetic
and all the other things that Fifi Fricot
should be. Miss Ada Stirling is a hand-
some and impressive Cora Angelique. Fred
Leslie is an ideal Blinkv Bill. Miss Tilly Wood-
lock portrays his affinity, Mamie Clancy, and a
more satisfactory affinity no reasonable man could
desire. Miss Elsie Stevenson (Kissy) is another
graceful, dainty and wholly alluring circum-
stance. And, to finish with, the classic idles of
Snifkins, Kenneth Mugg and the Counts are in
excellent hands.

Sydney Palace is still “Married to the
Wrong Man,” and at time of writing the manage-
ment had taken no steps towards the Divorce
Court. The drama now wears a record-breaking
look in its eye, and the bulge of great wealth is
visible in its tail pocket. On Saturday, October
23, the first of a series of matinee performances
will be given, at which that seasoned drama,
“The Bells,” will be rung in strict accordance
with the traditions left by the late Henry Irving.
* * # *
The Shackleton films, which cost Charles
Spencer £4OOO, were first shown to the public at
Sydney Town Hall on Saturday night last and
drew a mighty audience. There had been a pre-
vious private view at the Sydney Lyceum Hall
which moved this scribe to make some remarks
on the desire for a quiet life engendered by the
spectacle of the Antarctic wastes. It is said that
the Eskimo once inhabited all Europe and
only penetrated into the frozen north when per-
suasion was applied, in the shape of a battle-axe,
by invaders from the east and south. Just possible
that, if the Fused Government continues the policy
of stagnation long enough, the Australia of the
future will be where Shackleton’s cinematograph
“ took ” the emperor penguin reviewing the line
of his subjects by the frozen sea. These birds
seem to be most trustful beings and peculiarly
helpless. With their utter deficiency of fighting
qualities one would expect that they would be very
timid ; but they are not and the expedition inter-
ested them immensely. So they formed a tourist
bureau and ran excursions to the camps. The
fact that some of them never returned probably
gave the proceedings a spice of romance ; any-
how the element of danger did not have the effect
of driving them away. An interesting feature of
the show was that as the views reeled across
the screen, woman after woman in the hall,
although it was hot enough, unconsciously drew
her wraps closer round her. It spoke volumes for
the realism of the pictures.

Another warbler from the aviary of Mr.
Steffani is written down for a public hearing at
Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall on October 26. She is
Miss Gladys Wahlberg, and her voice is a mezzo-
soprano. Her aides in melody-making will be
Messrs. Marsden, Woods, C. Monk and G.
Bell; and Miss Esther Kahn will interpret piano-
forte masterpieces. Miss Vera Barnett, ;is accom-
panist, will hit the teeth of the piano-animal and
put her foot on its tail.
* X *
Sydney Tivoli seems to arrive at the dol-
drums at intervals of about two months or so, and
that is its present location. The seals are due to
flop otf the bill just about the time the Incurable
Reader is opening this issue, and the present
scribe at least is glad that they are to have a brief
respite from stage work. They have practically
been balancing the whole show on their elegant
wet noses for several weeks past, and the strain
and anxiety has taken the gloss off their Arctic
overcoats. Whilst waving them farewell and
wishing them always plenty of fresh fish, it is
worth noting that the species never suffers froni
cold feet. Miss Athmore Gray, the languid
Salome dancer, will pack up the thinking end of
the Apostle and depart towards the end of the
week. The turns which will then remain are none
of them in the planet class. Blake and Granby,
the Two Bells and Ronald George will be about
the brightest spots in a rather empty firmament.

■•The Catch of the Season’’ took a sporting turn
last Saturday, and when one verse of the Dook’s
“living tableau” song introduced the figure of
Blue Book’s jockey, clad in manager Williamson’s
racing colors, the house roared its approval. The

** * *
Melba can gather people together with a
wave of her hand—or so it looks to a mere out-
sider. The Exhibition, which is but a vasty barn
with a dome to it, was crowded on Saturday until
the audience mounted into the galleries and filled
those to standing-room-only. Melba was in
excellent voice, but the beautiful subtleties
of her vocalising were lost in the nooks and
crannies of the circus building. During the whole
of the “Lucia” Mad Scene a spout valve above
the left transept guttered with the flood waters of
the rain, and kept up an accompaniment like
bones to a rag time coon song. It may not have
reached Melba, but the sacred vice-regal enclosure
had the full benefit of the “extra.” Rossini’s
flaming air and chorus from the “ Stabat Mater’’
had full field in the vastness. Full of devils and
flames and weird fear of rabble is the “Inflam-
rnatus, ” with the orchestra and great choir doing
their durnedest and the woman’s voice shrieking
above it all. It’s a great chance for a
soprano, beginning with the intensely abrupt
attack away up at C in alt. All the same,
one feels that the sensational thing, often re-
peated, would smash the delicacies of a voice as
surely as Wagnerian opera does. An encore
to the “ Inflammatus.” was a gentle little song,
“Down in the Forest Something Stirred,” which
dips into the delicate phrasing that only Melba
seems to reach. “Se Satan Rose” was another
encore in still another mood of abandon. Manager
Lemmone brought along his flute for the decora-
tion of the “Jewel Song.” Marshall-Hall’s
orchestra, with many borrowed minions, plunged
into Wagner and Hamish MacCunn. Ranalow
did his best with *“ O Star of Eve,” with the
’cellos in the orchestra sobbing out an accom-
paniment, and Una Bourne seemed to tackle too
much for her strength in attempting a Liszt
Rhapsody in the barnsome building.

** * *
Wirth’s circus spread its canvas to the rain
in Melbourne last Saturday night, when the
crowd came up smiling to the vast tent as of old.
With picture-shows booming in the vicinity, and
the new roller-skating rink right up against it,
the circus seemed to be putting too great a strain
on public patronage, yet the multitude bulked
larger than ever. The conditions were discom-
forting. The canvas had been having a dry time
of late, wherefore the rain dripped through the
roof in places and put a damper on everything
but the spirits of the small boys who had
paid a shilling and rejoiced in the outlay.
About 20 of the Circus turns have an interest
which, for the old, is mainly historical. The saw-
dust of bygone ages is on them. They breathe
the sentiment of a treasured past. If the feats in
the arena were very different, or the elephants
broke out in new directions and the clowns forgot
to kick one another, it wouldn’t be a Circus.
Sameness is the immortal soul of the show. A
slight further advance in the education of the
horses is permissible, and a new joke is not much
out of place; but it is to the most tried and trusty
items that this writer bows most cordially.
The audience on Saturday roared its loudest at
the acrobatics of the Frantz Family, which is a
new edition of the family imported by Rickards
some years ago. The performance is slick and
rapid, and the furious somersaults of one lanky
Frantz in long skirts are as droll in their way as
the gravity of the bounding operator. A more
astounding feature of the Circus is Datas, the
Man of Memory. The figures and facts which
Datas has once photographed upon his brain-film
are transferred at will to the tip of his tongue.
He neither forgets nor mislays any date he has
commitoed to memory. On Saturday night the
boys refused a hearing to the man who wanted to
“introduce” him, and were rowdily disposed to
Datas when he introduced himself, but under
peaceable circumstances this gifted person will be
found equally questionable and convincing.
Tait’s Pictures (Melbourne) are now lodged
at the Glaciarium, which has been much benefited
from a spectatorial point of view by the recent
addition of a sloping floor. Saturday’s menu was
the customary mixture of tragedy and comedy.
There was a yarn about Louis XI., who was pre-
sented as a tottery despot with a fancy for inflict-
ing sudden bereavements on ducal families. There
was a tale of love and a story of a man who runs
about frightening maiden ladies with his suit of
leaves. Narratives of sentiment and excitement
were “ A Convict’s Sacrifice” and “A Negro’s
Gratitude,” while pictures of the naval training of
boys were furiously appreciated by a herd of
scouts who had been generously invited to the
performance.
** * *
West’s Pictures (Melbourne) now contain
a beautiful view—“ Big Game Hunting in Africa”
—which this paper dimly suspects to be a fake.
The frowning cliffs and pieces of rock seein painted
and done up; though it is only just to admit
that sundry men and animals look real. A
picture that would be enthralling to revolutionists
is “ The Sword and the King,” which depicts the
slaughter of a tyrant by a popular demigod ;
while the tale of “A Broken Violin ” causes one
to wipe away a stealthy tear. “His Brand New
Hat,” a comic film, is almost as agonising; but
the pictures of “ The Ziegler Arctic Expedition ”
and of “Animated Cotton” are clever and enter-
taining.

Rita Hope is to give a pianoforte recital
at the Melbourne Masonic Hall on November 13,
which is the Saturday after Cup week. At about
the same time last year, when young Rita was
just back from Vienna with the LesChetizlcy hall-
mark new upon her, she had a concert at the
Town Hall and proved to be a prdfitess in her
own country. This time she will figure more
ambitiously, for there are two or three concertos
in the programme. A colleague of the male per-
suasion is to assist, when necessary, at a second
piano whilst the little lady devotes her art and
energies to piano No. 1. The recital will be run
on what has come to be known as the Conserva-
torium plan. Invitation tickets are issued, and
the holders pay a booking fee for their seats, an
arrangement which appeals equally to the social
and financial feelings of the best musical people.

Two long strangers were added to the
Rickards bill, at the Opera House, the other night.
Walter Emerson is a comedian who might easily
pass for a giant in an emergency, and Charles
Johnson, who is only less lofty, calls himself the
champion colored cake-walker of the world, with-

at close quarters. The Trapnell Sisters are leaving

and Bertha Stuart are still included with the
remaining members of the company.
Just one short year ago the Sydney Tele-
graph and Herald could not find language to
express their detestation of the caucus scheme,
whereby, they said, the minority in a party was
compelled to sink its individual opinion and vote
against its principles. But times have changed.
The Fusion holds caucuses now, and at one held
on Thursday last Prime Minister Deakin did not
hesitate to browbeat his party and demand that it
should vote solidly as he desired it, regardless of
its conscience. Members were asked not merely
to sink their opinions, but to go back on their
speeches. And neither the Telegraph nor the
Herald arose to express its detestation worth 2d.
AT POVERTY POINT.
A guinea prize in a London paper called forth a remark-
able variety of Shakspearian allusions to Bardsley’s
double century in the last Test cricket match. The bard
has quite a lot of references to “ashes,” “pavilions,”
“ matches ” and such things. “I’ll wipe away all trivial,
fond records” was a neat touch from “Hamlet,” and
“He hath in this action outdone his former deeds
doubly,” from “ Coriolanus.” But in this kind of com-
petition a pun on names always seems to score best.
Industrious searchers found “Even so thou out-runn’st
Grace ” (“ Timon of Athens ”), “ Gregory never did such
deeds as I have done this day ” (“ Henry IV.”), and (this
was the prize-winner):—
I do not think a braver gentleman,
More active-valiant or more valiant-young,
More daring or more bold, is now alive
To Grace this latter age with Noble deeds.
—Henry IV. (Part 1), Act V., Scene 1.
“ D.”: A fact which struck me while watching the
“ Candida” show the other day in a Sydney theatre was
the indubitable one that G.B. Shaw lacks what one may
call the “dress sense.” Why not a “dress sense” as
well as a “picture sense”? Shaw has absolutely no
glimmering of an idea as to what a modern male should
put on to prevent himself from looking an outrage
in the face of nature. He betrays this in his own
person as well as in that of most of his characters.
I have seen him bicycling about London 6ft. of
dun-colored Jajger clothing and boots and hat—an
apparition which any modest-minded man would
yearn to wipe off the face of the earth. I heard him lec-
ture years ago in a hall near William Morris’s house at
Hammersmith, and his elaborately careless all-red garb
burnt itself into my youthful brain, and stirred me to a
passion of inarticulate protest. Awful, indeed, are the
clothes which he gives his best-loved characters. For
example, Leonard Charteris (“ The Philanderer ”), the
man whom all the women of his set adore, “ is unconven-
tionally but smartly dressed in a velvet jacket and cash-
mere trousers. His collar, dyed Wotan blue, is part of his
shirt, and turns over a garnet-colored scarf of Indian silk,
secured by a turquoise ring. He wears blue socks and
leather sandals.” I think this deficiency in what I call a
dress sense amounts actually to a defect in the Shaw art.
Take the case of Charteris. We are asked to accept him
as a slayer of female hearts. Now the immemorial woman
can take all sorts of pride in a badly or care-
lessly dressed man; but she will not be seen, if
she can help it, in company with a Merry-Andrew. And
no woman loves the man she is ashamed to be seen with.
Women are notoriously the most conventional of
creatures. There are few that I have ever met capable
of philandering with a veritable Antinous provided he
was so ill-advised as to court the public gibe in “ a velvet
coat and cashmere trousers.” The thought of one of
these self-conscious beings, so keenly sensitive to the
ridiculous, whether in themselves or those around them,
gladly enduring the attention of one who, in addition to
the other things, wore “ blue socks and sandals,” strikes
me as being one of the most incredible things in literature.
“Harrison ,0.”: “ O.K.’s” remarks (B. l/7/’O9) concern-
ing the “ happy ending” which dramatic moralist H. A.
Jones almost invariably tacks on to any play of his have
set me thinking. After all, is the happy ending as popu-
lar as it is generally supposed to be? Plays such as “ The
Second Mrs. Tanqueray ” and “ Hedda Gabler” can afford
to conclude miserably ; the people who can appreciate
plays of this class prefer—or, at any rate, tolerate—an
artistic suicide, and do not demand an inartistic marriage.
But putting Art aside, when one calls to mind the most
popular dramatic successes, it is astonishing how many
end in tears, despair or gloom. “ The Sign of the Cross ”
ends with Mercia making a bee-line for the lions’ den.
The popular version of “Camille” ends in a sob of
agony from Armand as he falls across the corpse
of the frail, fair one. In “A Royal Divorce” Julius
Buonaparte is left standing upon a rock at St.
Helena, gazing with intense longing at a back-cloth
horizon. Just consider what an opportunity the drama-
tist missed here : he could have brought about a “ fusion ”
between Josephine and Julius, and left them standing in
a large patch of limelight while the lady blushingly in-
formed her lord that, after all his doubts and fears, there
was a possibility of her becoming interesting. The man
who would sacrifice such a conclusion to mere more-or-
less historical accuracy is deserving of the censure of the
gods. The most remarkable of all modern play-endings
is perpetrated by ’All Caine in the most volcanic of his
eruptions, “The Christian.” The last spasm of this
play is extremely difficult to classify, for it raises the
question : Is a marriage between a dying fanatic and a
reformed actress a “ happy ending ”?
“Locust”: The verdict against Wirth’s circus in the
recent accident case reminds me that the “ colossal ”
American circuses have a special man employed to nip
actions in the bud. In the big street parades, which are
a feature of the Barnum and other tours, when ele-
phants, giraffes, horses, animals in Cages, agents,
supers, clowns and other dangerous animals all
turn out and march through the town, there are
numerous accidents from pushing crowds, or by
reason of inquisitive little boys getting close to cages
to make dead sure if the inmates really are lions
or tigers. The “agent” is on the spot, and as soon as
there is an injury which might be actionable, he comes
up, purrs consolation, and offers a lump sum in satisfac-
tion, if the injured will sign an indemnity from legal
proceedings. It nearly always works, and has a wonder-
ful curative effect upon the victim ; for it is very difficult
for a patient who is claiming damages to get quite well
until the jury has assessed the damages.
The Taits are going to put the success of the Besses o’
th’ Barn band to the test of a revival. The Besses are to
tour shortly, and return to Australia in March
or April next year. A renewal of the old boom seems
hardly possible, but the band this next time will have
“ a champion cornet soloist,” and, if he be a real
champion, he will be a strong draw. The present gene-
ration of Australian has little or no idea of the exciting
possibilities dormant in a cornet solo.
Tom Driver, a compositor in a local printing shop,
lately drew the largest show audience that Wellington
(M.L.) can remember. Driver used to wander around
Australasia with a small conjuring show ; if he got a £lO
house he was delighted, though the only reason he didn’t
gather in more was that he didn’t scream loud enough.
So he dropped conjuring for a while. Then Bailey, the
“ medium,” came along with his “apports” and other
ghostly and spectral baggage. Tom Driver, who used
to be “ Professor Kudarz,” suddenly left his case to
gather the scalp of Bailey ; and amid tremendous applause
he produced spirits of a better brand than Bailey’s best.
The Royal Comics have lost one of their dainty dancers
—Jessie, of the Brown sisterhood, which has been in the
J.C. W. productions for some years. The lady who has
given over pointing a toe at the footlights did promising
work as Lucille in “ The Girls of Gottenberg,” and was
one of the Girdle “kids ” in “ The Spring Chicken.”
“D.J.”: A queer brace of musical fakirs has appeared
at Sydney Tivoli. They are tricked out in the alleged
apparel of the British workman—bowyangs, corduroy
breeks, short cutty pipes and so on. The curtain rises to
disclose them in the vicinity of a small harmonium.
One concludes that they are professional harmonium-
removers, and are there in the execution of their duty.
After a cursory glance at the instrument, one of them
advances on it, seats himself upon a box and causes
the property street to resound with “ The Lost
Chord.” The other joins in. Both sing the entire
ditty with much vigor. Meanwhile the harmonium
remains unmoved. The bogus artisans rise, acknowledge
the guarded plaudits of the audience with elabo-
rately clumsy bobs and forelock-draggings indicative
of the son of toil in an unaccustomed character, and then
once more assault the harmonium to the tune of
“ Sweet Genevieve.” They still don’t attempt to move
anything. The street remains deserted. No infuriated
employer turns up and asks what the blazes they mean
by wasting their time (which is his money) in so profitless
a fashion. The local policeman fails to emerge from
hiding. All is peace and leisure and dolce far niente.
Again the twain bursts into song. Once more the har-
monium stays unshifted. More bobs, more forelock-
tearings, and the turn is over. What I rise to ask is:
Why the clothes ? Anything less appropriate in the
circumstances it were hard to conceive. They aren’t
picturesque, and they tend to suggest that the
wearers are unscrupulous folks who go about
getting harmonium-removing money under false
pretences, and ruining their employer’s instru-
ment, to boot, by performing upon it, all unauthorised, in
the damp street air. The songs which these false alarm
toilers render give them a particularly snuffling and
hypocritical aspect. The ditties are all about honesty
and virtue ; the Tenderers are palpable neglectors of duty,
poseurs, deceivers of their good kind masters and all the
other things that are unsympathetic to a right-minded
'Arry Rickards crowd. If they sang about burglary or
even petty theft the effect would be better and more
convincing. Their staves, their clothes and their actions
disharmonise, as things are, most villainously.
“ Mugg ” : Snips, most deadly of buckjumpers, repaired
to the Elysian pastures last week with a broken foreleg.
He fell at Many Peaks (Queensland) with a local horseman
and had to be destroyed. I was maniac enough to get on
Snips once. A remembrance of the episode will remain
with me to the tomb. I never had a briefer ride in my
life. My legs gripped the saddle, and then a convulsion
of Nature seemed to occur—a wild phenomenon which was
part earthquake, part whirlwind, and part simoon,
with a dash of dynamite explosion and aeroplane
smash thrown in. When I was able to remember
my debts I was amongst the wooden benches sur-
rounding the arena wherein Snips gambolled nightly.
I had been thrown clean over the dividing fence.
A bump the size of a cricket ball disfigured the top of my
bright young skull. My back felt as though a Dread-
nought had fallen upon it from a great height. The
crowd was delighted. Snips was more formidable than
Bobs at the latter’s worst, for he was quicker-actioned
and more savage. Bobs has alw'ays struck me as
a sort of “ made ” buckjumper. He knew what
was expected of him as well as the Winston
seals, and did it conscientiously and effectively.
But he never brought to his work the insane,
frenzied, screaming enthusiasm that Snips did. • That
section of the world which admires to see its fellow-man
humbled in the dust (or amongst the benches, as the case
may be) and grievously injured and rendered a gore-
smeared laughing-stock, will be the poorer for the passing
of this demented quadruped.
“ Cynic ” : Somebody who professes to know what he is
talking about informs The Bulletin that he “ has been
applauding EllalineTerrissin adult characters for the past
20 years,” but the wild alligator omits to mention the
names and dates of the first productions in which he saw
the lady. Ellaline is the daughter of murdered William
Terris, who was born just 60 years ago and made
his first notable appearance in London, as a very
young man, in December, 1871. When I met him,
two or three years later, he was unmarried, I believe.
The alligator goes on to say: “The Dango lady
I know not, except from the wrong side of the footlights ;
but if she is 30 I will engage to eat the big drum.” I
would like to see him devour the instrument without
more ado. Miss Dango was introduced to Australia as a
sister of Lettv Lind, a charming danseuse and actress of
the Gaiety Co., who came to Australia in July, 1888, and
was then said to be “about six-and-twenty.” I don’t
know whether Letty was the eldest and Fanny the
youngest member of the family, but assuming, for the
sake of argument, a difference of 17 years in their ages,
it is still evident that the alligator aforesaid must either
eat the drum or forfeit his engagement.
Dear Bulletin,— Touching the youthfulness and charm-
ingness of the character Miss Dango plays in “The
Catch of the Season,” the fiend who wrote The Bul-
letin's notice rises to mention that he “didn’t pen it
under considerable of a misconception,” as “ Ceph ” sug-
gests. The fact, or allegation, that Miss Ellaline Terriss,
for whom the part was written (she being the wife of the
manager and part author) was older at the time than Miss
Dango’s present age has absolutely nothing to do with
the case. Neither is the fact that Miss Terriss can’t
sing any argument in support of the contention that Miss
Dango’s voice is equal to the requirements of the music of
a soubrette part which happens to be poetically dainty.
The part is that of a 17-or 18-year-old damsel, of beauty
so remarkable that all the men are struck silly by it. In
short, the character is Cinderella, and the Melbourne
stage just now has no lady on the musical comedy job
who is quite so young and charming as the ideal Cinder-
ella.—Yours, etc., The Fiend Himself.
St. Kilda (Melb.) is about to strengthen its attractions
as a show place by the sea. Edward Branscombe, encour-
aged by the financial results of the Scarlet Troubadours,
has fixed up, on similar lines, a little comedy-cwm-variety
co. under the name of “The Jesters ’’—nine souls in all—
who will start to jest at Arcadia on October 30. Arcadia
is on the St. Kilda Esplanade, opposite the Figure 8, and
near to where Dreamland fretted its fleeting day of yore.
Premier Murray will presumably go to the Melbourne
Waxworks and weep bitterly during the next few weeks.'
A keen, robust-looking alien, with a number of medals
hanging from him, has retired to the transparent seclusion
of a glass-case at the Waxworks with a view to living
there for 53 days, nourished only by soda water and
cigarettes. As a teetotaler, this disconcerting crank
holds the record for temperance; but as a smoker he’s
just a Fiend. On the one hand, the “Fasting” Man
appeals for Murray’s best blessing, and, on the other, he
is a shocking- example of the habit that Murray would
like to suppress by Act of Parliament. In Britain alone,
leaving- other countries out of the question, he has fasted
on soda water and cigarettes for nearly two years in
periods ranging from 14 to 52 days. Smoking as many as 50
cigarettes a day, he ought to be a nervous wreck. Taking
nothing but soda water with his nicotine, he ought to
have died of heart failure, orsomething, longago. Instead
of being wrecked or dead Sacco Homann, the fiend in
question, earns a good living by sitting in a glass room
and inviting the medical profession to assist at the
funeral of its pet theories. Other fasting men have
proved nothing that was not already known. This one
proves that cigarette smoking is the healthiest of occu-
pations for a lazy citizen who draws very little fresh air
into his lungs and takes liberal draughts of soda water
into his stummick. Apparently he feeds his will power
by doing what is said to weaken will power. He employs
two negatives to make an affirmative. He is the most
interesting waster in the world, for he spends three stone
of flesh at one sitting in baffling medical science.
“ Ossian ”: Melba probably struck her most mixed
congregation in Grafton (N.S.W.). Behind me sat Combo
and black Rosy, his gin. Combo didn’t care for it and
wanted to quit, but Rosy held him back —“ Him lovely
music, Combo.” So Combo grunted and went to sleep.
Presently Melba produced one of her trills. “ Combo,
Combo!” she said. “Listen, Combo—whaffor all the
same lolanthe’s whistle ! ” The lolanthe is the ferry boat
that shrieks its way up and down the local river.
From “S.”:
There is a deuce of a lot of aesthetic and religious
clamor against the public boxing displays going on in Mel-
bourne just now, and not a Woodfuil of the whole jugful
utters one passionate yelp against Marathon races. I’ve
watched boxing closely for 10 years, and have never seen
a boxer fought into a condition of blind weariness like
that of half a score of the runners who finished the 26-mile
race here the other day. Some of them could scarcely
stagger along, sagging pitiably, sightless, and quite crazed
from protracted strain. They were beaten 'into wakeful-
ness with wet towels, and encouraged along the final two
or three miles by clamorous followers who sprayed them,
squoze orange juice between their teeth, and performed
every office necessary to keep the done men from falling
in their tracks. This was a cruel exhibition, if you like ;
but no Woodfuil denounces Marathon races.
Colonel Burston is Melbourne’s gaud
Mayor for the second time ; he beat Davey, who
was Weedon’s understudy when the little man
was away fishing for a title, by 14 to 12. Burston,
who isn’t personally over-popular in the council,
can thank the late “ Bill ” Cook for his re-election.
Cook, a plasterer by trade, was a great under-
ground engineer, and got from “ Tiger ” Gardner
(the old South Melbourne “ruck” footballer)
and others a promise that Burston should have a
second term. When it was seen that Davey
couldn’t win, an effort was made to bring out
Gillott again; but the gentleman with the
milliner’s-rnodel figure wouldn’t take it on except
with the unanimous vote of the council ; and he
didn’t have a hope of getting that with Burston
in the field.
A cry from Newcastle :
The McMyler hoist and the generally inadequate coal
loading appliances of Newcastle are subjects upon which
Premier Wade has of late been speechifying with much
numerousness. Wade wants the country to believe that
he is doing great things for Coalopolis ; but, in attempt-
ing to prove that the McMyler is all that it is not, his
latest “ statement to the press” makes him look more
remarkable than ever. Wade said that the McMyler will load
Maitland hard coals without breaking them, and when the
soft coals of Newcastle are worked out the McMyler will be
just the thing. That is all very interesting in its way,
but Professor David estimates the unwon soft coal of the
Borehole and Burwood seams as 2,562,000,000 tons, and
during last year a little over 6,000,000 tons were raised
in the combined Newcastle and Maitland districts. The
unwon soft coal at that rate will last just over 400 years
before it is all worked out, and even Wade should be able
to realise that that is a long time to wait for improved l ,
loading appliances.

show for the £200,000,000 or so that he spent in.

Chow miner whom the Yidsteins of Johannesburg

wouldn’t let them have, proved such a horribly

were mighty glad to be quit of their bargain. So

ling, scratching pack, Bull’s friend Yidstein said
as usual that he would be ruined, and then hung
round in fear and trembling lest Bull should
change his mind, and not take them off his hands.
So the Chow contingent has been gradually
shipped back out of his slave-* 1 compound,” and
last week the very tail-end of the curse said
" Whaffor?” and was bundled down to the coast.
A BRAW SCAWTCH JEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You know, I am Scotch.” (Laughter.) Foster
Fraser.
“ I am Scotch, but my wife is Irish.” (Boars of
laughter.)— Gov. Carmichael.
Speaking at a meeting of the Sydney Jewish Literary
and Debating Society, George Reid said he would like to
become a member, only he was a Scotchman. (Uncon-
trollable merriment.)
I paid three silver shillings for a stall—
Three hardly-earned and most infrequent bob —
And sat within a darkened lecture hall,
Among a mob
Of persons who had gathered there to hear
A travel-wearied journalist discourse
Re Russia—where blood flows like Christmas beer 1
Without remorse.
He showed us pictures of the mangled dead ;
But later how we rocked with laughter when
He slyly screwed his features up and said
“ Ah’m Scawtch, ye ken.” . . .
Next morn I ope’d my paper in the train
And saw the headline, “ Gov’nor’s Witty Speech ” ;
I read it through—the phrasing caused me pain—
I did not screech
With laughter. O, ’twas sad, ’twas passing sad
(To put it vulgarly, ’twas tommy rot).
I found one single joke— which made me mad—
“ I am a Scot.” <
I turned the page, another headline saw :
“ A Speech by Mr. Reid.” “ Here’s wisdom ripe,”
I mused,“This orator, this man of law,
Will scorn such tripe.”
My faith in him received a horrid blow ;
He said—unless the printer made a botch
Of setting the report: “ I’d have you know
(Yes-no) I’m Scotch.” . . .
No doubt I’m dull, but I confess I fail
To comprehend this deep and subtle joke :
Such wit I fear is hidden by a veil
From common folk.
O ! why allow the humor to escape
Poor common mortals till the Day of Doom?
Won’t someone please resolve this braw Scotch jape ?
Dispel the gloom I
VICTORIA.
HARRISON OWEN.
THE IMPERIALIST’S VIEWPOINT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Earl Gray is wrong in supposing that any baby navy
belonging to a colony can safeguard commerce on the
sea.—Sydney Sunday Times.
In the voice of the rooster at dawn,
Making melody loud on the lawn,
Lo, the Sunday Times speaks
On Defence, and it leaks,
Fairly leaks cold contempt of the spawn
Who no longer on Britain would fawn.
“Guard themselves!" cries the Times, shedding
tears ;
“ Why the thing is preposterous ! Years—
Countless years must go by
’Ere such people may try
To do that which a fair deal appears
To the Dago, with rings in his ears.
“ There is only one thing to be done
By good patriot-colonists—one
And no more ; ’tis to lie
In the shade (or a sty)
And hire others to handle the gun
When the foreign dog grabs at their bone.
“ For this proposition is plain,
If they struggled again and again
To produce fighting men
The betting is ten
Unto one they would have all their pain
And exertion and worry in vain.”
It’s an axiom, say those who shriek
For the Empire, that none but the weak
And the useless abide
Or are bred on this side ;
He who’s looking for fighters must streak
To the land which is ancient and bleak.
N.S.W.

Thus “Gent One,” from Melbourne :
Lastweekthe Vic. Full Court delivered judgment on
?an application by the Age newspaper for a new trial of
the libel case that had resulted in politician Swinburne
■ obtaining a verdict for heavydamages (and Costs) against
the journal aforesaid. Two sets of learned counsel spent
four days in piling up some more Costs, although it was
• obvious from the beginning of the hearing- that all argu-
ments in favor of a new trial were wasted on the Full
1 Court. The barristers were there to talk, and they talked
- on unchecked by the learned Judges until they could
think of nothing more to sajv After which the
learned Judges reserved their learned judgment, not
because they hadn’t made up their minds, but
in order that one of them might prepare a
preposterously long explanation of his reasons for refus-
ing a new trial, whilst the other two Judges jotted down
a few well-chosen remarks in support of their long-
winded brother’s deliverance. The Age’s application was
a lamentable mistake. The pretence of the Full Court
in professing to give the question its gravest attention
was strictly legal and quite according to precedent. It
is the business of Full Courts to give as much nega-
tive encouragement as possible to the piling up of Costs.
In this hearing, which lasted five days in all, there was
but one simple question to consider. A new trial was asked
for on the ground, mainly, that one of the 12 jurymen
who awarded heavy damages and Costs to the fortunate
Swinburne had conversed with a clerk in the employ of
the Age’s solicitors whilst the trial was proceeding. His
conversation, according to the solicitor’s clerk, had ended
with a suggestion that “ somebody from the Age office
had better see him," otherwise a verdict in favor of
Swinburne would assuredly result. The juryman, for his
part, denied that he had made such a suggestion. Had
he admitted it his bare admission would not have
amounted to any imputation on the honesty of the
other 11 jurymen who had practical]}’ decided to
find a verdict for Swinburne. But on the strength
of what their lawyer’s clerk told them concerning this
conversation, the barristers appearing for the Age had
the legal “gall ” to demand that the whole jury should
be disfranchised, and the libel suit tried all over again
before a new jury. Their “bluff” failed as a matter of
course, whereupon they withdrew from the case in mock
indignation, leaving the jury and Judge to laugh at
them behind their backs. The fortunate Swinburne got
his verdict a day or two sooner than would have happened
if the defendant’s barristers had remained on the job, but
otherwise “ the course of justice ” was unaffected. Vet
the appeal for a new trial was neither more nor less than
a second attempt to bring off the bluff that had so sig-
nally failed in the first instance. A number of other
arguments were dragged in, but none of these amounted
to a row of pins. The Full Court was asked to say that
if in the last stage of a costly law case the barristers on
one side can get information, through a juryman, that
their side has not a ghost of a chance to win they shall
have the right to cancel the trial. Naturally the Full
Court said “ Rats 1”
“ Craignathan,” where a fatal fire occurred
on Sunday morning, is an historic bouse at Neutral
Bay, Sydney. It was built in the thirties of last
century by persons now forgotton. Benjamin
Boyd, the ill-fated pioneer and speculator, after-
wards occupied it, and from a large tank on the
premises, fed by a small stream which still finds
its way to the head waters of the Bay, he watered
his whalers. Later on the old house became the
property of John Mann, explorer and surveyor,
who married a daughter of another explorer,
Thomas Livingstone Mitchell. When Mann sold
the place to Leonard Dodds, the latter added a
storey to the house and let it as a hash estab-
lishment. The locality is now known as Hash
Town, from the fact that one lady runs three
large boarding-houses at the Bay tram terminus.

From a man who has seen much service in
“ the East ” :
The heathen rages and threatens a boycott of Aus-
tralian produce. The local Chinaman says he wants a
competent Chinese teacher to diffuse a knowledge of
literary Chinese, presumably among the barbarians of
Australia, just when tne people of China itself are pitch-
ing literary Chinese and all belonging to it into the dust-
heap, and pinning their faith to European science. This
recalls the “ Hindu revival ” ; in that case—and it might
be the same in this—the Asiatic had to get Europeans to
teach him to understand his own language and literature.
When the Indian Government took in hand the investiga-
tion of Indian antiquities, the officials found to their
horror that the oldest sacred books of the Hindus were in
a language quite unintelligible to the most learned Hindu
pundits, who had long ago given the thing up as a bad
job or a sacred mystery. A German, Dr. Hang, tackled
the work* and in due time produced a translation of the
“ Vedas,!’ with a grammar and dictionary of the old
dialect in which they were written. In fact, he taught
the Hindus their own sacred language. British officials
have sinCe then compiled grammars and dictionaries of
several Indian dialects —Sindhi, for instance, which was
never before reduced to writing. Likewise, the European
interpreters in the Consular service have done very
much in the way of clearing up Chinese history and
philology. The Asiatic is a feckless creature, and if the
Chinese of Australia really want a competent professor
of Chinese they will have to get a white man to fill the
bill, for no yellow person really knows half so much about
his own?language as some dozen Europeans who could be
named?
NAUGHTY.
“ Let's go gay for once.”
“ Yes, let's visit this cincmatograjihc exhibition.”
Westralia is about to acquire a novelty in
the shape of an industrial colony in which all
shares, stock and land are to he held by
women, and unattached women at that. The
concern seems to Vie pushed along by the British
Householders’ League, of which Mrs. Emily
'Crawford, Miss Brooke (of the Worcester Agricul-
tural College) and Miss Hetty Sawyer, M.D., are
leading lights. Their intentions, though strictly
honorable, are somewhat vague at present; but it
will interest Australia’s Tories to learn that they
picked on Australia as a camping ground because
women have the franchise here. That, of course,
doesn’t apply solely to the Western State, but
possibly the relative scarcity of lovely woman
there may have had a deciding influence.-

IST PUNTER : “ The mare ran badly. She never seemed to tale hold of her bit."
2ND PUNTER: “I didn’t notice. I know that Moses Lazarus has got hold of my bit."
“ J.” tells of a horror
The annual report of the British Humanitarian League
came my way last week. It ranges over every variety of
horror, from capital punishment to the “blooding” of
children at fox-hunts. Incidentally, it touches on “Snake-
feeding at Zoos.” My interest quickened, for I was privi-
leged once to visit a python during its dinner-hour, and
the memory has never faded. The mighty reptile lay
coiled in its glass marisipn asleep. The roof was lifted
and an infuriated gamecock was lowered protesting]}’
down. In order that it shouldn’t career about and in-
commode the serpent when the moment of its doom
was due to strike, a string was attached to its leg and
fastened outside. Then the python was stirred up with
a stick. A long, slow wave coursed adown its gaudy
frame, its gleaming eyes twinkled and its flat head was
projected in the direction of the ’pock. Was the bird
afraid ? No ; or, if it was, it was so in the sense only that
heroes are afraid. It remained captain of its soul. It
didn’t quail or fall back an inch. On the contrary, with
a kind of forlorn-hope-Balaclava-charge expression of
countenance, it actually advanced on its enemy, and was
only checked from spurring it by the limits of its leash.
Three several times it sprang at the awful face, now
stationary opposite it, and then desisted hopelessly. It
seemed to realise it had a waiting game to play, and drew
back so that when the attack came it would, at the
worst, have a few' inches of freedom wherein to
pass out fighting. And now' a strange thing hap- -
pened. The serpent grew cold on the business. Its
meal was too aggressive. To the anguish of the
attendant, it coiled up, with its head as far as possible
from the frantically-watchful game-bird, and composed
itself for sleep. With many an immoral objurgation, the
python’s valet dragged forth the fowl that knew not fear,
and bawled directions to a colleague outside. The col-
league entered shortly with a small, white hen. There
was nothing heroic about this bird. It looked harmless
and silly and respectable and unobtrusive. The roof was
lifted once more, and the new-comer placed inside. The
mighty bulk of the python reclined, immovable, near it.
It paid no attention, but walked about briskly, pecking
sharply at the grains of wheat on the floor. Again the
reptile was prodded, and again it raised its head.
The wretched hen looked unsuspectingly up to meet
the baleful glare of its companion fixed full upon
it. It fainted—that is the only word to express
it. Its legs gave gradually away and it collaused
to a sitting position. Violent shudders shook it." I
fancied I could discern an icy sweat upon its brow. The
python seemed gratified. Something resembling a smile
passed over its grim visage. Slowly it surged towards
the terror-stricken fowl. The latter moved not, but
rocked from side to side in helpless agony. The holocaust
was about due. I had paid a bribe to witness the
tragedy, and wasted time waiting for it to transpire, but I
had seen enough. I broke for the door and never returned.
I don’t know that I wouldn’t as soon attend a hanging as
standby again while a snake rang its dinner bell. The
snake, by the way, is a habitual swallower of its own kind
alive. An all-merciful Providence fixed things that wav.

Joseph Collinson, secretary of the Humani-
tarian League, London, defines “Incorrigible
Rogues” as they are understood in England
m r ' •. , . i r .... .. , ...
The title of the Vagrant Act, under which one Thomas
B J' rneB - <*>tat. 65, and allegedly, an “ incorrigible rogue,”
WIIS sentenced recently to 12 months’ hard labor, and 12
strokes with the birch, is a misnomer; and it is also a mis-
nomer to describe the persons who are liable to flogging
u,lder as “ incorrigible rogues.” Briefly, “incorrigible
ro k, ue ” 18 a legal or technical term designed to cover a
multitude of dissimilar offenders, but m practice it rarely
amounts to anything, worse than a person who has been
re P eate diy convicted at Petty Sessions of any one or
more of the following, viz., begging from door to door,
sln tring in the streets and soliciting alms, endeavoring to
oollect charitable contributions under false pretences,
exposing wounds or deformities in a public place, sleep-
lng out without visible means, destroying clothes jn a
workhouse or refusing to perform the allotted task, refrac-
conduct in police-cells, assaulting the constable when
apprehended for peddling .without a .license, fortune-tell-
mg, palmistry, etc. Some of these alleged “ incorrigibles ”
are unemployed laborers who have never been convicted
of any serious offence in their lives. They have been un-
fortunate in their battle with the world, and have gradu-
ally slipped out of work through inefficiency. According
to the recent report of the Departmental Committee on
Va k ran cy, such men form the readiest recruits for the

AN APPALLING FAILURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fremantle, Tuesday.
Among the passengers arriving at Adelaide by the
Scharnhorst was Mr. W. J. P. Giddings, editor of Fauld-
jng’s Medical Journal. Mr. Giddings said : “ The status
of the delegates aroused great resentment among mem-
bers of the Press Club of Great Britain, where it was felt
that the delegates should have been composed of working
journalists, instead of alleged pressmen, nviny of whom
travelled merely on the names of their papers. . . . Mr.
Fink, representing the Melbourne Herald, was a lawyer ;
Mr. Syme, of the Age, a doctor ; and of the New South
Wales delegates one was proved to be an engineer,
another an auctioneer. Working journalists from over-
seas would have been much more welcome.
Fremantle, Wednesday.
Among the passengers arriving by the P. and O.
steamer Macedonia to-day was Mr. W. L. Baillieu, Minis-
ter for Works in Victoria, who is returning from a
private trip to the Motherland. Interviewed, he said
there was a wonderful amount of ignorance in Australian
affairs shown in England. He suggested what a fine idea
it would be for the Commonwealth to send over to Great
Britain a picked journalist with instructions to dissemi-
nate news concerning Australia and its resources with
the best methods. “ They have no idea of our aspira-
tions,” concluded Mr. Baillieu. — Sample of news items
dropping in re alleged “ Press” Conference.
Alas ! And is it possible ! Can it, indeed, be true ?
Should we not weep hot tears, bitter tears, burning drops
of regret ?
Have all our prayers and entreaties, and all the royal,
ducal and general shivoos miscarried and come to
naught?
Did our delegates, our representative journalists, mighty
Nation-Pushers and Exponents of the Crimson Bread
of Kingship fail us ?
Are their efforts, their speeches, their posings, their
sufferings of no avail?
Is it credible that those who suffered stomach and head-
ache for their country—
Who braved so many perils—
Attended so many parties—
iDragged unfamiliar clobber round in strange places and
amid unfamiliar company in the name and on behalf
of Australia—
Is it credible, I ask, that these persons did NOT accom-
plish their job?
We’ll not believe it!
Hence, horrible rumor, hence !
WHAT ?
Were all those meals eaten, those drinks drunk, those
clothes worn, those remarks made for NOTHING ?
Does no duchess understand that we are not all black in
Australia, no London editor believe that we have at
least nine more policemen than politicians?
Do the Royal Society, the Balloon Society, the Society
for the Protection of Alb-ns, and “Society” gener-
ally not understand that there are actually real
sparrotvs out here ?
WHAT?
Did Theodore Fink, Mark Cohen and the rest of them
leave the great British public in ignorance of the fact
that we understand high, low and broad finance and
practise it in all its blanches?
Is it possible that our aspirations were not referred to ;
that our ideals were taken as read ?
Did none mention WADE and the great city of Yass-
Gehenna, and Australia’s National Debt of so-elegant
and millionantic proportions?
, Himmel und Kiimmel ! Aeh Gott!
■ Oh, oh ! Those interviews !
-Oh, oh ! Those portraits in the Daily Nuisance and the
Weekly Pkrturbbr and the Court Toady !
| Oh, oh ! Those at-great-length-reported-and-immediately-
cabled-out speeches !
'Oh, oh ! Those royal, and ducal, and Lord-mayoral hand-
shakes, and that bonhomie of marchionesses off the
chain !
<Oh, oh, oh, oh, oh, oh, Oil ! ! !
All wasted —lost —vanished—shadowy—useless— gone !
They have no idea of our aspirations ! ”
OH ! . . .
Who are we to have aspirations?
Brothers, if all those mighty thinkers, writers, philoso
phers, poets and journalists—
Those Movers of Things Here—
Those Who Chose Themselves To Do The Job—
If THEY could not explain, develop, illustrate our poor,
little, inarticulate aspirations and things—
Who CAN !
Oh, doctor !
oh, lawyer!
Oh, auctioneer !
Oh, aspirations !
Oh, our poor country !
ICHABOD.
N.s.w.
O. O. CABOT.

Victoria’s honorariums have been taking a
serious view of their duties again. The other
day two of them, at Port Melbourne, considered
the case of James Armstrong and his wife Ellen.
Armstrong had obliged the lady with a black eye,
a bruised forehead arid a cut face, so the awful
honorariums rewarded him with a fine of one
shilling ! They would doubtless have handed him
a trifle from the poor-box, only unfortunately
there was nothing in it at the moment.
* ■* * «
Hobart folks have just learned that
persons who die at the local charitable institution
are interred in true “ rattle-his-bones ” fashion.
Like Jehoiakim of Judah, they are “buried with
the burial of an ass.” The bodies are placed in a
rough coffin), a bit of tin is tacked on for a name-
plate, and the name or number of the departed is
scratched thereon with a
nail or something of the
sort. Tha coffin is then
carted to the cemetery and
dumped into a deep hole
wherein perhaps 10 or 15
predecessors already repose
on top of each ocher. The
pressure on the bottom
predecessor is terrible. It
wouldn’t be so bad if the
coffins were decently
covered with earth; but
the other day a newspaper
man glanced into the open
hole and discovered a dead
man’s coffin-plate staring
up at him with a surprised
expression on its kerosene-
tin countenance. It had
stared up atthe sky through
the interstices of a wooden
shutter flung over the
mouth of the hole for over
a fortnight. In the sub-
sequent trouble it trans-
pired that a pauper’s
funeral costs, at contract
price, 305., five of which go
to a clergyman. The items
are coffin (including the
tin naipe - plate), 15s. ;
burying the same (whien isn’t done), 10s.; prayers
(which a priest admits in a letter to the papers
are not always read at the time the box is dropped
into the hole), ss. All things considered, 30s.
seems a ruinous price to pay for the job. Crema-
tion would be quite as cheap and a lot healthier
and more decent, and no wandering pressman
would be frightened into convulsions by a
fortnight-old corpse’s label staring up at him from
the bottom of a dark hole.
* * • *
“The Heathen” writes in his blindness:—
The Anglican Church seems to be an unhappy and con-
fused institution these days. The period of unrest and
recrimination began with the mysterious Canon Nash
shindy—a religious riot remarkable for the fact that the
cause of it was never once put into plain speech publicly
or printed in black and white for a harassed people to
read. In the art of skating round a subject and avoiding
the point at issue, the Nash case is a model of clerical
agility. Since the stage-thunder of that threepenny
persecution died down, the Anglican forces seem to
have been afflicted with discontent; and the everyday
cynic doesn t hesitate to ascribe it to Providence—a
bored and impatient Providence, tired to death of kitchen
clatter and small-beer vengeance. The discontent was
first voiced at the recent Adelaide Synod by some of the
rank and file country parsons, who spoke forcibly regard-
ing the smallness of their stipends, and asserted that the
business of soul-saving in the Backblocks was more like a
scramble from the Insolvency Court than a dignified
carrying of the VVord. On the same day that these state-
ments appeared in print, the tearful remarks of Sydney’s
new imported Archbishop were also presented to the
readers of the morning paper. He had not, he asserted,
dreamt of coming out here to crowd the religious field
and take the highest place and salary ; but the “ call ”
had come and he felt it his solemn and sacred duty to
ansvyer it. His friends also felt that way, and thev had
g*ot round him and unred him om So he was coming" at
full speed. Up to then the commissioned officers of the
Anglican soul-forces had not been heard to complain, and
the inference was that they at least were comfortable and
contented. But, a week or so back, Bishop Barlow,
of Goulburn (N.S.W.), rose up and denounced the practice
of raising" funds for the Lord’s work by means of race
meetings, dances and sports. Presumably, he objected
to being" placed in the humiliating" position of owing" the
upkeep of his gaiters to the bookmaker and the swift
animal at six-to-four. No alternative means of support
was suggested in reply; so it is reasonable to suppose
that he is still subsisting precariously on the proceeds of
four furlong flutters and the Merry Widow waltz. Hardly
had his protest faded into the back-numbers on the files,
when Bishop Anderson, at a meeting of parishioners
in Deniliquin, remaiked that “ it was a scandal and dis-
grace that clergymen were not properly supported.
During 12 years he had been compelled to spend £4OO a
year above his income for the privilege of being Bishop of
Riverina. He did not believe if he resigned that they could
get, the position filled. If the people could not pay, the
Church ought to put, up the shutters and close." The last
sentence puts the whole thing on a hard business basis,
and the introduction of the shop-word “shutters” is
particularly apt. Put brutally, itseems to be: ‘‘ If you can’t
pay us a living wage to save your souls, we might as well
nail up the shop, stow away the Bible and let you slide."
It is a fairly violent statement; but, if what the annoyed
shepherd says is correct, some violence is called for. To
the onlooking Pagan, the astonishing part of the whole
thing is, that while the workers in the Anglican part of the
vineyard assert that though they are shamefully underpaid
a strange archdeacon, who says he was perfectly happy
in his English charge, should be dragged across the seas
at great expense to fill the highest position and take the
biggest salary the Church has to offer. A religion run on
such lines will never gain the confidence or esteem of the
workers.
* • * *
The Commission that into the
management of Pentridge (Vic.) gaol got a size-
able hint lately from a couple of witnesses, the
foremen of the tailoring and the brush and broom
factories. They declared that practically every
prisoner under them loafed for all he was
worth. The prisoners could easily produce twice
as much in the time; but, these witnesses
said in effect, there was no incentive to do
so. As long as a man behaved himself,
the lazy one got his liberty as quickly as
the bogger-in. The remedy seems obvious.
Let the sentence be so many pounds instead of so
many days or months or years—so many pounds
to be earned by honest toil over and above the
cost of the prisoner’s board and lodging—and let
the gaol be made a hive of industry at which a
dozen or a score of trades are constantly in full
blast. Then the habit of sprinting like the very
devil would grow on the criminal community. As
things are the prisoner learns to be the slowest of
slow workers. As things might be he would learn
to be a streak of lightning.
A JUST COMPLAINT.
MISTRESS: “ The fact is, servants nowadays want
better treatment than their mistresses.”
MAID: “ Yes, mum, that's just the trouble. I
will not have master kissin’ me in the hall.”
Wilks’ Postal Commission almost justified
its lengthy existence the other day. when it dug
out some startling information about the oldish
damsels employed in the stamp branch of Mel-
bourne G.P.O. Several of these ladies got into
the branch as supernumeraries about 20 years ago,
and are still supernumeraries on a salary of one
whole quid a week each. Apparently they couldn’t
get appointed to the permanent staff with all
that staff’s superior privileges, although they
were doing the same work as the permanent
hands; and so they worked on while their
youth and beauty faded just as relentlessly as
their ardor. Permanent old age came upon them
in the middle of their temporariness.

At the Victoria Barracks, Sydney—soon
to pass into the hands of the old bricks and
timber speculator—there are some venerable relics
sprawling on the lawns as though they belonged
to nobody, and hadn’t a care in the world, or a
sin to answer for. One rusty piece—a squat,
podgy old girl—is of an unknown age, suggesting
that she was turned put during those times when
brands and dates did not matter, Half a
dozen are long guns of 4in. or sin. calibre,
with “G.R.” in raised letters of a glorified
size—probably meaning George Rex or George
Reid. A diminutive brass chap recalls an age
when the fighting masters went nap on that class
of machine. There is a swaggering piece pro-
claiming “G.R. and T.” on one part; on the base
it mentions that its German master had it made
in Germany—“l. and_P. Vergruggin Fecerunt,
1779.” The other members of the gun family are
mostly of early nineteenth-century origin ; but
science has placed an immense gulf betwixt them
and the death engines carried on the Dread-
noughts of these days.
The report of South Australia’s Lunacy
Commission ought to make even Private Enter-
prise do a little blushing. An official visitor
said :
A little while ago the potatoes were bad. I complained
right through about it, and they (the asylum authorities)
replied, “ Oh, well, it’s between seasons, and you cannot
get good potatoes, and the price is so low that the con-
tractor cannot be expected to supply them better at this
time of the year.”
It s a pathetic thing, this regard of the asylum
authorities for the poor contractor who tenders to
supply a standard of quality, and apparently
doesn’t carry out the bargain. Again :
They buy the bread by weight. The consequence is
that in the case of a contractor tendering at a low price
it is to his interests to see that the bread weighs heavy,
and that as much water is used as can be got into it.
The bread is often sodden, and pressed together in the
hands it makes almost a lump of dough.
The unfortunate Kings and Queens who inhabit
Parkside are compelled to eat this stuff whether
they like it or not; yet, as one witness declared,
the food had been so bad sometimes that the
mere attendants on the Kings and Queens would
not eat it. Probably Private Enterprise will offer
some explanation, but it’s up to the asylum to
make its own bread and to grow its own potatoes;
and if Private Enterprise must supply something,
it’s also up to the Government to pay a price
which won’t offer the shark any excuse to play
horrible tricks on these unfortunates.

The oceans always seem to be having trouble
witli their beds. The captain of the steamer
Wonga Fell, which arrived in Sydney lately from
Ocean Island, reported that on the passage across
the Pacific his ship suddenly found the waters
running about beneath it in all directions. The
force of the liquid riot threw the Wonga Fell tem-
porarily right off its course and shook it till its
teeth rattled. It was almost as hopeless as trying
to skate across the backs of a mob of stampeding
pigs, and navigation was practically suspended
until the rolling ship managed to kick its way
clear of the turmoil into calmer waters. The only
explanation the puzzled mariner could offer was
that the Pacific had been having some trouble
with its bed and was struggling to adjust it. The
clothes had slipped off or something.
At some time or other nearly every man
has looked at the sea and wished that he knew of
some way to harness the breakers so that he could
get an unlimited supply of power for nothing, and
thereby be enabled to manufacture some super-
fluous thing for next to nothing, amass an
immense fortune and pass the rest of his life
doing nothing. Some men have tried to make:
the sea work for them, but the results have beem
poor, and the waves mostly roar and rage with-
out profit and do nothing for their salt. A Vic-
torian at Sorrento is the latest to tackle the-
problem of harnessing the sea in a practical way,,
and he seems to have got nearer success tham
most. He has a model machine on the ocean side,,
and it generates 3-h.p. His confidence in the:
apparatus is such that he threatens to instal a-.
50-h.p. plant with which to light the town. It
he succeeds in making the ocean take off its .coate
and work for nothing, Niagara will retire to the
back row as a power source. A lot of waterfalls
have steady jobs now, and there doesn’t appear to
be any reason why the sea shouldn’t start work.
It is old enough, Lord knows.
THE ELEPHANT AND REFRESHMENTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the Sydney Zoo the receipts for last month were :
Prom gate, elephant and refreshments, £222 17s. lOd. ;
and payments, £5OO 16s. 3d.— News item.
The elephant looked at the old Bath bun
When the gate was closed and the day was done,
And he telephoned through his dusty trunk
When twenty-two gallons he’d softly drunk—
“ Friend Bath, I think it’s a shame, don’t you?
We two and the gate keep the whole darned Zoo !
Old Stripes is a bit of a draw, I know,
But a Bengal tiger don’t make a show.
Why, he and the Lion can’t raise a roar
Between ’em ! They lie on the dusty floor
And sleep like two loafers. They never growl;
The most they will do is to yawn and prowl—
While the people linger, and peer and say,
“W’y, they ’aven’t no life in ’em t’day ! ”
And they wish the family props were there,
So that they might stir up the lazy pair.
Then what does the blessed hyena do ?
D’ye think he brings custom to the Zoo ?
And the sacred bull and the cold, flat snake—
Just passengers both ! D’ye think they'd take
Two shillings a week if they toured the towns
In a circus teht? If they took two brow'ns
They’d be doing w'ell! Oh, it makes me wild
When I walk around and some shrill-voiced child
Screams out that I and the bears in the pit
Must be very poor ’cause our clothes don’t fit !
That’s the sort of remark that hurts my pride
When I’m walking round at so much per ride !
It’s enough to sour an elephant who »
Carries tons and tons for a loafing Zoo.
Why, Bath, there are times when I feel that I
Could chuck the job. Well, by-and-bye,
I might resign. Of course, Bath, you know
That the only reason why I don’t go
Is that in this dashed White Man’s countree
There isn’t much use for the likes o’ me.
Now if I were back in old Ceylon,
That’s where a good elephant can get on !
I could get some work with a builder, or
I might take a job dragging guns to war.
There, always an elephant built like me
Could be sure of somethin’ ! Par’s I can see
There is nothin’ to do out here but track
Round with bundles of kids upon your back.
But I wouldn’t mind if they wasn’t so
Rude and ungrateful!.. . Well, I must go !
Good-night!. .. Yes, old fellow, it’s me and you
And the old gatepost that keep this Zoo.
N.S.W.
