The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Newton Moore, Treasurer of Westralia, expects
to end this year with a surplus. It is a way Wes-
tralian Treasurers have. They always expect to do
that. But if Newton Moore gets what he expects it will
be the first time a Westralian Treasurer has done it for
sevfen years. The State’s record since Federation got
into its stride has been as follows:
Revenue. Expenditure.
1902 .. £3,354,123 £3,151,427 Surplus £202,096
1903 .. 3,630,238 3,521,763 * „ 108,475
1904 .. 3,550,016 3,698,312 Deficit 148,296
1905 ~. 3,615,340 3,745,224 „ 129,884
1906 .. 3,558,939 3,ft32,318 „ 73,379
1907 .. 3,401,354 3,490,182 „ 88,828
1908 .. 3,376,641 3,379,006 „ 2,365
1909 .. 3,267,014 3,368,551 „ 101,537
For the current year the Treasurer, who has only lately
taken over the job, figures on a revenue of £3,436,118 and
an expenditure of £3,386,624, which would give a surplus
of £49,494. His predecessor was nearly as cheerful in
his estimates a year ago ; he counted no iess than 123,565
more golden chickens than he hatched. Newton Moore
expects to produce his surplus through increasing his
revenue by £169,104 while increasing his expenditure by
only £18,073. As £54,546 of the increased revenue is to
come from railways at an additional cost of only £14,415,
and as railway revenue is a very ticklish item to forecast,
this year’s surplus doesn’t look by any means too
safe. From dividend duty he also expects an increase
of 30 per cent. ; and that isn’t assured either.
Westralia has, in fact, got into the habit of
over-estimating its income ; and thus successive Trea-
surers have dodged the unpleasant task of imposing new
taxation. All that Newton Moore threatens in that way
is a trifling increase in the probate duties, by which he
expects to get an extra £19,868.
Still Newton Moore is entitled to credit for
tackling the outgoings, and cutting down some of the
shameful waste. True, the total expenditure will
be, he estimates, £18,073 more than it was last
year; but all of that and £36,962 besides is
due to increased expenditure under special Acts
—which mostly means interest on last year’s heavy
borrowings. Tne estimates proper have been cut down
from £2,337,261 to £2,300,299, in spite of an increase in
the Education vote and a few others. In Westralia’s
champagne days the public service got into a bad way.
Money was so plentiful that it didn’t seem necessary to
inquire whether, if a friend wanted a billet, there was
anything for him to do, or if he could do it, anyway.
Further, there wds such a rush of work in some depart-
ments that anybody who could sit on a stool and hold a
pen was put on. The champagne has now turned to
plain beer, and things in the service have been more or
less systematised; so a cleaning up became necessary.
Whether the right men are being swept out can’t be toid
at this distance ; that and the rest of the shaking-up pro-
cess is a detail which must be left to the heads. But if
once again the promise of a surplus isn't realised, the
broom and the pruning-hook will have to be got out once
more ; and taxation will have to be laid on with a trowel,
instead of with an afternoon-tea spoon.
And not only taxation either. In fact, one of
Westralia’s worst failings has been its neglect to charge
a lequately for services. It has been bled all round and
all tne time—from the days of the Midland Railway con-
cession onwards. Considering the millions’ worth of
gold which is being hustled out, the business depart-
ments—railways, waterworks, etc.—ought to have been
giving a big profit. This gold-getting boom can’t
last, and the country should have made hay
while it did last; yet in 1908-9 what are called
the “trading departments” showed a loss of 10s. 6d.
per head and the “public services” 7s. Id. per head
of the population—a ridiculous result for such a State to
show. A good deal of the trouble has arisen from the
fact that the biggest industries in the country were in
the hands of people who didn’t care tuppence about
Westralia. Their one object has been to take out the
kernel and leave Westralia the shell ; and the Govern-
ments have been too much afraid of the bogey of driving
capital out of the country to stand up against them.
Local bodies have also been spoon-fed ; and this was to a
great extent part of the same swindle. Newton Moore
undertakes to do a trifle towards stopping these scandals.
The charges for some services have been increased, and
the municipal subsidy—to the horror of Propputy—is to
be kept down to ss. in the £.
The net loan indebtedness —that is, debt less
sinking fund—was £19,718,368 on June 30. Of the total
£1,464,535 was borrowed last year. Details under this
bead are not yet available. It is characteristic of
Westralian State finance that the Loan expenditure is
scarcely ever thought worth more than a mere men-
tion. Dozens of items have been charged to loans
which ought to have been charged to revenue,
and nothing has been said about it. The result
has been that the deficits have been represented to be
smaller than they would have been if the charges had
been made properly. “Repairs to a glass-house for
breeding fruit-pest parasites” is one item that has been
laughed at already. Of the £18,469,989 borrowed to June
•30, 1908, only £10,254,315 was. put into railways and
tramways; the balance had gone mostly into things
which yield no direct revenue, and very largely into
things which Cohen’s money never should have paid
for. And until Westralia keeps a much closer watch
on its Loan expenditure this sort of foil}’ will
no doubt continue, with the result that later on, when
the big foreign capitalists have got the kernel, they will
qqit the State, and leave the farmers and the small pro-
ducers generally to hump the burden of interest, and in
the end to face the bill for the principal.

There was a time when the idea was carefully cultivated
that it really wasn’t safe to trust, the State with any
money—some politician would be sure to get hold of it
and either put it into his own pocket at once or go
through the formality of bribing or rewarding some con-
stituent with it. It must be hard work to get anybody
to swallow that yarn now. Besides, Victoria has had
some very bitter experience in the matter of trusting its
all in the other sort of hank. And, if it conies to that,
this bank has had bitter experience too. Even now it
holds some old Private Enterprise bank-wreckage of the
face value of £9739, which it reckons to be worth, with
accrued interest, only £5408.
The total funds, including the reserves, at the
disposal of the Commissioners on June 30 were
£14,515,898. The principal assets at that date were :

The balance is mostly till money, and Board of Works
and other debentures. Foreclosed properties represent
£109,140 and premises £140,000. The latter have been
steadily written down out of profits ; and, besides, a
fairly handsome reserve fund nas been accumulated.
Only something very grave in the way of disaster could
make such a wreck of the securities as to wipe out these
reserves ; for the whole credit of the State is at the back
of nearly two-thirds of the securities.

This State land hank was star ted in 1896 to help the
farmer, and was put under the general management of
the Savings Bank, of which it forms a department. The
funds were originally supplied by issuing £25 debentures,
repayable by lot as the farmer paid back his instalments.
Later on the bank was authorised to issue £lOO
debentures repayable at fixed dates. The term is usually
from 10 to 12 years ; but if money is dear at the moment
when the debentures are issued, the term is shortened.
Since 1991 all the debentures issued have carried interest
at the rate of 3£ per cent. ; but they have never realised
par. The discounts total £36,868; that is to say, the
bank, in consideration of the low rate of interest, under-
takes to pay back £36,56S of principal more than it has
received. Part of this amount is very rightly written off
out of profits each year. In the foregoing table the total
amount outstanding, and not represented by assets, is
deducted from the reserves shown in the balance-sheet:—
Reserves £75,987
Less Discounts (net debt not repre-
sented by assets) 19,609
True reserves £56,378
During the past year £300,000 was raised, nominally at
3J per cent. ; but in one case the minimum price was
97, and in another 96, which meant an actual cost in
interest of £3 16s. 4d. per cent, in one case and £3 18s. 2d.
per cent, in the other. When the public does not apply
for the whole of an issue of debentures offered by the
Commissioners, the latter take up the remnant with
Savings Bank funds. Of the total outstanding on June3o
the public held £658,675 and the commissioners £921,175.
The interest payable by the farmer has for the
most part been 4£per cent. In the droughty timeof 1903-4,
how’ever, the department itself had to pay as much as
£4 2s. Id. per cent, for money; and as expenses can’t be
kept under half-a-sovereign per cent., it became neces-
sary to put up the price for new loans to 4f per cent.
But that only lasted till money got a bit more plentiful,
andthe.priee is back again to 4£ per cent. Tbe next
table shows, the total of advances outstanding and some
other points:—

The gradual reduction of advances in 1906-7-8 isn’t any-
thing to grieve about. No doubt in some cases it meant
that the private Cohen, being for the moment very flush,
was cutting in at a lower rate than the bank’s steady 4J
or 4£; but it also meant that times were good for the
farmer too, and he was paying off debt instead of loading
himself up with more. Still the farmer can make up his
mind to this : if he clears off to the private Cohen when
money is cheap, and sneaks back to the public Cohen
when it is dear, the public Cohen will never be
the success he ought to be. When you come to think
of it, £1,312,204 is a very poor proportion of the
total amount advanced to Victorian farmers. If the
bank had three times the business, it ought to be able to
cut down its expenses by at least 4s. per cent. ; and,
under ordinary circumstances, that would allow the
hank to lend at a steady J per cent. less. The farmer has
learned a good deal about some aspects of co-operation.
He hasn’t yet learnedmuch about co-operative borrowing,
though.
The bank is accumulating a sizeable reserve
fund ; and as its own interest earnings have to be added
to it, it ought to go on growing faster and faster every
year, provided, of course, that nothing goes wrong.
Nothing has gone wrong lately, and there are no fore-
closures on hand, and practically no arrears of either
interest or instalments. But the top of the hill will be
reached sometime, and then securities will be thrown on
to the bank’s hands. Certainly this bank is very well
situated : the regular repayments of principal make it
unlikely that any but the newest securities will be
thrown at it when the slump comes. Consequently
though the reserves are undernper cent, of the advances,
they are really much less insignificant than they look.

The paid capital of this co. is £25,000 in 200,000 shares of
2s. 6d., with a liability of £4 17s. 6d. The reserves have
now therefore passed the paid capital. Of the share-
holders’ funds £20,000 is on deposit with the Govern-
ment, and £30,716 is out on mortgage ; and between these
and anything that the co. may have in the way of an
inner reserve, consisting of reserved commissions, it is
very likely that the interest earnings alone are now
almost enough to pay the dividend. This'would leave
nearly the whole of the commission earnings, less work-
ing expenses, to go to the reserves. The growth of the
reserves looks rapid ; and by this time, if it had been some
other sort of co., the directors would no doubt have trans-
ferred some of them to capital. But, as a matter of fact,
the reserves haven’t grown as fast as the risks, judging
the risks by the total value of trust estates in hand. For
instance: —
Trust Estate Total Proportion of
Balances. Funds. Funds to Trusts.
1899 .. £1,230,958 £28,808 £2/6/- p.c.
1909 3,075,817 51,038 1/13/- „
Down to the present the risks don’t appear to have been
very serious ; but a trustee co. is much more likely to be
hit when property values are running down-hill than
when they are climbing up, as they have been lately. It
is when beneficiaries don’t get as much as they had ex-
pected that they look around for somebody to blame ;
and very rightly they will not hesitate to try to make the
professional executor pay.if they don’t think the work
has been done properly. And when a trustee co. is hit it
isn’t usually for tuppence or anything small like
that. The large additions to reserves may therefore
be regarded in part as premiums paid into
an insurance fund; and whether they will be
called for later on nobody can yet say. Assuming
for the present that all the profits are true profits, the
earnings in the past year were at the very fine rate of
£ll 18s. lOd. per cent. This co. follows the safe and
prudent practice of not crediting itself with corpus com-
missions until they have been actually received. All
trustee cos. eharge so much (generally 2J per cent.) per
annum on the income of an estaie, and so much (gener-
ally 2£ per cent.) on the capital value—the latter payable
only once, of course. Some take credit in their earnings
for the latter 2J per cent, in a lump sum ; this co.
apparently spreads its revenue from this source over the
whole period that the estate takes to realise and wind up.

however, only £1 per share, or £37,500 in all, is paid up.
With ordinary luck, the reserves should therefore be

the difference in the look of things on September 80.

Early in 1908 the Wunderlich brothers, after building up
a reputation of which anybody might be proud, turned
their concern over to a new company, partly with the
object of taking in the metal and tile department of
W. H. Rocke and Co.’s business in Victoria. At the outset
the shares were held in a few hands, and the Sydney Stock
Exchange, regarding the concern as a private co., did not
list the stock. In view of that, the first two balance-
sheets did not disclose the goodwill item, which was
lumped with freeholds and leaseholds. Later, however,
the stock was listed ; and the goodwill item, which had
been increased meantime by the purchase of businesses
in Queensland and Westralia, stands in the last balance-
sheet at £64,289. In the foregoing table the reserves are
described as nominal; in the light of the information now
given there is, of course, an actual deficit of £53,083, re-
presenting the amount of the paper asset less reserves.
The present paid capital is £150,011. Of this £27,500
is 7 per cent, cumulative pref. shares ; on these
the dividend is limited to 7 per cent. The balance
(£122,511) is in ordinary shares. The directors
are entitled to issue another 15,000 pref. shares. Unless
the div. is six months in arrear, pref. shares carry no
right to vote at shareholders’ meetings. At present, the
pref. shares call for £1925 a year ; if the full 42,500 were
issued, the div. would be £2975, It isn’t very much for a
co. of this size to pledge itself to carry. Further, as the
pref. capital is preferential as to capital as well as to
dividend, the security for this class of share is just about
as silt-edged as it is possible for an industrial security
to be.
Then there are the ordinary shares. These,
with certain reservations, are entitled to the balance
of the profits. The reservations are, however,
rather important. The three Wunderlich brothers
are permanent directors, to whom, for their services, a
total of £3OOO a year is payable, plus 5 per cent, of the
total half-yearly net profits, provided that enough is left
to pay the pref. div., and 6 per cent, to ordinaries. Also
they are entitled to anything up to a further 5 per cent,
on the net profits in excess of £lO,OOO per annum, sub-
ject to the ordinaries getting three times as much, over
and above the 6 per cent. For the past year the net
profits were £20,500. The amount the ordinaries received
in excess of 6 per cent, was£239s; so that, in addition to
the first 5 per cent., the managihg or permanent directors
would be entitled to an amount-equal to one-third of that,
under certain circumstances. Being limited, however, to
5 p.c. on the profit in excess of £lO,OOO, they could last
year draw only £525, in addition to the first 5 per cent.
( = £1025). The total directors’ commission was therefore
£1550, which would be equal to a 5 per cent. div. on a
pref. issue of £31,000. In the accounts the profits are
shown net, after the deduction of the commission to the
managing directors. Out of the profits provision has also
been made for depreciation, amortisation of leaseholds,
etc., and an employees’ bonus and pension fund. The
amount of these provisions is not disclosed.
The goodwill is a very heavy item. At the
outset of the co. it represented nearly half the paid
capital. The original issue of capital was £144,500, plus
seven shares no doubt taken up for registration purposes.
This capital was apparently made up and distributed as
follows :
Wunderlich assets .. .. •• £66,000
~ goodwill .. •• 38,500
Rocke and Co. assets.. .. •• 17,000
~ goodwill .. .. 20,000
Subscribed by Bocke and Co. .. 3,000
Total £144,500
Bocke and Co. (which is W. L. Baillien and others) held
the “ B” shares in the old Wunderlich Co. ; it was in
return for this stock that the 27,500 pref. shares in the
new co. were issued. Also the same people are entitled,
and may be called on, to subscribe at par for the 15,000
pref. shares previously referred to. This is open until
June next vear.

No Melbourne building sooiety came out of the boom
troubles of the early ’nineties better than this one. All
the way through the management had held the opinion
that the law which allowed societies to take from deposi-
tors three times as much as they had got from share-
holders was dangerous. Having restricted its own bor-
rowings, it was therefore able to pay all deposits as they
fell due. The only trouble was with the investing shares :
an arrangement had to be made with regard to them. In
1893 the reserves were £21,269. The whole of that was
passed to a contingency fund, to meet probable losses on
securities ; and, to further help the society out, dividends
had already been dropped, and they were not re-
sumed till 1899. That contingency fund, to which
additions were subsequently made, has now got
down to £7138. In the table on top of
these remarks this fund is included with the reserves;
hence they are spoken of as “ nominal.” Whether the
£7138 is enough to square things up can’t be told. The
total foreclosures amount now to only £28,084 ; and they
must be a poor lot of remnants if they won’t fetch
£20,946. Down to 1902 the society lumped its assets ;
since that date this has been the movement: —
Deposits, Fore- Other
. etc. Advances, closures. Assets.
1902 .. £12,616 £51,854 £68,857 £lBOB
1905 .. 12,229 59,172 64,245 3678
1906 .. 12,545 67,007 48,189 964
1907 .. 16,399 73,473 44,105 1087
1908 .. 19,294 81,587 36,797 859
1909 ~. 18,057 86,209 28,084 1814
In 1902 the contingency fund was £24,979. It is now
£17,841 less, and meantime the book value of the fore-
closures has been reduced by £40,773. But there is no
way of telling whether it has taken £17,841 to make
good the losses on the sale of £40,773 (book value) of
assets, or whether other assets have been foreclosed
during the period; in the latter case, of course,
the loss has been proportionately less. In either case,
however, it isn’t a cheerful record. The advances —
which may be put down generally as new business—are
highly profitable ; you can’t, however, tell from a
balance-sheet such as this whether the loans are made on
a safe valuation or not. It is a pity that the old fore-
closures are not kept separate from new ones, if there are
any new ones. And if there are any new ones the losses
on them shouldn’t be charged against the old contingency
fund : to do that simply swells current profits by relieving
them of a charge which they ought to carry. The present
capital is £64,270 in permanent shares and £9439 in in-
vesting and paid-up shares ; and in proportion to this the
liabilities are very small indeed, and the risks are corres-
pondingly moderate.

This is the old Federal, passed through the fire of purifi-
cation, and cleansed. The unregenerate Federal had a
great spree during the Melbourne boom-time, and when
it woke up in the morning it found that it owed £389,939,
and had on hand a large assortment of very dubious
assets. So it swore off for 12 years, ant) lived on porridge
and hopes ; and in 1904 it decided that the time was ripe
for a change. A new society was therefore formed with
a capital of £96,351; and, in view of the re-valuation of
assets then on hand, the new- concern started with re-
serves of £26,488 indicated above. Since then a good deal
has been done in the way of getting rid of the remnant of
the old properties, and rapid progress has been made in
regard to new business. This shows how :
1904. 1907. 1908. 1909.
Deposits, etc. .. £18,691 £25,048 £32,651
Advances .. ..£63,160 124,510 136,217 143,452
Properties in pos-
session.. .. 54,071 26,252 , 20,124 18,813
Cash balances .. 4,779 2,548
For the first few years the properties disposed of showed
a surplus over the new valuations ; but in the accounts
for the past year no surplus is mentioned. Three things
may have happened : (1) there may have been neither
profit nor loss ; (2) there may have been a profit, but it
may have been used as an internal reserve to
write down assets ; or (3) there may have been a loss,
which has been set off in some way against the year’s
revenue. The last seems from the accounts to be the
least likely. No trouble has apparently been met with in
letting out the money derived from sales ; in fact, the
new advances have called also for an increase of deposits.
The latter are still, however, very moderate in comparison
with the capital and reserves. The advances give a very
profitable return, the interest earnings being £7 12s. per
cent, gross on the average sum outstanding. Whether
the society has lent too much on the securities can’t, of
course, be told while things are on the up-grade ; it takes
a slump to discover that weak spot. Bet as the reserves
alone are equal to 20 per cent, of all the non-liquid and
shrinkable assets, not only the borrowed money, but the
capital also, is so far well covered against anything in the
shape of an ordinary catastrophe.
The Australian Alliance, which an English co.
is to take over, has a life department, though no new
business has been done for many years past. It is said
that;, though complete arrangements have not been
made, the National Mutual will take over this part of the
concern.

Victoria still is easily first in the matter of total produc-
tion, being about £100,000,000 to the good of N. S. Wales.

W * * *
Last week in both the Federal and the N.S.W.
State House members showed that they were getting the
knife ready to rip open the “horrible understanding” in
the Inter-State coal and shipping business. Neither
Government will vet commit itself to any thing definite,
though the Commonwealth Attorney-General says he is
probing the matter to see if it’s all in accordance with the
Anti-Trust Act. The most serious aspect of the case yet
put forward is that coal is being sold for foreign use for
about Bs., while for use in Australia’s own factories the
price is 11s. ; and if that is the result of an “ understand-
ing” no Protectionist Government, at any rate, can put
up with it for long. #
That the Broken Hill South Blocks is likely to
get a good western ore body at depth has been empha-
sised of late bv the crosscutting done in the hanging-wall
side of the lode at 526 ft. Strongly mineralised ground
has been carried down on the west side of the present
lode, beginning at the 200 ft. level, and the deeper the
sinking the better the values. The theory 1 is that this
western body—if there is one—is the continuation of the
big South mine lode, which at the 970 ft. level is now out
near the boundary of the South Extended, on the
western side of the South mine. If this is so, and the
ore shoots continue in the South Blocks pro-
pertv, the underlay will carry the ore at a
depth of 1000 ft. or so under the Zinc Corporation’s
plant, which is on the western side of the South Blocks
hill. Color is given to this theory by a story told of an
old-time manager of the South Extended. He was sink-
ing, a shaft due west from the present new shaft of the
South Extended, and at a depth of a few hundred feet he
cut sulphides in the bottom. That stuff was no good in
those days, and would only have blown out the show 1 ; so
the manager threw back the dirt and blotted out the
traces of his guilt. This shaft is on the supposed contin-
uation of the main South lode, and is no doubt connected
with the ore passed through in sinking the present South
Extended shaft.
« * • *
Long ago The Bulletin pointed out how poor
was the encouragement for throwing money down a shaft
on the eastern ridge of the White Leads flat, Broken Hill.
The only encouragement that could be got by anyone in-
clined to test the eastern ridge was the long line of
abandoned mines which stud the plain on the western
ridge, and that was a very negative sort of encourage-
ment. In spite of all, the Silver City Blocks has,
nevertheless, plugged along for several months
past. The other day there was a great Stir in the
shareholders’ camp, all because of a little lode
material that had been cut in the bottom of the 85ft.
shaft. The daily papers cackled over this joyfully. A
picked sample was assayed, and it turned out to be all zinc,
without a trace of lead or silver !
• * * *
Broken Hill Junction is swapping horses while
crossing the stream. But the responsible people would
probably say that there are other streams still to cross,
and that those who have been in charge of affairs haven’t
proved much good as horse-marines. In opposition to
the two retiring directors, the Broken Hill contingent
put up two shareholders, who won on a ballot. There-
upon the rest of the Board resigned, along with the
secretary. As the mine has not paid a dividend since
1897, and now wants £30,000 or so of new capital for new
plant and development work, it seems a fair thing to give
somebody else a show. The old hands, anyhow, can’t
complain that they have been rushed off the premises
before they had had time to show what they vvere made of.
Wyalong (N.S.W.) isn’t feeling well over the
collapse of its pet property, the True Blue, which only
12 months ago employed 200 miners. Recently several
thousands had been spent in poppet heads andmachinerv
at the Brilliant shaft. For 10 years the mine returned
handsome dividends, but a break in the shoot of mineral
ore, and the refusal of the majority of shareholders to
finance the co. over what the manager considered was
only a temporary difficulty, brought the end within
sio-'ut. The whole concern, including the battery, went
to"the Junction Co. for £lO,OOO. The Brilliant shaft is
down 1400 ft. on stone, but for the present at least the
new owners don’t intend to continue sinking.
Kyloe (N.S.W.) Copper Co. wasn’t much good
at guessing when its new mill would be able to make a
start. First it said the end of August; but it will be well
into November before the wheels go round. Still, even
then, unless many alterations and adjustments are found
necessary, the Christmas dividend may arrive. After
a rather "depressing time the workings underground have
within the last fortnight told a very cheerful tale. At
the bottom (400 ft.) level there is a drive out north and
south; the ends are about 400 ft. apart, and both
wings are in good ore. One has from ift. to Bft. of it,
of which nearly sft. is worth about 20 per cent. ; the other
is sft. wide, mostly very good milling ore, but carrying a
vein of rich stuff. It has been looking lately as though
the lens which carries rich ore was narrower at the 400 ft.
than at the level above, but the recent developments are
putting a comfortable end to that theory.
Kangiara (N.S.W.) has joined the crowd of
those that have rushed into the lead-smelting business
and have arrived at the usual conclusion—that unless
you have a very big mine, capable of supplying a perfect
furnace mixture, it is more profitable to let someone else
recover the values. The Kangiara’s lead bullion and
copper matte have not yet been disposed of, so final
returns are not known. As, however, a large pile of ore
from which great wealth was expected has been carefully
picked over, and the plums subjected to a erhde and ex-
pensive smelting treatment, there is little chance of the
anticipated revenue being recovered. Rumors of a
larger plant have not been so popular lately ; and
if is guided by the results achieved during
the recent campaign, the management will probably
think matters over a long while before doing
any more smelting. The statement that the plant
has only been experimental is doubtless true, as far as re-
sults go ; but this view was adopted only when the final
result became obvious. Before the campaign commenced
the objective was to smelt the whole of the oxidised ore
with a proportion of raw sulphides. This scheme was
abandoned as soon as a metallurgist arrived on the scene.
Oxidised ore was discarded, and only those sulphides which
passed a careful hand-sorting test were sent to the roast
heaps. Roasting, likewise hand-sorting and elimination of
silica and zinc, were not given a place in the original pro-
gramme. The co. is now erecting a concentrating mill.
This plant consists of a small rock breaker, one pair fixed
rolls, May jig, stampers and tables. Some of it is new ;
some of it scraps. This plant promises to later on join
the smelter ; thep no doubt it will also be spoken of as
“experimental.” The usual difficulty experienced with
concentrators is loss in fines, more particularly when
working on friable sulphides in a hard, silicious gangue,
as at Kangiara. Mutilating these sulphides by means of
stampers has long since been abandoned by most metal
mines. The trouble is that, though you certainly reduce
the bulk, you don’t improve the original grade by much.
Attention is for the moment being concentrated
on the hand-picking and selling of the selected ore. This
promises to be the operation which must furnish all profits.
Its disadvantage is that about 50 p.c. of the values are
left from even high-grade ore, and it has yet to
be demonstrated whether these discards and the
lower-grade mine ore can be profitably handled.
Wet weather and bad roads have prevented th#
dispatch of picked ore for some months, but teams are
now being driven furiously between the mine and the
railway. Besides the accumulated picked ore, the dumps
are being turned over on the surface, so shipments
may be fairly large for some time. The dividend pro-
mised next month depends on the quantity of shipping
ore which can be rushed through from the accumulated
ore, the stopes and the dumps. The main shaft is down
200 ft., and after sinking a well a start will be made to
crosscut to the lode. No. 2 level needs to come up to the
most optimistic prophecy to prevent the co. striking
trouble. Bv the time this level is driven, all the plums
will have been removed from No. 1, and the dumps
picked over. Low-grade mixed ores will then be left with-
out a plant to handle them. A continuance of the shoot of
rich copper-gold ore met with over No. 1 north drive is
badly needed to postpone the troublesome problem of
plant.
At Kangiara Blocks the surface is being vio-
lently disturbed in search of an ore body. Costeens and
pot-holes, dug on the slightest provocation, give the
lease the appearance of an alluvial rush. These, in
addition to a couple of shafts and a few crosscuts, have,
so far, not disclosed anything worth looking for.
* * ■* •
Great Cobar (N.S.W.) has blown in No. 3
furnace, which is probably part of a policy of impressing
the market. The last return was 515 tons copper, 13440 z.
gold, and 77540 z. silver. But still not a word is said
about the tonnage or the purchased ore. Last year’s
report provided such dismal reading that, unless costs
have been reduced or the extraction of copper per ton of
ore has increased, there is no cause to rejoice over an in-
creased output. It is never safe to assume that because
a mine is producing heavily it is making good profits ;
Mungana (Chillagoe) is a case in point. Great Oobar’s
best hope is a reduction of working costs to something
like 20s. per ton ; if it can do that, an extraction of 2 p.c.
copper and ss. worth of gold and silver per ton of ore
would allow a reasonable profit, with copper at £6O per
ton. But its capitalisation is far too heavy, and its
finances are in a bad way.
Ooolgardie Redemption (W. A.), after a shame-
less existence, has come to an ignoble end. Several
distress warrants for unpaid wages have been returned
marked “No effects,” and as the State holds a first mort-
gage for £lOOO, and as someone else has a second mort-
gage for £2OOO, the above endorsement is easily explained.
Meanwhile the lawyers are “ acting ” for unsecured credi-
tors, and various officials, not liking the look of things,
have “ chucked ” their jobs. Indeed, were it not that
shareholders’ interests have been so neglected and that a
number of workers have lost their wages, the spectacle of
a crowd of people trying to levy distress on such a
notorious bag of tricks as the Redemption would be
ludicrous.

Golden Links (W.A.) has just added 12 acres to
its holding by the purchase of the Golden Eaglelease. It
appears that the drill in its wanderings proved the Link
shoot to extend into the latter property, with the result
that the owners were asked to name a price.

For the half-year to the end of July Golden
Pole’s receipts (principally tributors’ bullion) amounted
to £18,604 ; and but for writing off the whole of the ex-
penses in connection with the abandoned Kohinoor South
option (£7389), a working profit of £Bl6 would have been
shown. All the tribute agreements except one expired
on August 31 ; and as there was not sufficient ore devel-
oped to keep the mill running full time, the Board
decided not to renew the agreements for the present.
The mine has been thoroughly l sampled, and on the
advice of the manager the co. has decided to do a little
development work on its own ; and while doing so the
payable ore still available, amounting to between 2000
and 3000 tons, will be stoped out and treated. The co. ■
now l has about £6657 in hand, and the profit derived from
these reserves is expected to sw'ell this sum a little.

According to the general manager’s estimate,
Ivanhoe (W.A.) had 963,797 tons of 48/3 ore in sight at
June 30, as compared with 934,098 tons of 49/8 ore at the
end of the previous half-year. After checking measure-
ments and taking samples throughout the mine, the con-
sulting engineers (8., M. and Co.) reckon the total
tonnage at 1,125,753 tons, worth 47/10 per ton, as against
1,112,502 tons of 48/11 ore at Dec. 31 last.
Great Fingall (W.A.) is still deep-sinking. An
inclined winze below No. 13 level has followed down the
mostly unpayable but strong ore body for 700 ft.., and it is
intended to go to 1000 ft. before taking a spell. Some
diamond drilling is being done north of the main fault at
No. 13 level to see if the shoot of gold continues north of
the disturbed section, but so far nothing of value has
been struck. The vacuum slimes plant recently installed
on the property continues to do satisfactory work, and is
now 1 treating 25 per cent, more stuff than was originally
estimated.
* * * *
Whim Well’s sins are finding it out. In his
preliminary report on West Pilbarra (W.A.), Assistant-
Geologist Woodward condemns the co. for “discontinu-
ing development and concentrating its entire energy on
scratching ore from the old upper workings upon a
s.vstem which would be expected of a party l of tributors
who have no interest in the future of the mine.” In
another part of his indictment Woodward says it is quite
distressing to see such a fine body of ore worked in this
way, as it not only l means the discarding of all ore (about
three tons out of every four) that will not assay 16 per
cent, in order to make a profit, but the output r-presents
only l about one-tenth of what could be raised if there were
smelters on the spot, andif a 5 per cent, ore could thus be
made to pay. So far the lode has proved to be the largest
and richest oxidised body yet discovered in the West,
and, judging by its promising appearance at water
level, Wpodward thinks that prospects at depth are
encouraging, but still problematic until development has
been done. In the meantime all the cream ore that the
co. can lay its hands on is being shipped to the smelters,
and only the other day a dividend was paid so as to make
things look more attractive still. In other words, the
real and permanent interests of the mine are being
ignored, and when the rich stuff gives out the market
will collapse like a pricked bladder.
Associated Northern (W. A.) has taken an option
on the Forest Belle, at Mt. Ida. There is an Bft. reef in
the property 1 , and as it has crushed well and looks, well
as far as opened up, the chances of a deal being effected
seem rather hopeful.

For the half-year ended July 31 Gwalia South
(W.A.) crushed 11,599 tons, and also treated a miscel-
laneous lot of residues; for a return of £28,450. Working
expenses mopped up £14,287 ; and after deducting £8849
for depreciation and development, a surplus of £5313
remained. The 2s. dividend paid in June absorbed £6OOO,
so that, as The Bulletin remarked some time back, the
co. has, in order to keep up appearances, been living be-
.vond its means. Of course, now that the price of shares
has come down a bit, shareholders are gravely informed
that, in view of diminished profits, further distributions
are “ off ” for the present; but this is poor consolation
for those who got in when regular dividends were being
talked of and the market was at its top. Luckily, the
co. still has£s6oo in hand, and so as to make a splash in
development, and to prospect for the continuation of
some promising shoots already proved in the adjoining
Sons of Gwalia, shareholders are to be asked at the meet-
ing to empower the Board to increase the capital from
£30,000 to £50,000 by the issue of 40,000 new 10s. shares,
and to dispose of the lot at not less than £1 each. It is
estimated that recent developments on the western lode
have added 20,000 tons of 355. ore to the ore reserves in
this mine.
* * * *
Rutherglen (Vic.) is in a poor plight. Ask the
mine director why, and he swears that it is because so
much of the miners’ time is taken up in travelling be-
tween the shaft’s mouth and the working face. The
managers say ditto, because they can tell which side their
bread’s buttered on. But the men know that the wash
is poor, the ground heavy, the distance from the shaft
great, and the ventilation often dreadful. The old idea
was to work from a central shaft in a big alluvial claim ;
but that plan will have to give way to working
from two or more shafts. The Great Southern
has gone slow on divs. Some say the narrow gutter
has scoured the gold out. The truth is that the bottom
is uneven and the wash irregular. Great Southern Consols
doesn’t seem to approach the dividend stage now any
more rapidly than in the days of yore. More profits are
probably being picked up by some people by watching
the Cathcart market than by watching the Consols.,
Then Southern and Prentice are going the way of all
Bewiek-Moreing’s deep-lead shows. The firm doesn’t
seem able to get low costs, and high costs won’t answer
with poor ground. Rutherglen, therefore, looks like
providing a crop of tombstones unless some new tribu-
taries come along to sweeten up the wash of the big
mines.
If the gods are still at Walhalla, they are not
getting fat out of the Long Tunnel in these days. The
grade of the ore in the mine has at times lately got down to
under 7dwt.; and the gold is worth only about £3 12s. 6d.
per ounce. Fortunately the Board had the strength of
will, not often found in Victorian directors, to build up a
reserve, and they clung to it as tight as a limpet to a
rock. Even now, when the battery is up, the power-
station completed and the winding-engine at wprk,
there is still a bit of oof left over for emergencies.
What is wanted is that there shall be less scraping-up
of stone. Walhalla folk say it is the contract system
that’s wrong. The fact is there ought to be more'faces
on stone, more stopes opened up, more progressive work,
and more stuff going through the battery. With the new
plant this should be possible. Anderson has-his chance,
and if he gets stone he will most likely put things right.
Walhalla people believe in him, and in Finlayson, even if
they haven’t got much faith in the Melbourne crowd.
London came into the market, as it was ex-
pected it would; so shares in New Brilliant Freehold,
Charters Towers, have taken a long jump. No. 2 east
level, on the main footwall formation, has been driven
621 ft., and driving is also going on along what the Free-
hold supposes to be its top boundary, adjoining the
East Mexican ; but most of the stone on the footwall side
is being left intact, pending a proper definition of the
boundary l . Rather a good little reef branched off on the
hanging-wall side from No. 1 east, and the face at 77ft.
from the junction with the main level shows Ift. of heavy l
mineral stone. Then there are Nos. 6 and 7 on the
hanging-wall reef, which retain the same numbers as in
Mills’ United. An average of £5 6s. 3d. from 422
tons gives a fair idea of the quality of the
stopes over the two footwalls and the two
hanging-wall levels that are being worked. But the
diminishing size and erratic values of the reefs as they go
eastwards makes any comparison with Mills’ United im-
possible. The reef recently cut in the N. B.F. shaft shows
how topsy-turvy things are in the neighborhood, for it
faulted in the middle of the shaft, and there is now only
lft. of stone showing on the footwall side instead of 3ft.
No. 2 level (from the shaft side) is now being driven east
and west; the eastern face is of interest to the Brilliant
Blocks, whose boundary is less than 170 ft. away.
* * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, has given up
prospecting the 830 ft. level, not having been able to get a
payable show. It will now confine its attention for a
while to the Day Dawn reef on the New Brilliant Freehold
boundary l . This boundary l level is a continuation of No. 6
from Mills’ United ; small crushings taken from the reef
in extending the level for 100 ft. into Mexican ground
were worth over £ll per ton. The boundary isn’t yet
defined exactly, and the hope of getting a larger slice of
the good stone has put Mexican shares up a bit, though
the mine mainly depends on an extension of the payable
shoot for some distance over No. 6 level. The develop-
ments in No. 5 and the crosscuts into the footwall gave
very little encouragement; y r et, with good stone so close,
it is quite possible for things to improve further along the
Freehold boundary, which extends for 1200 ft.

Stannary Hills (N.Q.) has hit the Ivanhoe lode
in the bottom level from the new 1 shaft, and for a start
got into 8 per cent, tin stuff. The striking of the
lode here was practically a foregone conclusion ; for the
ground had been proved in the workings from the old
shaft. The Ivanhoe claim has been the co.’s stand-by ;
but to get the best results from the reorganised mill the
co. needs to develop a patch of more cheaply-got ore
handy to one of the other shafts. And there are still
hopes of that.
■ • • *
One Williams, late of S’Africa—which is a pretty
broad address—has been enlarging the hopes of the
Sapphiretown (Q.) gem-seeker. He has discovered the
“ bantam,” or quartzite pebble, which is a camp-mate of
the diamond in S’Africa ; also a species of decomposed
lime common in diamond fields ; finally l , he reckons that
the present bottom, of granite, is but the cloak of dia-
mond strata deeper down. In fact, he has got nearly
everything—except diamonds. Sapphiretown, which has
grown tired of chasing a satisfactory market for its
sapphire gems, wishes him much luck, anyway l .
* m »
The opening of the Pieman track is responsible
for the Stanley Reward Tin Co., of Hobart. The Stanley l
is a small tributary of the Pieman, in very rough country,
and the river flats have been carefully shepherded for
years. The wash is deep, and in places very rich ; but
the width of it is very doubtful. The vendors collar not
only 20,000 paids out of 50,000 shares, but also £lOOO in
cash ; and, in view of the size of their bite, the least they
could have done was to produce some more definite in-
formation about their property, presuming they believe
that it would stand testing. The co. will have to get an
elevator to work the ground.
* * * *
Mt. Lvell (Tas.) consigned 4373 tons copper
for the half-year closing Sept. 30 ; but it didn't produce
that much. Tonnage treated has been considerably reduced,
though a better extraction has partly lessened the gap.
Six months since the management estimated that copper
values had improved from 5'75 percent, to 6 per cent. ;
also ore reserves have very much improved. In the
ordinary course this w'ould be followed by a bigger metal
output; but Mt. Lyell has been satisfied to regulate
its consignments out of its stocks of metal, for the copper
market hasn’t been sufficiently tempting lately to induce
it to rush matters.
* * * »
It is worth while keeping an eye on Tarcoola
Blocks, in the Far North of S. Aus. So far it hasn’t had
success enough to make anybody wealthy, but it has
mostly l earned its own keep. Lately l the co. recon-
structed, and a little more energy has been put into the
prospecting at the deepest levels. The country is partly
slate and partly l quartzite. While they l are in the
quartzite the reefs are rarely worth much ; but when
they get into the black slate good values are carried.
Until quite latelv the bottom workings have been nearly
all in quartzite ;'but within the last few weeks the pro-
spects have considerably improved, and the outlook
is now very healthy.

The erection of the new 300-ton Blast Smelting
Furnace for the Great Fitzroy mine, Q., is now rapidly
approaching completion at the mine. The. dimensions of
this furnace are as follows —168 in. x 44in., and 24ft.
high to feed floor. It is water jacketed from top to
bottom. These jackets are made of steel plates flanged
in one piece. The furnace was designed by the mine
superintendent, and has many features of excellence and
novelty in the detail of its construction. The jackets are
made interchangeable, only one type and size of jacket
being used ; and they will further be constructed so that
thev are reversible from top to bottom—-thus practically
the life of the jackets is doubled. This furnace, as well as
the previous one, was built at the works of the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne.* [2

A SIMPLE REMEDY.
Said to Relieve Rheumatism, and Anyone Can
Prepare It.
Thousands of men and women who have felt the sting
and torture of this dread disease, which is no respecter
of age, person, sex, nation or rank, will be interested to
know that while for many years rheumatism was con-
sidered an incurable disease, now it is one of the easiest
afflictions of the human body to conquer. Medical
science has proved that it is not a disease in itself, but is
a symptom caused by inactive kidneys. Rheumatism is
uric acid in the blood and other waste products of the
system, which should be filtered and strained out in the
form of urine. The function of the kidneys is to sift
these poisons and acids out, and keep the blood clean and
pure. The kidneys, however, are of sponge-like sub-
stance, the holes or pores of which will sometimes, either
from overwork, cold or exposure, become clogged and in-
active, and failing in their function of eliminating these
poisons from the blood, they remain in the veins, decom-
pose, and settling about the joints and muscles cause the
untold pain and suffering of rheumatism and backache, -
often producing complications of bladder and urinary
disease, weakness, etc. .
The following simple prescription is said to relieve the
worst cases of rheumatism, because of its direct action
upon the blood and kidneys, relieving, too the most
severe forms of bladder and urinary troubles: Tincture
Gentian Compound, one ounce; Syrup Rhubarb, one
ounce; Liquid Darkola Compound, one ounce; and
Syrup of Ginger, five ounces. Mix by shaking well in a
bottle, and take one teaspoonful after each meal and
one before retiring for the night.
The ingredients can be had from any good chemist,
and, being purely vegetable, are entirely harmless and
safe to use at any time.*

“ Sandgroper ” : For the Queerest-named
Station Stakes I enter the Shadow of Death, in
this Yalgoo district (W.A.). The station itself is
not a large one, but is more or less famous for the
wayside house on it, which till recently was
known by the same name. The Oldest Inhabitant
says the name was given because the natives aver
that the ghost of a monstrous William goat
appears at certain times on the ridge overlooking
the house.

“Wang”: The gUn-wisdom of “ Monta-
lex ” (B. 16/9/’O9) reminds me of a wonderful fluke
made by a lucky fool with whom I once had to go
shooting. The fool was on a visit to the station,
and one day the boss presented him with a gun
and instructed me to arm myself and take him
out for a day’s rabbiticide. The boss, not being a
wildly generous person, had provided the visitor
with a muzzle-loader. “ Cartridges are too d- d
handy and expensive for a mug,” he explained.
Nevertheless, the fool was in great glee. The
ancient piece of ordnance was quite up-to-date for
all he knew to the contrary, and he energetically
loaded and discharged it for seven solid hours
without even hitting me. Late in the afternoon
we turned homewards unburthened by anything
dead. The fool was loading up for a possible shot
on the way back, when there was a flash and a
roar and the ramrod whizzed skyward past his
nose. He staggered back with a look of horror
and sat down with unnecessary violence amongst
the bracken, just as a fat crow came flapping down
from the blue with the ramrod spitted neatly
through its chest. The fool was highly delighted
and amused, but I extracted the ramrod thought-
fully, and offered to carry his gun home for him.
I had a dim sort of idea that if the crow hadn’t
been around the ramrod might have been my
share. It was a more or less absurd idea, but I
had it. I scratched an impromptu grave for the
bird. It seemed hard for a wise bird to be killed
by an ass.

“Phil Hamm ” : He the wheelbarrow as
a means of shifting Matilda and other worldly
goods (B. 16/9/’O9). I remember an
itinerant temperance apostle who used
to tour the Grenfell and other districts
of N.S.W. in the early ’eighties. He
always pushed his little lot before him
in a barrow. Besides blankets and
water-proofs, the little lot mostly con-
sisted of tracts which alleged that the
road to Hell was paved with empty
beer bottles. But in these degenerate
days it is found that it makes the back
ache less to push a pram. Last year I
struck a traveller near Parkes whose
perambulator contained a decent-sized
swag, small tent, kit of tools, and odds
and ends. For comfort he reckoned the
ordinary fashion of “waltzing Matilda”
wasn’t in it alongside his method.
“ I tan ”: Dr. Ilayner, of Auckland (M.L.),
comes back from a visit to Uncle Sam with a
statement that he has secured the rights to the
Burbank thornless cactus, and that the Australian
claims to the vegetable have been bought in Sydney
for a fabulous sum. If the Dominion does not
know anything about the pear cactus, Australia
does, seeing that it has been robbed of
thousands of good acres by the curse. If
the Burbank variety is thornless, and will
continue thornless, all right. But it has
been stated publicly in America that the
Burbank freak creation, when planted out, de-
generates, and speedily becomes the common old
spiny curse, the same as its forefathers.
It is like the rare six-legged cat whose
descendants won’t remain six-legged. Fur-
ther, even if the Burbank pear is a successful
fodder in Yankee land, it does not prove that it
will be so in Australia; for the common cactus
kind is produced in Texas for its fodder value,
whereas in Australia the same article goes off
tremendously in nutritious constituents, probably
on account of its wilder growth. Anyway, the
pricky pear that we know is such a colossal curse
that the Australian and, Dominion Governments
should sit on their doorsteps and watch that this
alleged new thing does not get entry except under
the strictest supervision ; or if it is proved that it
does degenerate in America, then it should be re-
fused admittance no matter b«w much supervision
may be lying around.

“Wang”: Speaking of decadent town-
ships, one of the most sorrowful examples of the
type is Steiglitz, about half-way between Geelong
and Ballarat (Vic.). Away back in the “ early
days” some fossickers settled themselves at Steig-
litz, and dug holes in the ground, and in a desul-
tory way kept scratching and getting enough gold
to make ends meet. Then the prospects and their
enthusiasm petered out, and Steiglitz relapsed
into its primeval state. About 1890 things began
to hum again, consequent upon the discovery of
much of the yellow metal. The principal mine
was the New Mariner, which was turning out
many ounces each Week, and alleged mines sprang
up like wild cats Springing up trees all over the
place. I had my first experience of share-speeu :
ating in connection with one of these claims.
An alleged friend induced tne
to part up all my rabbit-skin
money, and buy shares in some-
thing else with fur on it. Leigh
Road, or Bannockburn as it is
, now called, was the nearest rail-
way station to the El Dorado,
and Cobb and Co. ran a line of
coaches twice a day between
Steiglitz and Leigh Road. For
three or four years things boomed
at the town with the foreign
name, and the cats multiplied,
arid the coaches rattled gaily
backwards and forwards' every
day. Then came another slump,
and the mines, one after the
other, closed down, and the wild
cats pegged out, or bolted to
some other place, and the gay
coaches stopped running, and the
Plough Hotel stables became de-
serted, and the beer in the bar grew
less numerous, and Steiglitz lost
most of its population. To-day a
couple of unhappy looking pubs, and
two or three shops, arid a trio of
churches and a lot of humpies that
are falling to pieces, are all that is
left of Steiglitz to remind the infre-
quent visitor of the glory that has
departed. A visitor there is a
curiosity, and the oldest inhabitant
totters lafter him, and in return for a
pint tells him of the bygone days. Soon there’ll oe
no oldest inhabitant, and when there isn’t there’ll
be no Steiglitz.
Some imported curses recently proclaimed
noxious weeds by a worried Bananaland shire
council, whose dominion is threatened with
annihilation : Bathurst burr, Scotch thistle, lan-
tana, castor-oil plant, African box thorn, bindii,
Noogoora burr and prickly pear. And there are
dozens more, all imported.
“Jimmie Pannikin” : Be the discussion
between “Gip” and “ Polstan,” as to the drown-
ing of snakes. I lived for 10 experienceful years
in the Big Scrub. During the first five floods
were common. It used to rain between the
showers. I often found dead snakes after the
flood waters had subsided. They had not died of
whooping cough nor of measles. They had not
been drowned. My theory was, and is, that the
fierce swimming exercise, which the snakes were
unused to, overheated them, and caused them to
catch chills, congestion of the lungs, and various
pulmonary disorders ; and exposure and want of
suitable food finished them. Of course the dead
snakes that one finds after floods do have adrowned
look, which misleads superficial observers like
“Gip.” “Gip” only guesses. I agree with “ Pol-
stan.” He knows.
* * * *
When J. B. Patterson and his Lands
Minister, Jock Mclntyre, hit upon the Village
Settlement idea to solve the Victorian unem-
ployment problem, a settlement was planted
in Kamarooka forest, a most barren region,
20 miles north of Bendigo, and 20 miles
from anywhere else. By dumping the
settlers there it was thought they
would have to stop, since they
couldn’t well get away in a hurry.
One by one, however, more than
half the settlers managed to clear
out somehow. The rest hung on
to their uninviting land ; and
then, when a new race of settlers
began to spring up, it was found
that the youngsters had to either
tramp about seven miles to get
to school or else run wild. In
winter, the tramp is often through
gluey mud ; in the summer, it is
along a treeless track, swept by
hot winds. After a few years the
settlers agitated for a school, and
at last the Minister was appealed
to by Labor-member Tunnecliffe,
who had meantime been elected
for the district. Then the Minister
said he would remove the nearest
school on to the settlement. But
the cocky or two who live handy
to the school started to pull the
other way ; and, although the
Education Department every
month or so sends along word
that the school is going to be re-
moved immediately, nothinghas been
done, except that the site for the new school has
been purchased—butthat wasyears ago. Yet, when-
ever Fat wants to throw a particularly fierce gibe at
“ the unemployed,” he mentions the old village
settlements, and says that the failure of these
places is quite enough proof that you can’t do
anything for these worthless fellows. What the
“ worthless fellows ” have to put up with if they
stay on their settlements, Fat never mentions a'
all.'

ITOOKASHOO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Wednesday.—The Peary Club, New York, has
issued its mueh-heralded statement in disproval of Dr.
Cook’s claims.
The statement asserts that Commander Peary examined
the Etah Eskimos, including the boy Itookashoo.
Far away, it is true,
Lives poor Itookashoo.
In the chill Polar breeze,
With a name like a sneeze
And a nose very blue,
Dwells good Itookashoo.
Where the Polar bear prowls
And emits hungry growl's ;
Where the floes crack and drift
And the black, yawning rift
Awes the blubber-fed crew,
Shivers Itookashoo.
Where the Peary doth rage,
And at so much per page,
Doth imperil his soul
By describing the Pole,
Whilst his proud nigger pipes
That he hoisted the Stripes
And the Stars on it too,
There sits Itookashoo
In the midst of the lies
And the ice, with his eyes
Peeping out from his furs,
Lo ! he gaily occurs !
With his comedy name
He enlivens the game,
And it matters not which
He supports. Little Tich
Couldn’t cheer us up more ;
So we give him the floor
With a joyous “ Whirroo ! ”
And, “ Bedad, is it you!
Your name is your fortune, young Itookashoo I ”
N.B.W.
FOE.
BACK AGAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you cast off the social veneering,
The white and immaculate vest —
Arid in dirty old “ moles ” go careering
Across the big plains of the West,
I)o you growl at a burning or wetting,
At the pitiless sun or the rain?
Or rejoice in the glory of getting
Right back to the savage again ?
Do you pine for Society’s bondage,
The hollow old lies and the “ frill,’
As you shoulder the sweet, dewy fronoage
When the breath of the morning blows chill ?
When the fragrance of common “ Black Nugget ”
Makes a high-priced Havannah seem plain ;
You.could take your old briar and hug it
Going back to the savage again.
When the fierce black nor’-easter comes raging,
Like a demon to swallow your boat,
Is there aught the old hunger assuaging,
Like the sting of the spray at one’s throat?
As the combers go racing before us,
And we shoot down each long leaden plane,
How we laugh as we join their wild chorus,
Going back to the Vikings again 1
Though coldly Convention may ravage
The instincts of heart and of mind ;
Still we yearn to go back to the savage,
And cast all our cant to the wind,
And we turn from new gods as we make them,
To old ones barbaric and vain :
Till at last we abandon and break them,
Going back to the heathen again.
N.S.W.

Thus Sydney Evening News (16/10/’O9) :
Premier Ward had occasion, when speaking' in Maori-
land the other day, to deplore the malicious remarks
made by their own people about, the country. If this is
the case in Maoriland, it is infinitely worse here. A
large section of the community never loses any oppor-
tunity of publishing abroad the most malicious and un-
truthful reports about Australia, and it is a pitiful thing
that the Labor party should be systematically employed in
doing this. In Canada, both press and people suppress
every detail that would tend to do the country any
harm abroad. For instance, nothing is ever said of the
long, bitter winters, as this might frighten away immi-
grants. There is a tacit “conspiracy of silence ” among
Canadians on this subject, and a stranger would infer
that he was going to a particularly genial climate. This
pride and confidence in their country has made Canada
the best-known and most important of the British
Dominions. In the United States, also, during the
depression last year, an organised and determined effort
was made by the entire press and people not to make
public the facts about distress and stagnant trade, and to
show a bold and cheerful face to the world. It is a pity
we have not the pluck arid patriotism to stand up for the
country we live in, and forcibly discourage the man who
libels us abroad.
And in the same issue the same Evening News
tried to impress upon the stranger what a “par-
ticularly genial climate ” this country owns :
Each morning, drought or no drought, the sun rises, a
red and fiery ball, breathing heat from the very moment
of his appearance above the horizon. At 6 a.m. the
mercury is already among the eighties, and it
keeps steadily climbing up, up. till, often for
weeks together, it reaches well over the cen-
tury. What wind there is in summer is generally
hotter than our hottest north-easters in the city ; and
when it blows with violence it comes accompanied by
veritable simoons of fine sand and dust —sand that stings
the skin where it touches ; dust that blinds and chokes,
and permeates food, clothing, bedding, everything. . . .
And the nonsense that is talked about “ the dry
heat of the interior,” and its consequent lessened
power of fatigue compared with the “muggy” atmo-
sphere of Sydney! Possibly, regarded from a meteoro-
logical standpoint, it may be drier. But the fact that
the exertion demanded, by slow riding on horseback is
sufficient to bathe both horse and rider in perspiration,
does not seem to say much for the dryness from a
practical point of view. As for the manual laborer, the
sweat fills his boots and flows over the tops. But. the
nights—the citizen cannot begin to conceive the horror
of them.
And so on. It’s really nice to have a paper
which will put in a good word lilce that for this
country now and then.
* * * *
1 ‘ Portite ”: The old gaol at Port Macquarie
(N.S.W.) is to go. It was built by convict labor in
the ’3o’s, and has for years been one of the show
places of the locality. Its chief asset was the
alleged grisly “ memories” which hung around it.
Whenever a stranger arrived at the Port he was
hustled to the ancient place of reputed blood and
torture. Then the oldest horror-mongering in-
habitant let himself go joyfully on the subject of
the hardships the convicts underwent there in the
long ago, and told shudderingly of floggings,
hangings, drawings and quartering.*, and other
occurrences dear to the simple, honest, blanky-
minded bush person. The identical spot where
the criminals used to be scragged was indicated,
and the dark cell was dragged out into the light
of day and expatiated on at length. Now all this
is to end, and the district will be the poorer, con-
versationally, for it. Often have I been shown
the cells wherein Ned Kelly, Rufus Dawes, Deem-
ing, Starlight, Guy Fawkes, and other notabilities
in their line of industry foregathered at different
times ; and my marrow has grown chill at the
yarns of .what they did during incarceration. An
interesting feature was that, as a matter of hard,
unimaginative fact, thedashed old jug never had any
gore spilt in it worth mentioning, and at no time
harbored any of the famous evildoers whom tra-
dition ascribed to it. An aboriginal named
Terramidgee was the oidy person who died
violently there. He helped to murder some
cedar-getters at Gogo, and was strung up on the
green in front of the Port’s place of bogus horrors,
the entire nigger population of the surrounding
country being given free passes to witness the
show. The other habitues of the place either died
there of complaints such as croup, measles,
asthma and so forth, or went free and pegged-out
elsewhere. But that is a mere detail to an up-
eountry thrill-purveyor.
HAYSEED'S IRRELEVANCE.
“ Want a diary, sir?” “No. Took some medicine yestidy.”
“ Billy T.” : When Bussby, the celebrated
and most eloquent apostle of the hydropathic
cure for our Great Australian Thirst, came to
Crowfoot, the big apple centre, the only convert
he could make was Norwood ; but as Norwood
had been the ground-and-lofty exhibition drinker
of the district for 25 years, to have secured his
adherence to total and eternal abstinence was an
achievement, and Bussby was extremely proud of
it. Bussby was an itinerant curse-lifter, and
carried on his campaign in surrounding townships
with the aid of a horse and trap. Eventually his
nag maimed himself in a rut, and Bussby sought
to swap him for Norwood’s pale mauve pony.
Norwood declined a deal. Pressure was brought
to bear, and still Norwood resisted. The proselyte
was interviewed and cajoled. He was stubborn.
Reasons were asked. “ I won’t part with that
pony,” said the brand new Rechabite. “ He’s the
only horse I can get on and trust myself to when
I’m thoroughly drunk.”
“S.S.S.”: That letter which the seagull
took to Goode Island, Torres Strait (B. 30/9/’O9),
wasn’t written by a lovelorn Jap, I guess. The
writer was much more likely to be a Manbiang
maiden. To turn seabird or beast into a postman
is a well-established Torres Strait custom. When
a Manbiang or other island maiden takes a fancy
for a Badu buck she writes to him, and ties the
message to a turtle or a gull. If to a turtle, she
puts the message in a tin. The creature may
take it where she wants it to go; anyway she
has committed her fortune to the gods. If the
Badu boy does get the message, he paddles across
in his oanoe, and he and the lady go before the
Council. Each of these tiny islands has four
councillors, who take themselves “ big serious,”
and adjudicate. If the Council consents, the men
go forth and spear a dugong, and the women
make a mighty coppa-marie, and there is a
marrying.

MUM'S HOLIDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old man’s takin’ Roaney out (the bntchm’ straps is
bad), . ,
The ducks and geese is screechin’ out and Rover s barkin
mad,
The fowls an’ turkeys flock aroun’, the kids hez stopped
their play, t
An’ rush like rabbits through the house, ’cos mum s
come home ter-day.
She’s on’y bin away a week ; it seems a year an more ,
An’ Billy’s split his Sunday pants, an’ Rosie’s eyes is sore ;
The emu swallowed Harry’s watch ; the calves got in the
hay,
An’ dad’s bin narked as forty cats whi e mum has bin
away.
She never had no hollerday, as fur as I could tell,
And so the ole man “reckoned we could spire er fur a
spell ” ;
But, Lord, afore she’d scarcely gone, the hens forgot to
lay ;
An’ all the kids went runnin’ wiid when mum had gone
away.
Our old cow, Daisy, slipped her calf (them dogs o’Smith’s
is brutes),
An’ Joe he fell an’ sprained his wrist, through cartin’ in
some roots ;
The Cochin chicks hez got the pip, the goslin’s went
astray ;
An’ Sue, the fat ole sow, pegged out, since mum has bin
away.
But now she’s home we’re right again ; the place is bright
indeed,
An’ dad is whistlin’ down the yard (ole Roaney’s right
for feed),
But, ’pon me word, I often think, when workin’ in the
hay,
The world’ll stop, as sure as eggs, when mum is called
away.
S.A.
M. J. O’HAIR.

AN ADAMLESS EDEN.
Perth, Thursday—Negotiations have
been proceeding for a considerable time
for the establishment of a purely
woman's colony at Wilson's Inlet,
between Denmark and Albany. Only
widows and spinsters are being allowed
to come in.
That Australian bush tiger has turned up again. Two road-
makers, while at work near Jamberoo, heard him roar very
distinctly, although the weather was quite foggy at the time.
They downed tools and fled.
“For what,” they asked in telling the tale, “wliat’s a pick
or a shovel agin a tiger?” i
