AIi)VKRTXSKR (12/10/’()9): —
HEMMING WAY-STARR. —On the 6th of August,
by the Rev. J. Goodwin, Maud Heinmingway, of
Oarrieton, farmer, to Grace Starr, widow of the late
Stephen Starr, of Kooloolie.
The wedding of Maud and Grace sounds like
something out of the beaten track. Still this
paper has known a man named Maria.
Died in Sydney a few days ago, aged 80,
a Kanaka king, Philip Hitoti Henry. Henry, the
son of a Wesleyan clergyman, was born in Tahiti
in 1829. A native chief who was childless adopted
him in Pacific Island fashion by bestowing on him
his own name of Hitoti. So on the old man’s
death Henry became legally a Kanaka monarch.
He reckoned, however, that the life would be too
exciting, and he didn’t care for boiled missionary.
So he was just an uneventful bank official, and
never ate a missionary in all his days.
Parson Woodfull, the Methodist shepherd
who has been advertising his virtues from Colling-
wood (Melb.) of late years, is going to replace
Parson Worrall at the Methody tabernacle in
Golden Square, Bendigo. The difference between
Woodfull and Worrall is that the former is more
various in his advertising than the other shrieker.
When he gets to Bendigo he’ll discover some
awful social evils that the local inhabitants have
been blissfully ignorant of. Bendigo will never
know how very wicked it is until Woodfull has
been sending round the plate for a few months.
EMULATION.
President Roosevelt is heingpaid a dollar a word for his
hunting experiences. Well-known fact.
Picture of budding author writing a book with a
dollar a word and a fortune in view.
When the late Thomas Shaw, of Woori-
wyrite, in the Western District of Victoria, cursed
the Labor party and threatened to sell his land
and leave the country, he apparently had no land
to sell. At any rate, neither what was always
known as Shaw’s Worriwyrite estate (30,000 acres)
nor Yaneannia station, in N. S. Wales (about
500,000 acres), figure in his will, which has been
proved for a few hundreds only. Yet Woori-
wyrite alone is worth about £150,000. Apparently
Thomas had transferred his estates to his sons,
and had done so long enough before his death to
dodge the probate people.
Labor members nowadays turn up in
places that you wouldn’t have dreamt of looking
for them a few years ago. The chosen candidate
for the Franklin (Tas.) Federal seat is W. E.
Shoobridge. owner of Bushy Park, the finest
orchard and hop farm in the fruitful Derwent
Valley.
Mrs. Eliza Charlotte Kater, an old
identity, died on October 12 at The Meads,
Enfield (N.S.W.), aged 89 years. She was a
daughter of Major Edward Darvall, and married
Henry Herman Kater on July 30, 1840. Her
husband came to New South Wales in 1839 in the
ship Euphrates, which he chartered and loaded
with cattle and horses. The cattle helped to form
the celebrated herd of the late W. H. Suttor
of Brucedale, Bathurst. H. E. Kater. M.L.C.,
who married a daughter of William Forster, of
Brush Farm, one time Premier of N.S.W., is a
son of the deceased lady.
From the Oldest Inhabitant :
Apropos the Australian Alliance Assurance Company
and the London and Lancashire blend (B. 14/10/’O9l, this
is the story of how the A. A. Company was established.
In 1855 Michael O’Grady, of Roscommon, came to Sydney
from England, to found a branch of the People’s Pro-
vident Society, and in 1856 he established his head-
quarters in Collins-street West, Melbourne. He abandoned
the People’s Provident, and undertook the management
of the European Assurance Society. In 1861 he entered
Parliament for Villiers and Heytesbury, under the wing
of John O’Shannassy. He sat in six successive Par-
liaments, and was twice a Minister. The European
Assurance Society, being a dull-headed, unimaginative
brute, didn’t see any honor or profit in having its general
agent in Parliament, so Michael O’Gradv severed his con-
nection with it and joined with John O’Shanassy, R. S.
Anderson and others in forming the Australian Alli-
ance, which started business in 1861, at the south-
west corner of Elizabeth and Collins streets, where
Sayce and Cheetham had a crockery warehouse in
the mid-’fifties. O’Grady managed the Australian Alli-
ance till his death in 1875. His old English company, the
European, became in later years part of the notorious
Albert and European smash. Its end was bad but it
might have been a great deal worse. It at least helped
to make history. The most prolific cause of mortality
among British' insurance offices is simply that the
manager takes all the funds, sells the furniture, locks
the frontdoor and booksinlo the river. Then
the next Insurance Blue Book includes that co. in the
long list of those which have “Gone from Address.” A
single recent Blue Book recorded over a dozen such dis-
appearances. Sometimes it is added that Shyster and
Co., solicitors, 10 Back Alley, off Burst-street, can supply
information. Mostly nobody supplies anything, not even
the office boy’s back wages.
“ Srailer ” Hales, one time a Sydney pug
reporter, lias got his name into the cables again,
this time as the inventor of a “rifle grenade,”
which has been put through a successful test by
the German General Staff, whereupon “ Smiler ”
pocketed a trial- order. One way and another,
Hales is seeing a bit of life and the world. He
started as a carpenter in S. Aus.; then he was
reporter, prospector and mining expert; and
wherever there has been a shindy during the last
15 years he has poked the stump of a finger into
it. That stump, by the way, is “Smiler’s”
trophy of one of his earliest shindies, arising out
of a paragraph he wrote.
“Lorenz” : G. Reid seems, of late, to be
aspiring to the mantle of Bent, deceased, as
champion egotist of the Commonwealth. Bent
said he had been everything, at one time or an-
other, from admiral to sea-cook, and from un-
crowned king of Vic. to street minstrel. Reid,
not to be out-bragged, has in quick succession
claimed to have been the most audacious truant
of his school-days, a most multifarious lover, a
bad Presbyterian, also an astoundingly good
Presbyterian, several sorts of Plymouth Brother,
and varied descriptions of Methody (all unusual
to a degree), a cricketer of note, a champion
lolly-shifter, and about 1400 other utterly unim-
portant things. At a recent distribution of prizes
at N. Sydney he delivered himself of the follow-
ing deplorable stuff re “fagging” as practised in
the schools of Hingland :
Don’t imagine that fagging is done with after you
have left school, /left school when I was 13 years of age
.. . and I have been fagging ever since—for my employer
earlv in life, for iny clients at the Bar, and when in the
House for my constituents. After I became Premier I
fagged for the Governor, etc., etc.
There is seemingly no text that Reid can’t work
into a reference to some past doings of his own, in
which respect he remarkably resembles the late
Bent. What he lacks is what Bent lacked—a
sense of proportion to enable him to grasp the
complete unimportance of his prattlings.
The tribute of Frank Croson to a noble
woman who has departed :
Victoria would have done itself more honor—though
the recipient would scarcely have appreciated it—
had it, instead of publicly burying Bent, handed a
State funeral to Selina Murray Macdonald Sutherland.
The Scottish identity, whose firm face and mannish attire
belied her heart, commenced her strenuous life when she
reached the Wairarapa district of Maoriland in ’6B.
Masterton (M.L.) Hospital, the Melbourne Maternity
Home, and the Melbourne District Nursing Society were
all founded by her ; but it was as Miss Sutherland of the
Victorian Neglected Children’s Aid Society that she was
best known and loved. Her later years were stormy
ones, but since she parted from the Scots’ Church Society
and started her last refuge, there has ne\er been any
hesitation or doubt amongst city constables and others
what to do when women and children needed help. Was
a derelict mite picked up on a doorstep at dead
of night, the invariable remark was, “Take it to Miss
Sutherland.” Was a wayward girl in- a mess and
friendless: “Go round to Miss Sutherland ; she’ll look
after you.” She always seemed to have room, and was
always able to do the best thing. A young woman with
a baby at breast was once sent from a Gippslarid town-
ship to Melbourne gaol in default of a fine. Miss Suther-
land heard of it. There was no long confabulation, no
parleying with red tape, no tiresome deputation. She
went to the two or three who could pull the strings, and
when the train reached Princes Bridge a cab was waiting
to take mother and baby to the Home till the fine could
be financed. That instance was typical of hundreds.
And the hopeless, helpless youngsters I Two hundred
and forty-eight passed through her hands last year, and
that has been going on for 20 years. No wonder there
were sad eyes in many homes when the news was told.
Even the recent legacy left by Miss Meglin, of Diamond
Creek (Vic.), which would have made her closing years
more fruitful and more peaceful, came too late : the land
was to have been transferred the very day she died. So
there is no reward. Neither honor, nor riches, nor fame
—as the world knows them. Only the knowledge that
she fully carried out the command of her Master: “Suffer
little children.”
NORMAN
LINDS A Y.
Melbourne’s pro-
tector of waifs and
strays, Miss Suther-
land, left little but the
record of her good deeds, her sole possessions
being about £lO in the Savings Bank, a gold
watch and chain, and a few household goods. The
cash and furniture go to the home, the jewellery
to a relative in Scotland.
“ Melb ” writes :
Baroness Burdett Coutts suggested “ The Catch of the
Season ”to Seymour Hicks. His heroine is Angela, the
Cinderella. The Baroness was Angela, a kind of Cinder-
ella, with two proud aunts, the Marchioness of Bute and
the Countess of Guildford. James Coutts, banker,
married his brother’s servant girl, Eliza Starkie. She
became the mother of Lady Bute, Lady Guildford and
Lady Burdett, wife of Sir Francis Burdett. Angela was
the daughter of the Burdetts. On the demise of Mrs.
Coutts, Coutts married Harriet Mellon, an actress. Lady
Bute and Lady Guildford turned up their proud noses.
Coutts left ail his money to Harriet. She became
Duchess of St. Albans, and turned up her proud nose.
She left all her money, about £2,000,000, to poor little
Angela Burdett.
The two biggest ecclesiastical guns at the
recent R.C. congress in Sydney were good
sports in their day. The Cardinal’s strong
point was the high jump ; he was easily the
champion “ lepper ” at the Irish College
in Rome. Archbishop Redwood, of Maori-
land, went over the sticks too; but he
preferred to be carried. He wasn’t exactly
a jockey, but he rode several of his father’s
horses on the old Hornebush (N.S.W.)
racecourse. The Archbishop’s father was
gamekeeper for the Cliffords, who lived
on the borders of Norfolk (England).
When the Cliffords went to Maoriland, he
also went; he took up land near Nelson
and Marlborough, and bred sheep and
horses. Some of these horses have run
on Australian racecourses, and the Red-
wood animal’s descendants can still hold
their end up.
The 11th Regiment left Sydney for
England in 1857 ; but though that is a good
while ago there are still living in New
South Wales? three old soldiers which it left
behind—Sergeant Lees, aged something be-
tween 80 and 90; Charles Smith, of En-
more, aged 82; and James Sangford, of Hamil-
ton (Newcastle), aged 87.
Mrs. Mary Campbell, a colored matron,
died a few days ago at the Macquarie Mission
Camp, Wellington (N.S.W.). Her exact age was
not known, but as her memory professed to go
back to the time when Wellington was a convict
depot, and she spoke of the flogging of convicts
on Die Hard Hill, she must have been of some
venerability. She was the last of the aborigines
who were ministered to by Parson William Wat-
son, who (assisted by his wife, Anne Watson) was
aboriginal protector at Wellington Valley in 1832.
N.S.W. Secretary for Railways, Hugh
McLachlan, brother of D. C., the Commonwealth
Public Service Commissioner, sweated himself to
death. He succumbed to mental exhaustion at
53, when he was physically sec up for another 20
years. A while back he went to the International
Railway Congress at Washington, more for a
relief from long and continuous strain than any-
thing else ; but he was rushed over thousands of
miles of railway lines, and he toiled all the time.
He didn’t recuperate in the least, and he resumed
a billet which required ceaseless attention and
activities when he ought to have been rusticating
on a farm, chasing the cow out of the corn or
shooing the crow off the fig-tree.

of Zeppelin, the man who gave the stait to the
new craze for flying. Of Zeppelin :
Retired Wurtemberg cavalry general. Fought on
Union side in American Civil War. There got 1
about military ballooning. Won the Iron 9 ross , •
Franco-German War. Then started a-pestermg
airships. Floated a £lO,OOO airship co. at last. ay nuu-
summer, 1900, his first dirigible was built at HaKe Con-
stance. Only kept up 17min. Tried again in three
months. Kept up lhr. 20min. Funds ran out. Could
not trv again till January, 1900. Terrible storm . busted
the ship when 1500 ft. up. Had another in nine month..
Unqualified and practicable success; carried i j
passengers 69 miles in 2hr. 17min. The Ge man
War Office was then converted from scepticism
to faith. In June, 1908, the Zeppelin IV went round
and about over Lake Constance. On Ist July she electri-
fied the world with her famous cruise into Switzerland
12 hours at over 37 miles an hour. The Government
granted £25,000. Public enthusiasm swelled it to
£300,000. Government took over Zeppelins dockyard.
On March 19 this year a Zeppelin carried 26 military pas-
sengers over 150 miles, round Lake Constance, (“Oppin,,
imaginary borubs on St. Paul’s, Westminster Abbey, i
Houses of Parliament, Woolwich Arsenal, Buckingham
Palace, and so forth. Kaiser chuckling, the flagship is
now stationed at Metz, and a training ship at Beilin,
Nobody outside knows how many Zeppelins are being
built at his dockyard of Friedrichshafen. Biggest known

Sergeant Cawood, of Parra-
matta (N.S.W.). holds the Aus-
tralian record for long service
as a volunteer. He has entered
upon his fiftieth year of the
business. During the whole
half-century he has been con-
nected with the Parramatta
company, and is believed to be
the most continuous volunteer in the British
dominions.

A LAY OF WELCOME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The fife and drum band was present to welcome the
Premier. — Maoriland News Item.
J. Ward has come again, the skies are blue.
Fill the shrill fife and tootle—rootle—too
A lay of welcome.
His praise is borne afar ; he went to do
Vast deeds of prowess—boodle—boodle—boo !
He brings a million !
Thump on the drum, he left us in the stew
’Twixt beef and onions—doodle —doodle—doo,
He holds the ladle !
The spring has come, and all the world is new,
Be sure he’ll pull us throotle —throotle —through,
He’s been to Cohen.
A simple folk are we, our wants are few ;
All that we need is cash, and surely—surely—shoo
He’s gone and got it.
Someone must pay the piper, it is true ;
But does it matter who the—who the—who
The devil does it!
J. Ward has got the cash, the skies are blue,
Fill the loud fife, and (cootie —tootle—too)
Make him damned welcome !

Trout Streams of Kosciusko.
During the summer months, when the great thaw sets
in, and the melting of winter’s levelling snows reveals the
peaks and rugged gorges of Kosciusko, the world’s oldest
mountain, the call of the highlands gradually fades from
the ear of the ski-runner, tobogganer and skater, and
renews the enthusiasm of the trout fisher and moun-
taineer.
Those famous snow-fed trout streams —the Snowy,
Thredbo and Eucumbene —lie within easy distance of
the Government Accommodation Houses—The Creel,
on the banks of the swift running Thredbo an the foothills
of Kosciusko; and the magnificent Hotel Kosciusko,
situated on the uplands, at an elevation of 6000 ft. Round
about the hotel, trout streams may be seen in the making.
Thousands of tiny rivulets babble and splash among
granite boulders in their headlong rush from the uplands
to the valley bottom. A short walk from The Creel
leads to the confluence of the Thredbo and the Snowy,
which at this point have broadened out into noble
streams, and are well filled with beautiful fish. The
trout season commences on November 1 and lasts till
April 30.
From the Hotel Kosciusko a good road leads to the
cairn on the summit of Mt. Kosciusko. The scenery is
remarkably grand. A veritable wildferness of tumbling
mountain ranges stretches away in every direction—to
the south deep into Gippsland, and to the east to the
fringe of the Pacific.
The journey to Kosciusko is generally via Oooma, which
is 12 hours by rail from Sydney, and thence by motor to
The Creel and coach to tfie hqtel.
Full particulars will be supplied by the Government
Tourist Bureau, Challis House, Sydney.
TROUTING ON THE KOSCIUSKO UPLANDS.
1. The Creel on the Thredbo River—a famous trout-fishing box. 2. The Snowy River, near the Creel.
3. The Hotel Kosciusko, adjacent to some of the finest trout waters in the world.

THE BIG THING IN
CUBING INDIGESTION
*
Is to give the Stomach more
Strength.
*
A bad Sufferer with Indigestion
Tells how This can be done.
There is one great point about curing indigestion
and stomach trouble that sufferers with the com-
plaint must remember. When the stomach be-
comes too weak to digest ordinary food, the
pain and trouble caused by eating an ordinaly
meal is called indigestion. The stomach is
intended to digest good wholesome food,
and the condition called indigestion can only be
cured by restoring the stomach to a proper state
of health. If you take a purgative to move the
meal along or a slop diet, the stomach isn’t
gaining strength, is it?
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, which are a blood
former and nerve tonic, aim at strengthening all
the organs of the body. The fact that anaemic
people always suffer with indigestion shows the
intimate relation between the blood supply and
the digestive system. That’s why the blood
making qualities of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have
cured indigestion in a common-sense way. This
Brisbane man tells how he was cured
“ My occupation is a pretty trying oneattimes,
and though I am naturally robust I got a had
attack of indigestion,” said Mr. Arthur Cook,
Warry Street, The Valley, Brisbane. “My hack
began to ache terribly. When I stooped I’d feel
as if I were going to snap in two. My head would
ache terribly and quite daze me. Sometimes I’d
eat ravenously and then not fancy a bite for days.
I lost all energy. I got very pulled down. I
grew thin and very pasty looking. I didn’t sleep
well. Each morning my tongue would he furred.
After I ate anything it would form in a hard
lump at the end of my breast bone. I’d have a
nasty taste in my mouth and occasionally bring up
what I had swallowed. I’d have specks floating
before my eyes and I suffered a lot with heartburn.
The doctor’s medicine did me no good, so I
thought I’d try Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. The
first three boxes didn’t seem to do me much good,
hut after that I started to mend. The food began
to digest and soon I could eat a hearty meal. The
strength returned to my limbs and by the time I
had finished the seventh box the ache was nearly
gone from my hack. I filled out again and the
headaches vanished. Ten boxes put me in the
best of health.”
The price is 2s. fid. per box, 6 boxes 15s. 3d.,
and if you have trouble in getting them, send a
postal note for the amount to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd., Sydney, and
they will be sent post free by return mail.*

POINTS
Three members of the Peake (S.A.)
Government are to retire early next year to make
room for tliree Butlerites. Honorary Minister
Addison will stand for the Senate, and Attorney-
General Mitchell is to go to the Northern Territory
as Govt. Resident. Lands Minister Coombe may
be made Commissioner of Police if he thinks it
good enough. He is a clever journalist, who can
adjust himself to almost any position.
Senator Trenwith has on hand a Bill “ to
amend the Constitution to give the Federal
Parliament power to adjust the industrial con-
ditions in the various States”; and if he can
ioree it to a division it will be interesting to watch
the side-stepping of the Fusionists. Only the
pledged Fusionists will oppose it, and they
scarcely dare do so directly. The elections will
be very near by then, and the electors will have
had time enough to analyse the excuses and not
time enough to forget them. By the way, what
are the odds about the Age backing Trenwith?
It dearly loves to be on the winning side ; but it
also dearly hates Trenwith.
Of course you want health, that is natural,
and Chamberlain’s Tablets will positively help
you to get it.*
Labor leader and local preacher Verran
(S. A.) is a hot opponent of gambling in all shapes,
and he feels very sad because Blundell, one of his
party, has brought in a Bill providing for a half-
crown totalisator. The measure has been intro-
duced to give “equal opportunities to all.”
Hitherto the machine has refused to look at any
bet under five shillings.
Ex-Senator D. J. O’Keefe, who lost his
seat by a few votes at the last Senate election in
Tasmania, has been selected by a large majority
of Labor League ballots to again chase the job.
Ex-State Attorney-General Gilmore is up in the
preliminary Labor ballot; but so far he isn’t up in
the front bunch.
Although she had to be hustled a bit, the
woman voter of Brighton (Vic.) did her duty
pretty thoroughly at the first State election in
which she was entitled to express her opinion.
Of the total females on the Brighton roll, no less
than 83 per cent, dropped a paper in the ballot
box. while the male element only scored 63 per
cent.
The old thing happened at a sale of dairy
farms on the St. Petrox and Lamb’s Valley
stations, outside Maitland (N.S.W.), the other
day. There were 11 lots or farms, and two
men bought six of them. The fear of a pro-

cut up their principalities, but it wjll take the
tax itself to keep principalities from growing up
again.
Thus the Old Soldier :
Reuter cables again that the authorities in India are
getting anxious. “ The Amir of Afghanistan is said to be
unsympathetic towards India, while his brother, Nasr
Ullah Khan, is actively anti-British.” Nothing more
likely. They have been importing arms, ammunition and
very likely cannon through Persia, and perhaps think
they are ready to repeat the old plundering game.
Afghanistan has lived on the loot of India for the last 900
years; and even since it has been nominally under
British protection, the Amirs have been paid what was to
all intents and purposes blackmail to keep them quiet.
But times are changed, and the Hindu has forgotten his
fear of the Afghan, and has begun to agitate against the
Government, which is really his only protection against
plunderers. He claims, on the strength of his nominal
two-thirds of the population, the right to rule all other
races and creeds, and it is really a fine chance for the
Amir to set himself up as the champion of the 80 or 90
millions of oppressed Mohammedans, who are 53 per cent,
of the people of the Panjab, the nearest province to
Afghanistan, and 62 per cent, in Eastern Bengal. Still,
numbers never have mattered much in India. Till ’4B,
when the Punjab was annexed, for example, it was
governed by the Sikhs, who are only 7 per cent, of its
people.
“ Red Tape Junior ” speaks :
The eternal feminine cropped up before the Wagga
(N.S.W.) Land Board at a recent sitting, when the eligi-
bility of a lone woman as a closer settler was debated.
Woman, it is ordained, should aspire to closely settle in
the arms of her doting husband ; but the lady in this
case had settled on mother earth, and wished to get rid
of the job. The applicant was a single girl who had been
allotted an area of State land, and she wished to transfer
it to her father. The land had been worked and managed
by the father, who was ineligible under the Act to acquire
the land originally, and on the face of it the case looked
like dummying. The Board found that bad faith was not
proved, and having granted the application proceeded to
discuss women’s rights as far as State lands are con-
cerned. Single women, the Board considered, are a
nuisance. Either a parent appears, or a husband, whom
perhaps the Board would not have on its mind, becomes
virtually the owner of the selection, or the lady becomes
an old maid of unimproved value after a long struggle
with nature in the shape of drought, dingoes and land
agents. On the other hand, if a single woman of
undaunted mien, with an axe in her hand and a hatpin
in her mouth, should decide to chuck waiting in a city
restaurant at 10s. a week and chase the wily heifer to the
milking bail, why shouldn’t she be allowed to? Why,
indeed, should a woman be daunted by the said heifer if
she is capable of trimming race-nats for infuriated cus-
tomers? And, anyhow, women have votes, so it wasn’t
for the Board to say they weren’t to have selections.
The opinion the Board came to was that the single
young woman is a desirable settler and a great annoyance

The decision of the P. and O. Company to
run its nigger-manned ships across to Auckland
(M.L.) is the sort of thing which shrieks for strong
measures of repression. The nigger is reprehen-
sible all the time ; but he is more so than usual
now, when Australia has to create a fleet of its
own and needs to rear all the reliable white sea-
men it can. The P. and O. was always a brutally
selfish and unpatriotic animal, which, so long as it
saw a little extra profit ahead, apparently didn’t
care twopence if the breed of British seamen died
out for want of employment and the British navy
was left derelict on the seas, and its further
extension hereabouts will be a calamity.

“ H.R.” writes from up North :
Re “ Sara S. Nolan ” and her wild, weird whoop against
defence. It may surprise the lady to know that not only
is the conscription law still in force in Britain as regards
the county militia, but the press-gang law is still in full
force, just at it has been for centuries past. Of course,
it isn’t used now, seeing that there is no great naval war
on hand, but then it never was used sfive in the case of
naval war. As regards the lady’s comforting assur-
ance that there will never be war with Germany
because that country’s very existence depends on its trade
with the British Empire, she will probably be surprised to
hear that the British Empire depends more on Germany,
in a trade sense, than Germany does on the British
Empire—that is to say, Germany buys more from the
British Empire than it sells to it. Instead of talking
ignorantly through her Merry Widow hat, let the lady
hunt up the article on Germany in the ‘‘Statesman’s
Year Book” and learn something. I, for one, have no
desire to see money spent on armaments if they are not
necessary, but a lady who has contrived to be out-
rageously wrong in every important statement she makes
fails to convince me re the lack of necessity.
The uselessness of Bruce Smith’s nigger
friend as a seaman was again shown in the case of
the British steamer Parua, wh.ch was Wrecked
among the Fiji Islands a few weeks ago. In the
dead waste and middle of the night the Parua
drove its lofty, thoughtful brow into a coral reef.
Straightway the colored crew went mad and
drunk and frantic and raving, and cantered all
over the place uttering a weird disarrangement of
sounds. In the end a lifeboat was manned, and
some of the passengers sent ashore. But instead
of returning to the ship for the rest of the
people the colored sailors left the boat on the
beach and fled inland, howling as they ran. As
it happened, the Parua had a deck cargo of tim-
ber, and out of that a raft was constructed on
which the rest of the people got ashore with diffi-
culty. Bruce Smith’s favorite nigger seaman
didn’t help them. He was still howling in the
adjacent forest.
The Labor party is beginning to see that
there’s a heap of waste going on in the Melbourne
municipalities, and that it is the producer who
has to stand the racket of it. Consequently, one
or two schemes are brewing : (1) in the direction of
amalgamating South Melbourne, St. Kilda and
P’ran ; (2) ditto as to Richmond, Collingwood
and Fitzroy ; and (3) in getting a comprehensive
report on tramway electrification and electric
lighting, the 20 suburban councils being invited to
combine for the purpose. The matter is still in
the conversational stage, which means that there is
no present fear of Socialism arriving and bumping
capital out of the country before it has had time
to collect itself, so to speak. Still, it is cheerful
to note that someone is making a start.
The exploded McMillan pushed himself
into print the other day to deplore the wickedness
of a N.S.W. proposal at present under discussion.
As every railway built with public money, and
every culvert laid down, and every harbor im-
provement made adds a fraction to the value of
the big idle landlord’s territory, the Government
proposes, in a very small limited way, to give the
big landlord notice that it doesn’t intend to go on
giving bigger and bigger prices for his land—it
wants the right to take it over later on at its
present value. “Such an Act must paralyse all
efforts and stopall improvements, ’’wails McMillan;
which proves that McMillan is still either a very
ignorant or a very unscrupulous person, seeing
that any improvements made either before or
after the Bill passes are to be paid for quite inde-
pendently of the land. And the idea of para-
lysing the efforts of the big landlords of N.S.W.
is humorous, anyhow. The fruitful Peel River
Estate, for instance, had been so energetically
improved that, when an accident happened to the
man who was valuing it for the owners, the driver
had to wander nearly the whole of a day and a
night before he could find anybody to go to the
injured man’s rescue.

The administration of the Old Age Pen-
sions Act is very languid in Westralia. Senatoi
de Largie mentioned, the other day. that a battler
of 80 had toddled into a Groper country
town to apply for his pension, and had
been kept waiting under consideration
for such a time that he had become
stranded and hadn’t a copper to toddle
back with. It seems that the I used
Government has appointed one special
magistrate to deal with pension claims
in Westralia, which is a territory of
975,920 square miles, and that makes
things a trifle slow for the aged and the
toothless.

The other day the Lalbert estate, at
Armadale (Vic.), was auctioned, and the lot
realised at the rate of £lOOO per acre.
Landlord,” remarked the evening Herald, “ has
held this choice block for some time, until it has
become surrounded by some of the finest suburban
homes.” And then Mr. Fat Landlord got his re-
ward—£looo per acre, mostly value created by the
builders of “the finest suburban homes,” and by the
State, which has provided the suburb with a rail-
way. No wonder Mr. Fat Landlord bitterly
opposes the land-tax, which would have secured
to the State a little of that £lOOO. But why the
State should present Mr. Fat Landlord with a
House of his own in which to prevent the State
from getting a bit of its own, the Lord only-
knows.
OUR DREAMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have wakened—like Van Winkle, we have risen from
our sleep ;
And we find our dreams exploded and our visions stale
and cheap!
We were dreaming of a city such as Athens was of old,
But we find they’ve planned a suburb, where the Herald
may be sold !
They require a tawdry village, near enough to heed the
call
Of a press that reeks with venom, and is poisonous and
small !
We had pictured us a city ’mid a thousand silver streams ;
But we woke to find Yassberra, and to hear the Herald’s
screams !
We were dreamers—we were foolish, for we thought that
men of sense
Would be speaking for Australia, and would cast the
Small Things hence.
We imagined that the Rulers who controlled this blessed
land
Would be Statesmen, would be Doers—and it’s hard to
understand ;
For we thought that Alfred Deakin was a person with a
spine;
But we find he’s tame and abject, only fit to cringe and
whine !
When we left him he was soaring like an eagle to the
sky ;
Now he’s Fused and tightly hobbled—will Jehovah tell
us why ?
We were thinking—we are puzzled when we think of
what we thought,
For we cannot see a symptom of the things Australia
sought !
iVe had visions of a Gateway where the arsenals might
be —
Where the battleships might flourish in the dockyards by
the sea !
But we find a shoddy harbor, not a real Australian port—
And we’re somewhat flabbergasted that the facts have
fallen short!
We were thinking of Australia, but the truth is very
plain ;
Yea, the Insects thought of ’Berra—and the contrast
gives us pain !
We were dreaming of a city with a Democratic press ;
But we find our dream is busted, so we speak with
bitterness.
We had visions—see the wreckage ! —of a press that would
expound
Views that fitted Big Australia with a gospel strong and
sound !
We imagined that the Herald and the Argus and their
tribe
Would be smitten night and morning by the nation-
building scribe !
We had thought—in short, we reckoned that this con-
tinent would gee ;
But we find that sundry prophets slump and shrivel
horribly !
We were thoughtful—in our visions, lo ! we gladly sailed
away
To the island once discovered by one Raphael Hythloday !
Yea, we thought our keel had grounded on Utopia’s
blessb 1 shore—
We explored (in dreams) the City that was raised by
Thomas More !
We encountered the Republic Plato fashioned long b.c.,
And likewise the land ideal of the recent Bellamy !
All of these we contemplated, and applauded—in our
dreams ;
But, alas, the Chance has vanished, and the whole a
swindle seems !
For the views of men are sordid, and their ways are very
cheap—
Jones may shout and yell for Labor, but his arm is slow
to reap !
Smith may prate of legislation “ when the Nation shall
control ”
But he pusheth with the Insects when the log is swift to
roll !
Yea, the gas evaporateth, and it leaves a sediment
Which is useless for the purpose of this harassed con-
tinent !
Brown is apt to sink his gospel sixty fathoms in the
sea—
Yet he dredgeth up its ruins when it suiteth Binks, M.P. !
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.

FORTY-THIRD REPORT. Presented to the Shareholders at the Ordinary General Meeting on 20th October, 1909.
The Directors have pleasure in placing in the hands of Shareholders the annexed Balance-Sheet and Statement
of Accounts for the half-year ending 30th September, duly audited and certified.
The net profits (including £4595 15s. 2d. brought from.last half-year) amount to .. .. £22,569 18 11
which it is proposed to deal with as follows, viz :
To pay a dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per annum £10,500 0 0
To transfer to the reserve fund (making it £122,500) 7,500 0 0
And to carry forward the balance .. 4,569 18 11
£22,569 18 11
The cash reserves of the bank are higher than usual at the moment, owing to the increase of State Government
funds, consequent on the recent successful flotation in London of a loan for £1,500,000 —the extra funds, however,
will shortly be absorbed in the ordinary course of business.
The Directors would once more invoke the assistance of the Shareholders in spreading the business of the bank
by introducing new accounts, etc. ; their services in this direction in the past have greatly helped to place the bank
in its present flourishing condition.
By order/ f the Board,
H. T. WILSON', General Manager.

WHY GO GREY?
Premature greyness is due to scalp disease, and is
curable under the right treatment. The scalp becomes
healthy and the natural color returns without using any
dyes or stains. The right treatment must be individual,
prescribed only for the case in question, and it need not
be expensive.
I have cured hundreds of patients. One writes as
follows:
“ The treatment has done my hair good in every way.
There is a fair amount of young hair growing ail over.
The hair looks very glossy, and there is not a single grey
hair left. lam indeed pleased with the results.”
11. C., Hobart.

Am 1 late, deah ? You’ll forgive
me, won’t you ? Ifeel so fri’fully
tired and seedy. What time did
you get away last night? I
had to stay at Auntie’s and this
morning when 1 woke up she
was standing over me with a
cup of that wretched tea of
hers-T-it’s so horribly scenty —1
simply had to drink it, but l
hav’nt felt well ever since. Why
on earth she gets the awful stuff
1 don’t know. Mother’s tried
time after time to make her use
R ob u r but she won’t, she says
“ she doesn’t believe in things
that are advertised” and says,
“she doesn’t care whether they
are good or not, she won’t use
them,” so old fashioned, isn’t it?
And she can’t be made to
understand that everything that
is any gocd is advertised in
these days. Oh! 1 say! there
are the boys! Come along, deah.
We’ll make them tak.t us down,
to afternoon tea. I’m just dying
for a freshly-made cup of Robur.
It’s the only thing that’ll put me
right.
The Block Girl.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Octobeb 19, 1909.
My Dkak Abini,—
I went to meet Mrs. Lily E -avton Asche deter-
mined that I would not ask 1 ler any stereotyped,
moth-eaten questions. I did not want to hear
where and when she made her first appearance, or
of the opposition of her people—conservative,
church-going folk—to her adoption of the stage as
a profession, or of her dreadiul attack of stage
fright at a psychological moment, or of
the stamped leather work with which she
tilled her leisure hours. Neither did I yearn for
heroics in praise of Australia, its audiences, and
the beauty of its women. Probably in any case
the visiting Lily would have talked none of this-
tiipe, but in case she might I drew up this list of
questions so that there should be no common-
placeness about my interview :
What age are you ?
What make-up do you use for daylight wear?
What is the length of your stride when travelling at
your utmost speed ?
Are you a Unitarian ?
Do you drink—milk ?
Do you sometimes borrow Petruchio’s ear-rings?
Are you a Suffragette?
What is your opinion about Cain marrying a monkey ?
Of which Polecat are you a partizan—Peary or Cook ?
Have you often dropped dead?
Did you ever hunt the wild ass in Persia?
Do you regard whale fishing as a sport for women ?
Unfortunately, I left the list in the tram ; con-
sequently there was no interview, only a pro-
longed causerie warranted not to reproduce suc-
cessfully in print, except in spots. One of the
exceptions concerns the fair creature’s house at
St. John’s Wood. It is an old place with an old
garden—the kind that recalls gardens in the verse
of Austin Dobson. The Asche pair had abode
there a year before one of them found a secret door-
in the floor of a summer-house irr the garden. It
opened on a spiral staircase of stone that led to an
underground room with thick concrete walls. A
suggestion of a fireplace is present, but nothing to
indicate the raison d'etre of thechamber. Possibly
it was a secret meeting place for some who were
agin the Government. It is too far from the coast
to admit of its having been a smuggler’s lair ; it is
older than the invention of the duty on whisky,
so it did not house an illicit still. A coiner’s den
or monk’s cell it may have been. Records credit
the one-time presence of a monastery in the
neighborhood.
A mention of “ Attila,” the play Mr. Lawrence
Binyon wrote for the Asches, is the cue for the
lady to speak admiringly of one Ricketts, a new
designer who planned one aspect of the pro-
duction. His genius runs to barbaric grandeur in
decoration and the weird and startling effect.
In the feast scene on Attila’s bridal night
(Sydneysiders who have read the drama will
remember that the great Hun married a lady
just to fill in a few unoccupied days whilst he was
waiting for a more exalted Burgundian princess
who was on her way to him), the banquet chamber
is pillared and decorated in blood-red. The
Hunnish King and his bride are robed in red, and
the dancing women are clad in red sheepskins.
Stephen Phillips? "Yes!” She looks a little
surprised, and asks : “ Anything of his been pro-
duced here?” "No. Some of us gloated over
the reading of his play poem ‘ Herod,’ and would
like to know if he wears nice ties,” “ I played
in ‘Herod’ when I was with the Beerbohm
Tree management,” replies the Lily dame. "It
was when the late Queen died. Miss Maud
Jeffries was the Queen Mariamne of the piece.
When she was taken ill I did my best in the
part.” She switches off to a panegyric on the
Jeffriesian art and beauty iq the r6le of Herod’s
Queen. (N.B. —This Herod —Herod the Great—
must not be confused with the foolish person who
gave away a head that wasn’t his to the Salome
girl now dancing at the Tivoli.)
The latest papers from London mention the
gifted Stephen Phillips’ bankruptcy. His
liabilities amount to £3OO. Not £300,000 —just
£3OO. And the receiver mentioned that the
bankrupt could not attend his examination
because he had not the means to travel from
Brighton, where he lived. As he is a distinguished
poet, this state of things may be Phillips’ own
fault. Indeed, report said of him some time
agone that he was well on his way to the demnition
bpw-wows. But the man who wrote “Herod”
ought to Vie retrieved by the State and kept
honorably and judiciously in the Hotel Cecil.
In the search for a word to describe the Asche-
Lily, "exotic” is the best that presents itself.
And her vivid coloring—large brown eyes, golden
brown hair and vermilion lips are most artfully
enhanced by her gowning of old rose in something
soft and silken and an Indian necklace of big
green, jade-like beads on a gold cord. She con-
fesses to a passion for old-rose tones. She
is a good height, too—half an inch short
of sft. Tin., and neither plump nor thin. Her
nose isn’t the transverse section that it seems
to be in her portraits and in certain lights on the
stage. It is probably the poetical type of nose
that Herrick (or was it Herbert ?) wrote verses to.
Mr. Oscar Asche, off the stage, looks like a
superior Eskimo. Perhaps he is one by descent,
as' his people come from near the blubber-oil
tracts of the tribe which is now so much in the
Polar limelight.
The Asches and some of “The Flag Lieuten-
ant” company have been sharing repasts with
the Dudley Excellencies at Federal Govern-
ment House. Dear, dear; how times have
changed for the better in the mummer’s case !
Once, if he did not belong to some baron or
other person of high degree, and wandered abroad
without a license signed by two Justices of the
Peace, he ran the risk of being grievously whipped
and burned through the gristle of the right ear
with a hot iron. That was an Elizabethan enact-
ment. And when he died he was often denied
Christian burial. In some old statutes he is still
classed with rogues and vagabonds. The wheel
has come full circle for the mime, and he is
swellin’ wisiblv either from the pride of life or the
Governor-General’s lunches.
The Oscar Asches have taken a flat at Cromer,
whence Mr. and Mrs. Jim Osborne have just fled
for their station. The actor-folk moved into their
new abode with a retinue of maids and valets last
Saturday.
Some day I shall stain the white radiance of
eternity with a woman’s blood. The tragedy will
occur at a matinee, and the victim will be the
woman who sits in front of me wearing a hat like a
balloonist’s parachute. At “The Tame Cat”
matinee on Saturday, the uglj' fashionable view-
obstructor raged in the front row of the circle
with uncommon ferocity. One particularly
objectionable big black hat, like a first-class
hearse in chip, made me its victim. Unfor-
tunately I hadn’t a match and no man was near
to give me one. Chip burns pretty well, and
there was a large area to be cleared.
Lady Dudley, her two elder daughters, and
Captains-Courageous Rome and Gilliat were pre-
sent to see the male Tame Cat rescued from the
beautifully manicured (or should it be pedi-
cured ?) claws of the cattess. The play was
reproduced by the Sydney Comedy Club in aid of
the Bush Nurses’ Charity now being organised by
Lady Dudley, and there was a well-filled house,
mostly of well-known persons.
Several of the players were shaky in their lines,
and they were seldom quick enough in pouncing
on their cues. They ought to rehearse their
pounces. Mrs. John Sandes, who was the
Elaine Cordemoy of the comedy, played
after a stately fashion in grande-dame style.
Miss Viva Frankel, as the Marchioness of Calm-
seas, was most satisfactory. She did what most
girls will not do —made herself up to look
the fatal forties, and she infused the right
amount of acid into her tones and manner.
Miss Winifred Birch, as Marjorie Heinault,
the nice girl who loves the Tame Cat,
looked an ideal ingenue. One doesn’t often
see on any stage so pretty a figure with such un-
commonly good lines of shoulder and back, iknd
she played well, too, with girlish gravity and re-
straint. Miss Elsie Prince managed well the part
of the girl (Moth Vayne) with the chronic head-
ache and a craving to be cuddled. And
Miss Dora Linden (as Pamela Gapp, badly
afflicted with canineolatry), was very amus-
ing with her loathly small dog. All
the women in the cast were unusually good
for amateurs, and the men were not so bad as to
be beyond hope. Mr. Fletcher, as the masterful
man of the piece, did capitally; and Mr. Leslie
Casey, as Cecil Thayer, the lovable Blamed Cat,
can afford to purr and arch his back with satis-
faction.
Young Dion Titheradge was greeted with “ All
hail, thou Benedict, thou new-married man ! ” by
some of his fellow brass-bounds in “The Flag
Lieutenant” on Saturday night—before or after
the play, of course. He was married on Saturday
at the parental home, at Rose Bay, to Miss
Margaret Bolton, an English girl who came out
by the Ormuz under engagement to the 21-year-
old youngster.
Mr. Harry Rickards of the Tivoli is probably
more easily pleased than Herod (which I suppose
was Hebrew for Harry) of Galilee. The fore-
going opinion was built up after seeing Miss
Athmore Grey do the Salome dance. Herod 11.
was no youth when he saw the Salome dance. He
had divorced his first wife and stolen his second
one from his brother. He had been walloped in
battle by his first father-in-law-and-cursed by the
first apostle. And he was a drifting, unambitious
man without enthusiasm. Now, when that bat-
tered tetrarch was roused to such wild excitement
by the Salome dance that he gave a fresh human
head in payment, it must have been something of
a dance. I should say the lady was in .the air
most of the time, and only struck the floor for
meals. I don’t think the original hop was at all like
Miss Grey’s wiggle-wagglings. When the curtain
goes up, revealing a twilight stage with burning
braziers and an ancient Hebrew or Sanscrit floor-
rug, you sit up, stiffen your moral spine and pre-
pare for shocks. You really desire shocks, but
hope they won’t be too severe or in any wise in-
artistic. Half of what “ fetched ” Herod will
serve you, for you aren’t prepared to give the lady
the head of Rev. George Walters. On comes Miss
Grey, a tall, slight damsel wearing an incredible
number of chiffon scarves. She dances as lightly
as a thing of air and indiarubber. No fault is to
be found with her graceful lightness. In her cat-
like pounces and skips she sheds One by one many
of her chiffon drapings; but to the last sheseems to
retain as -much clothing as a few of our Directoire-
gowned women wore at recent festivities. Indeed,
there was a lady at the late A.J.C. races who
would have made Herod’s eyes bulge in Galilee.
She wore obviously one long, costly satin gar-
ment, and—after all, the Directoire cult is based
on naturalness and gets as close as fashion may to
the nude. Miss Grey’s dance is a meaningless
series of; gyrations, and even when she retrieves
the prop“erty head and nuzzles it on the old Coptic
rug your\>ulse doesn’t jump half a beat. The
head, witUjts matted hair and beard, looks as if
it might be ’ like the original. John Baptist
doubtless had no chance of engaging a barber
while he was in gaol.
I feel sorry for Salome. So far as the evidence
goes, she had never seen John and hadn’t the
slightest interest in him. In the lightness of her
heart and her feet she danced before her step-
father, and the old man bade her name her reward.
She had visions of buying the best spring hat to
be found at Anthony Hordern’s in Caesarea and
selecting a bangle at Stewart Dawson’s in Tyre.
She went to consult her mother about the bangle,
and that fierce, middle-aged woman made her ask
for the wet, staring head of an apostle whom she
didn’t know from a crow. So she got no hat and
no bangle, and the blood off the head spoiled her
second-best school frock ; and that night Salome
cried herself to sleep and. refused to be comforted.
Yet she had to do the evil deed for fear of being
spanked with the cedar of Lebanon.
To make a real Salome dance you want a two-
storey stage. The upper storey should be a great,
richly-decorated room in the palace, with the
tetrarch loafing wearily on his throne and his
court around. (Why shouldn’t Mr. Rickards
appear as Harry Antipas ?) Then Salome should
dance before Henry, and the thing would look
real. The lower storey should be a gloomy cell
containing (1) a loaf, (2) a jug of water, (3) a heap
of straw, (4) John. Then the soldiers should
come down and behead John in a proper historical
manner.
Miss Athmore was here a few years ago—five or
six possibly—as Jennie Grey, and those who
remember her Carmen dance (clad in black and
scarlet and with a cherry in her mouth) wish very
much to see her in the same again. There never
was a more graceful high-kicker. The Carmen
dance would have got her anybody’s head; the
Salome dance wouldn’t.
The child, the foolish virgin and the vacant-
minded married woman who used to adore the
Teddy bear and transport it hither and thither,
are replacing it with a billikins. The new idol
has a semblance of humanity. In fact, billi-
kins is a grotesque human —a queer combination
of youth and eld with long, narrow, slanted eyes
and a huge crescent of a mouth that curls up at the
corners. He is Vhomrae qui rit, and a most
hilarious-looking one at that. Gazing at him one
feels the corners of one’s own mouth going
up, and one’s eyes narrowing to take the
humorous view of things—the billikins view.
There are variations of the billikins, but
all wear the wide, impish grin, even though
they wear nothing else. Mr. J. C. Williamson
brought an assortment of billikinses to his pro-
geny from his recent trip to foreign lands.
J.C.W. searched the earth with a microscope
for singers and players, and would seem to have
engaged everything of note in plays and mimes
for years to come. He did it all in a- very under-
fed condition—hot milk and toast was his dinner
what time he was in London —so small wonder if
his trip left him as bony as his own race-
horses. Every night he went to see a
fresh performance—to inspect it critically
with a view to business. And as acute-
ness of judgment is not enhanced by the
absorption of much food, J. 0. W. got through
his judgment nights on the milk and toast already
mentioned. At Milan, in his Italian search for
vocal talent, he met many young women students
w’ith beautiful voices and attractive appear-
ances—girls that he would have engaged for
work here; girls who lived, not from choice,
on an inadequate quantity of food, but who
cheerfully endured the semi-starvation because
they were meantime “pursuing their ideals.”
This “pursuit of the ideal ” is a tragic business.
If they quitted or interrupted the “pursuit” for
work in Australasia they might miss the rdle in
grand opera that is the “ ideal ” for which they
are starving. So they continue to cultivate music
on a few olives and spaghetti.
The Parbury-Willis wedding transpired at St.
John’s, Darlinghurst, last week after this, my
screed, had gone to press. Mr. Colin Parbury is
a tall, straight-featured young man who suggests
in appearance a quietly-dressed beauty-actor. He
is Very, very lovely and romantic-looking, and
withal doesn’t wear, so I am assured, a purple tie,
a pink shirt, a red waistcoat and blue socks. His
bridb, a dainty, and merry little girl, is a
daughter of the Mrs. Willis who espoused
en seoqndes noces Mr. Magistrate Macfarlane.
A Willis married a Knox in the dim past, and so
the big sugar family was represented in force at
the ceremony, and “Rona” was loaned to the
bride’s mother for the subsequent reception.
Later, the newly-weds took the usual old high
road that brings up ultimately against the same
old Port Hacking edifice, which has housed so
many honeymoon couples in its time.
One wonders often in the above connection
what sort of servants the Arthur Allens em-
ploy in their “Moonbarra” home. Are they
confirmed romanticists, skilled in the ways
of those in the first flush of youthful
affection ? Do they rattle the door-handle tact-
fully ere entering the drawing-room with after-
noon tea, and stumble with considerate uproar
over the dining-room mat as they bring in the
fish? In all likelihood, yes. Else they were
hardly suited to their highly diplomatic job. The
question then presents itself : Where are such
treasures to be found ? The ordinary householder
has difficulty in these days of the country’s pros-
perity, with domestics marrying and setting up
on their own, to find a maid with sufficient intel-
lect and regard for her employer’s comfort to
parley adroitly with the gas-collector. The idea
of setting out in chase of one or more capable of
satisfactorily coping with the exigenciesof a honey-
moon home is calculated to simply stun the
experienced housewife.
TheSkeys, husband, wife and two infants, left
for London last week. She was Miss Beatrice
Smith, a Melbourne beauty. He is what is known
in the King’s Navy as a “ Staff-Surgeon,” and he
performed his daily toil on Garden Island till the
call of duty came and summoned him away. He
is a mighty cricketer before the wicket and was
on several occasions the mainstay of Lord
Dudley’s Government House XI.
The twain were held up in Melbourne on Satur-
day to combat an action for breach of contract.
An ex-nurse claimed £lOO from them (1) because
they weren’t taking her to England ; (2) because
they had sacked her (allegedly) without just
cause. His Honor decided that there was nothing
in the nurse’s contention that she should be
franked to the absentee landlord’s Mecca. As to
the sacking, it was, in his view, more or less
unjustified. Mrs. Skey relied on the facts (1)
that the one-time child-minder had smacked the
children. (“But,” said the Judge with the
Sapience of the much-married man, “doubtless
they deserved it.”) And (2) Mrs. Skey contended
that the nurse had demanded to be called “ Miss,”
|and that didn’t appeal to the Bench as unreason-
able either. The upshot was that plaintiff got'
nothing by way of damages, and each side paid its
own costs.
What I may term a juridical romance is in the
air. A grave and reverend justice-dispenser of
this city proposes shortly to wed with a maid
many years his junior. The prospective groom,
who for long years had worshipped at no other
shrines than those of law and music, unexpectedly
encountered a twin soul a month ago, and with
the hot-headedness of eld (it is a fallacy to talk of
the young being hot-headed ; this is a quality
which far more often characterises the sexagen-
arian) he proposed and was accepted. The wed-
ding bells can already be heard in the mind’s eye,
so close are their feet on the threshold —in a
manner of speaking.
The proposed annual flower carnival is nearing
realisation. And doubtless a “ Wattle Day is
in course of flowering. It may all lead to some
nice girls—really nice girls, charmingly dressed
and with their heads screwed on the right way
taking up positions beside the G.P.0., and selling
flowers until they get married or otherwise make
a fortune. Of course, the “social” objections to
this are many and forceful, but what a field for a
smart, innocent-faced woman with her eye fixed
on the main (which is the man) chance ! Why
should ugly, prosaic males sell violets, flannel-
flowers and roses? And when Wattle Day comes,
who is to be first in the field with the natural
Australian scent—extract of wattle-bloom. It is
a delightful odor, and, I am told, was dealt in on
a small scale long ago by a Victorian chemist
named Watts. He is dead and surely in Heaven,
if the angels smelt it on him when he knocked at
the pearly gates.
The extensive Mort family is in mourning.
Mrs. Mort, widow of the late Thomas Sutcliffe
Mort, died at Haslemere, Surrey (England), last
week. She was a Miss Macauley, and was the
second wife of her husband. There are two sons
of this marriage. Both of them live in London
and are by way of being barristers.
The T. E. Donnes—Mr., Mrs. and small boy—
late of Maoriland, have taken a flat out West-
minster way in London, and settled down to the
work of acclimatising themselves. Mrs. Donne
sprained her ankle on landing, but as the weather
was the color of that famous friar of orders grey,
and as damp as if the friar had got bogged, it was
rather a pleasure than otherwise to be docked for
repairs. When the atmosphere is such that even
the sea-serpent steals an umbrella out of a sunken
ship for fear he may get his feet wet, it is good
to lie up.
That clever girl, Miss Miles Franklin, who
wrote “My Brilliant Career,” is announced as
publishing through Blackwood “ Some Everyday
Folks and Dawn.” It is an Australian story, and
the women in it are among the first in fiction to
vote at an election.
Yours affectionately,
ARENEHI.

brand are undoubtedly responsible for illuminating

achievements.*
