MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 18, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik,—
Caulfield had the dust of centuries and the day
of its life on Saturday for the Cup. Everybody
went out, gasping in the heat and taking in good
healthy loam at every gasp. The Carmichael
lady had washed-out purple roses in her black hat
and wore a faint purple frock; but in this
instance his Excellency was the really frocked
one. His frock coat was a pale lavender grey,
and his tie was a bunched-up petunia in a
vivid purple and white check. He wore a
mesanthebryum or labyrint’nodon or something in
his buttonhole, and looked as if he had backed
Blue Book. Miss Stuart Monteith, waving a green
veil from her red head, was in the party. Also
Mrs. Bogle and a stray Bosanquet. One Miss
Bosanquet is over from Adelaide. Possibly the
twin and mamma and papa will follow later. The
lonely Miss Bosanquet is a dark tropical lily with
black eyes and a radiant pallor. She was fitted
like a glove in a petunia-colored silk frock,
and had her lnur swathed tight in the very
newest way. The new way is to flatten and bind
the hair as if it had just been tubbed. Several
smart girls plastered their hair in. like fashion
under monstrous lids that have the benefit of
shadiness, at any rate. They are as good as a
parasol as a disguise. The inseparable Beau-
champ sisters —Mrs. Allen and Mrs. Thomas—
topped simple muslin frocks with pancake
umbrella hats of black, waving a garden of dyed
pampas grass from aloft. Mrs. Willie-motor Kelly
had the prettiest frock on the lawn—baby lace and
muslin covering pale blue, and pink roses in her
black peach basket hat. Mrs. Ernest Brookes
hobnobbed with the Willie pair. She carried
mole-colored chiffon velvet, with many coster
buttons and several interstices of Erin green.
Jack McComas was in favor as lap-dog. Jackie
loves to fetch and carry for pretty ladies. The
Ham-and-Clegg maidens attracted notice, one by
a parterre of scarlet and pink geraniums on her
hat, and the other by her successful attempt to
look like a Hook of Holland Dutch boy, with her
skirt buttoned up to her blue blouse shirt. Mrs.
Menzies worea dainty blue rig-out, and tried to hide
under a black town hall of a hat. Mrs. Purvis, in
all-blue toggery, hung some beautiful seed pearls
round her neck. Sister Miss Green togged in
tussore and black. The Madden feminines were
not apparent. Papa Madden and Mr. Vallange
wandered in unusual freedom from feminines.
The Vallange youngsters were giving a monster
party at “Cloyne,” and grandma and all the
aunties were throwing their souls into the
preparations. Mrs. Sheilds brought a daz-
zlingly pretty girl, who raised much com-
ment —there was even more comment than dust.
She was in white, with a severe black scarf round
shapely shoulders. A fine white face—adorably
young—just peeped from a bighat weighted down
with marguerites. Mrs. Norman Armytage was
in pink ; so was Mrs. Harry Cornish, ana they
both looked pretty. Everybody racy in Mel-
bourne and Sydney was panting at that meeting.
J. C. Williamson looked happy at Blue
Book’s feat. Ernest Raleigh was a lonely
solo from the beauteous duet, for Mrs.
Frocky Raleigh has decided to stay in
Sydney for a while. What will become of
first nights at theatres and the big race meetings
it is hard to say. A new type of dress paraded in
various persons on Saturday. It has either a
sash round the middle distance of a skimpy skirt,
or else is blessed with a small bow on a carefully
selected part of one’s anatomy. These dresses
must not be sat on —one sitting would be hopeless
to the contour of the bow until an invigorating
iron had got to work on the crush. They seemed
useless and bothersome decorations, those bows.
If they were made pneumatic they would be
comfortable to sit upon.
Talking about the necessary tea of an after'
noon at a Caulfield meeting, the V. A.T.C.,
that most kindly and aggressively wealthy club,
needs a prod. Between the carefully-appointed
members’ tea rooms and the awful scramble there
is no medium. Everybody cannot be a member
of that blissful club, and every woman cannot
secure a personal and tangible member at the
moment when she is longing for tea. So the
majority is forced to the public tea rooms at the
back of the stand* There is no reason why these
should not be dainty and clean and blessed with
an abundance of waiters and a generous supply of
small tables. As a matter of fact, there is a
barrack-like place that beats any railway restau-
rant for horrible hurry and slapdash serving. It is
worse, much worse, than the non-members’ tea
room at Flemington, which is saying much. A
long bar, very few tables, some waitresses throw-
ing wild hunks of cake at scratching women, a
wreck of bottles of chemicals meant for non-
alcoholic drinks and the chance of thick cups of
dubious tea—these are the leading features.
Cannot a club like the V. A.T.C. do better than
that for the public that spends its half-guinea ?
Henley intends to knock things this year. The
rule of the river is to be stringently enforced.
There will lie no more dallying of supercilious
motors when the course should be cleared. Every
mooring place is booked, and the house-boat people
are breaking their necks to outdo each other in
decoration. Every girl in Melbourne is paying
more attention to her Henley gown than she is to
her Cup dress. The Tasmanian rowers are over,
and are lashing the Yarra into foam. Unless the
weather bumps the Henley people, there’ll be a
great day next Saturday.
The Glassy Harem being closed, skate-maddened
youth looked round for new ice fields to conquer.
Wirth, like a crafty spider, had an acre or so of
smoothness ready, so youth rolled there and
fell and bumped to its bruises’ content. The
Wirths’ Park Rink coruscated on Wednesday
night, possibly because Wirth and Co. are so
excited about their opening circus that they had
to break into song or bust. It is a beautiful rink
for those who have conquered the rollicking roll,
and trams and cabs deposited hordes of weird
creatures at the doorway—prize-hunters in all sorts
of togs. A tree-fern took the cake of the evening.
It was a royal tree fern, uprooted bodily and still
fragrant of gullies and fresh earth. It was in an
apparently-pot, and it skated round on human
legs. An attendant arrested it for being so far
from home, and led it gently to the judges. This
attendant also had the duty of handing up the
“most elegant gentleman,” and His Elegancy
had much ado not to show elation. The attendant
refused to take a fee for escorting the “ best lady.”
The best lady was so numerous that two of her
took prizes. A pair of gorgeous butterflies in
silver and gold tore the blue ribbon between them.
The teams let a Gottenberg sextette jump clean
away with the reward. The Wirth rink boasts a
balcony for lookers-on which gives the show a
theatrical feeling. Also the roller rink spares the
non-whirler the risk of pneumonia.
The following notice on a fence in a popular sea-
side resort heralds the summer :
No hot water. No camping allowed. No meals at any
hours. No room to let. No boarders taken. Strictly
private. Trespassers prosecuted. Beware of the dog.
In this city, where even an oyster coming out
of its shell cries piteously for a mayor or governor
to open it—well, in this city a miracle has hap-
pened. The main hall of hundreds of thousands
of pounds’ worth of railway station buildings
opened itself. There was no champagne, no
golden key, no speeches, no nothing. Just a
vague drifting away of barriers and the stupefied
gaze of citizens who were used to dodging raw
masonry and loaded cranes, and who were now
confronted suddenly with a cave of grey stone
and sea-green tiles, wherein sit spiders of ticket-
men catching flies of rushed humanity. That
would be reasonable enough, but the flies, after
they are in the cave, have to traverse a 40-acre
paddock—an expansive flagged field—before they
plunge down the chutes that lead to their various
platforms. The healthy and active can swear and
bark their shins and tear the skin off their teeth
in the catching of the nervy train, but the
fat and the aged, the maimed and the halt
don’t take a train unless necessity pushes them
painfully thereon. As for real invalids, they are
not taken into the reckoning. No cab or ambu-
lance or hearse could get within half a mile of a
platform. Every other railway station in the
memory of ordinary mortals has a roadway that
cabs can traverse, but not this one. Even the vast
entrance porch has a flight of steps as an effectual
barrier to even a barrow ; also as a hidden menace
to a thronging crowd with its feet out of sight.
Layer after layer has been added to that ugly
barrack. Its squat beginnings were unpromising.
Then the addition of mosque-like domes of copper
gave a radiance of hope. Massively severe but
domedly handsome was the structure. Alas, the
layers did not stop there ! Scrolls and dinky
towerings were added to the domes. It seemed
as if the decorations would never end. Plaster
lines and potterings accent red brick facings. A
blind, staring clock tower has a face like a red-
white cow. The expensiveness of the white
elephant has been howled at. “ Well, if you will
have expensive local materials ! ” snort the Heads.
Yet in all the tens of thousands of “extra ex-
pense” the bills show a meagre £3OOO for using
solid local materials in preference to the shoddiest
tender from “ abrawd.” If ever there was a dis-
comfortable approach to one’s train it is the vast
rabbit warren with which the Railway Importants
have blocked up our valuable commercial street
frontage.
In aid of the Melbourne District Nursing
Society, and under the patronage of a Governoress
or so and various gaud-mayoral ladies, there is to
be a Cafe Chantant on Thursday at the St. Kilda
Town Hall. Murmurs from busy society
charmers promise all sorts of fancy dress and
dancing “turns”— amah-tnah, of course. Florrie
Green is training most of the unprofessional toe
in the way it should go, and some dazzling
maidens are letting out the most hidden secret
that Tom Payne of the pantomime is coaching
them.
Melba, at her huge Exhibition con cert, had a
deluge of rain to contend against. But weather
and such small deer make little difference to that
lady when she condescends to warble. Before the
rain there was dust, yet the five-bobbers and
the mere half-crowners waited for hours in
the flying elements for a chance of front-row
accommodation in the balconies. The cavern was
full of people, from the half-guinea pigs in the
blessed shadow of the vice-regal enclosure (red
carpet on the corridors thrown in) to the crowded
two-and-sixpenny seats and the standing-room-
only at the back of the choristers. There was the
usual army of fashionables, but they might just as
well have been Mary Ann or Bridget for
all the show they could make in that vast
ant-hill. The people scuttled in as best they
could, and got out in the same haphazard way.
The rain was like a broken waterspout, and over-
anxious train-catchers left before the rush and,
incidentally, before the last number—as also
did carriage folk who were afraid of the
crush in the queue. So Melba sang her last
encore to the staccato shouts from beyond of “Mrs.
FitzJobson’s mot -ah!" and “Mrs. Gaspipe’s
keb !” Last Melba Exhibition concert was also a
flood-wet night, and some mismanagement planted
the cloak-rooms in undiscoverable places, so
every woman who had not arrived per motor
walked into the audience hall in the full glory of
rain-coat (that smells like onions) and goloshes
and dripping umbrella. Thus a vast and
motley smell went up to Heaven with the
same breath as the song. At this concert
frantic ushers shoo-ed people right and left like
goats and muttons, to ticket off their outer gear in
draughty side apartments. Melba came in a closed
carriage drawn by a Cinderella pair of cream
ponies, so she did not get a bit damp. Her frock
was some marvellous shimmering thing of white
and silver, with a toreador sash affair of petunia
velvet, and round her neck she hung enough
diamonds to keep her from starvation for many a
day. She blazed like a comet with a ruby-
headed diamond snake coiled in her hair. Has
anyone ever noticed that Melba’s eyes are red
—not red-rimmed, but with a distinct red
glint in the brown? Somebody once told ir.e
that Parnell’s eyes had that all-conquering
red in their make-up. One scarcely imagines
anyone with red eyes being meek or tame.
St. Francis’ Church choir—6o of it—was at the
concert by Melba’s invitation. Rebottaro, the
gallant conductor, presented Melba with a horse-
shoe that must have fitted a prehistoric mammoth
horse, and decorated it with her racing colors.
Melba is throwing herself keenly into racing this
season. She is to be done with concerts and such-
like ties in time for Flemington. Having to sing on
Saturday night, she could not face the Caulfield
dust. A pack of Conservatorium girls (“ Cons ”)
made themselves obvious “ among those present ”
by chattering like magpies all through the concert.
Their criticism was a thing that must be aired.
No musical people know-so much as a “Con”
girl, and as for a Philharmonic chorus lady—well,
she doesn’t give a fig for stars. She feels
that, given the opportunity, she would stand up
and show just how that trill ought to Vie done.
Melba sings again on Saturday night—audaciously
bumping against Henley.
Last week, Miss Ruth Lincoln-Hook of Hol-
land took a rest and gave a chance to an under-
study. Miss Benetto, a pretty little danseuse,
fluttered out of the ballet for the star part, and
made the chorus envious by her warbling.
Frederick Ranalow, the famous baritone of the
Melba eo., after jolting over Australia for the
past eight months, returns to London by the
China on the 26th. He has sung at 70 concerts,
and says he’ll take with him on the voyage a peck
of the Turkish swpetmeat known as “ Peace to
your throat.”
“ Baby ” Boyd is filled with the arrogance of
extreme youth. The fever of “ private views ”
of paintings that is an epidemic among our artists
has attacked him for the second time. Since un-
loading £OO worth of canvas and daub on to a
gentle public the youth has worked 20 hours a
day at gum-tree forest. He is showing the
results at his studio, Oxford Chambers, from Tues-
day (26th) to the end of October.
Surely all the artists in Melbourne are taking
turns to give a private view or public exhibition
of their masterpieces. The latest card of invita-
tion is from Miss Esther Mullett (gold medallist,
London), who has hung her pictures at the club
rooms, in draper George’s building, Collins-street.
The gaud-Mayor and his lady undertook to open
the show jointly last Tuesday afternoon. The
press view was fixed for Monday afternoon,
when the thoughtful inducement of “after-
noon tea” was added to the attraction.
All things considered, the tea inducement
should never be omitted. And the mere man
might be glad of a drop of something in it.
A society wedding that managed to get through
without developing a theatrically society flavor
happened last Tuesday. Russell, of the Grim-
wades, and Mab Kelly carried out the process of
knot-tying in a decorous and quiet way.
Only the very next of kin and friends were
given a ghost of an idea as to the place
and time. Scots-Kirk Marshall managed the
proceedings, with the help of Meenister MacCrae,
incumbent of the Toorak Presbyterian Kirk,
where the wedding march was played. Mab
Kelly wore her Directoire satin frock, ’broidered
thickly with silver roses. As soon as she became
Mrs. Grimwade she jumped into Dutch blue
shantung for travelling gear. A couple of
Weatherly cousins and one Grimwade backed
up the scheme as bridesmaids in pink
and white and blue. The Norton Grim-
wades and the Harold Grimwades and the
rest of the big chemist clan came to
see the thing through. Bowes Kelly brought his
girleens in youthful white muslin. Mrs. Mamma
Kelly, in pearl-grey satin, helped Papa Kelly
receive the guests atMontalto, and the Toorakety
house was smothered in white roses for the event.
The bride expended the wedding breakfast on the
waifs of the Neglected Children’s Aid Society, who
were likely to get more good out of it than a worried
over-fed tribe of society guests. The newly-weds
motored to Macedon, where lies the mountain
humpy of the Grimwades.
The young Winter-Irving Staughton matron,
who went for a fling to Colombo with Miss Norah
of the hunting Powers, is back again with her
chaperoned damsel in tow. She had a few hours
in Melbourne to say “Hullo! Mummy’s dar-
lings!” to the small infants who were not wanted
on the voyage ; and then the giddy wanderer
dashed off to Sydney races to air her new
Directoire gowns. A mother's homely responsi-
bilities sit lightly on some matronly shoulders.
The little Harbinson doctor could not abide
widowerhood long. He has rushed into matrimony
with the nightingale who tended his wealthy
wife. Mrs. Harbinson No. 1 was one of the
Laidlaw ladies, who own wealth by the bucket-
load and rich pastures on the Heidelberg bends of
the Yarra.
From “Rosna,” who doesn’t know how well off
she is in Adelaide :
The Commerce delegates, having exhausted all the
revels in the sister States, put their baggage down in
South Australia last week, and officialdom stopped work
to show them around. We let them know we have a
little thing in the way of an Outer Harbor, and a few
wineries that are not to be despised, and a Victoria Park,
where we can give garden parties ; and, moreover, some
vice-royalties who are as good and picturesque and
affable" as anybody’s. There were hundreds of women
and some men "at the garden party, and 59,767
crimson roses hung their heads over the arches
and nodded to their little brothers who came by
the thousand in the girls’ hats. Mr. Herbert Phillips
and a pretty blue-clad daughter had to do all the ardu-
ous hand-wringing, while the committee stood by and
felt so clever to have avoided it. The Exes, were there
with a daughter in a braided pale-pink cloth coat and
skirt, and a large cream hat with pink plumes in it.
Lady Bosanquet’s chiffons were striped and pink and
heliotrope and cool. Then there was the Way dame in
purple and fine silk, arid Mrs. Mayoress Mayfield in black
lace, with a white-muslined daughter.
Next day the visitors u ere sent up to Hill River, the
Angas sheepcote, where they were given luncheon in the
wool-barn, and afterwards shown the blithesome jum-
buck being relieved of his overcoat and his leggings and
his wool hat. Another day was spent at Seppeltsfield
winery ; and on Friday the Government showed its visi-
tors the harbor, which is very useful, even if it isn’t so
ornamental as some other people’s. The same night the
Bosanquets shook hands with the Congressers and other
people at 9.30, and handed them out something for
supper. , .
The American boys have also been getting their slice
of attention. They had a “ civic reception,” which was
dull, and two packed concerts at the Town Hall and
Exhibition, which weren’t; and they helped at sundry
smaller-fry performances, and handed the bulk of the
profits to local charities. One day Archbishop O’Reily
o-ave the vounjrsters a luncheon party, and another day
some North Adelaide folk, the Saunders, had them at a
o-arden revel. And finally, on Wednesday afternoon,
when a youthful heat-wave was grappling with our tem-
pers his Excellency left his cool hearthstone to hand
them the South Aus. flag, which has a magpie laughing
in the corner of it. ”
The G. F. Clelands had a party at Braemar, Mount
Lofty the other day to say good-bye to their eldest
daughter, who goes hence to make some Maorilander
happv The warden is a gorgeous haunt of every flower
that blows, set amidst a forest full of gums and heather.
Some 50 or 60 men and maids gladly left, town in its dust-
cloak to-be there; and the bride-elect and her six sisters
saw to it that it was a joyous revel. .
On Tuesday Miss Guli Hack guided her part-singing
class through its last maze of song at the Conservatorium,
for at the end of the year she will leave them in favor of
Mr Willie Magarey. It was an Occasion with an “O”
like the dome of a cathedral, and all the Commerce
visitors were there to listen to the song-birds. The stage
was smothered in wistaria, amidst which Miss Hack con-
ducted her choir in a pale-green, silver-moonbeamy
raiment. Flowers by the hundredweight were sent up
to her. The angels themselves had scarlet and white
roses concealed about them, and at the end of the final
ditty they showered them upon their much-adored con-
ductress. The vice-royalties, the Chancellor Ways and
the Vice-Chancellor Barlows stayed behind to tell Miss
Hack some of the nice things they thought of her.
There are so many fetes and things to open that some
days the vice-regal family has to divide so that it may
conquer. On Saturday Lady Bosanquet recited the
necessary few words in the service of “Minda at the
Exhibition Building. “Minda” is the goodly charity
that houses the weak-minded children, and it is thirsting
for funds to extend its new premises at Brighton. This
big annual f6te is one that always arouses special interest,
and even local millionaires often spend quite sixpence
at it.
While the Rowing Club was having its first afternoon
tea alongside the Torrens the Hunt Club was shutting up
its season by leaping over Gumeraeha for the twenty-third
time. Society took its fiery smoking steed out of its
motored grange, and watched the pursuit of the wily
aniseed bag, which had a hard run for its life, and was
finally' killed after fleeing 10 miles over 60 fences. The
Crozier girls were in the motoring crowd ; also some
Melroses, Murrays, Cudmores and Hacks.
Mr. Bevan’s singers were reinforced into quite a large
company for the first Arthur Sullivan lecture, presented
to the citizens with the Mayor’s regards last week. After
a crowded audience had applauded its gloves into holes
the mayor and mayoress had a supper party, where the
youngs performers mingled with their Exes, and other,
people of tone and tonnage.
His Ex. is going down to Mount Gambier this week to
look at the dead or slumbering volcano, and generally to
give a fillip to the locality. One of the twins will go with
him, and will stay with the Kiddochs, who grow sheep
thereabouts.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANANA.

Here’s Striking Proof
of a Rupture Cure !
Unsolicited testimony to the efficacy of the Sherman Treatment is about the best
evidence we can give you as to its bona tides. If you call in at our rooms we will gladly
show you the originals of many such letters as the one below. It speaks for itself of a
wonderful cure
By the Dr. Sherman Treatment.
Cheltenham Road, Devonport, Auckland, N.Z.,
Mr. A. W. MARTIN, 3rd May, 1905.
Dear Sir,
Unsolicited, I beg to testify to the relief 1 have experienced from Dr. J. A. Sherman’s INVALUABLE
METHOD OF TREATMENT OF RUPTURE as supplied by you. Two years ago I ruptured myself
lifting a ladder out of a well 26ft. deep. About seven months afterwards I received a truss'and direlotions
as to treatment, which I strictly observed. I wore the truss about six months, but had to discontinue
wearing it, owing to the constant pain and agony I suffered. Fortunately, I saw your advertisement in
the Auckland papers and consulted you, and obtained Dr. Sherman’s Method of Treatment. Although I
am within eight months of 80 years of age, I am now' able to walk about almost as straight as I did before'
I met with this misfortune. lam well known in Auckland and Devonport, having been an officer of the )
City Council of Auckland for the period of 28 years.
You can use this letter as you like, as I consider Dr. J. A. Sherman’s Method of Treatment of Rupture
SHOULD BE MADE KNOWN ALL OVER THE WORLD.
Yours sincerely,
(Mr. Goldie now wears no appliance.) GEORGE N. GOLDIE.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Now is the time for punters to reflect sadly upon
the delusive character of those occasional Tuesday morn-
ing pars in the daily press, which are inspired by book-
makers soon after the publication of the weights for the
big-Spring handicaps. Paragraphs such as this: “Long
Legs has been mentioned prominently this week in con-
nection with the Caulfield Cup. He is an aged gelding,
and has only won a single race during his career. He
has not, however, appeared many times in public.” Or
this: “Stickjaw’s chance for the Melbourne Cup has
been freely discussed at the clubs during the last few
days. Stickjaw is a five-year-old that has been
long expected to win a good race* and his pedigree sug-
gests that t e Cup journey should not be too far;for
him.” Long Legs and Stickjaw and others of their kind
were never likely to see the post for either of the Cups.
The only people who mentioned them prominently, or
discussed them much in the first instance, were the book-
makers who hoped to get a few “ fivers ” out of them by
getting their names before the public. A par. in one or
other of the daily papers does the trick, and some of the
least enlightened of Ikey’s clients make an early invest-
ment on the “mug-catching” gee-gee with the idea that
it is likely to see a short price on fche day.
* * * *
“A Chat with Prince Foote’s Owner,” published
in Melbourne Herald the. other day, threw a pretty
strong sidelight on modern turf morality. Mr. “ Baron,"
it seems, “dwelt on the fact that he had lost Superb
(winner of the Doncaster Handicap in 1597) as the result
of an accident in the Caulfield Cup. Recently his Eng-
lish importation, King Berry, was killed in a race in Syd-
ney. The fear is entertained by Mr. ‘ Baron ’ that Prince
Foote will not meet with a clear passage in the V.R.C.
Derby, and he expressed a hope that the stewards will
give the matter their attention. His colt was blocked
three times in the A.J.C. Derby.” The question is not so
much “ will the stewards give this matter their atten-
tion?” as “ what will their attention be worth when they
have given it ?”
* ■* * *
In the running, as in the result, this year’s
Caulfield Cup was exceptionally unexciting. There have
been others —many others —which left 95 per cent, of the
audience sad and sorry ; but in most of those cases the
race itself gave various promises of popular victories and
a pretty general feeling of hopefulness had prevailed
until the end was near. This year the race came to pass
as sadly as a funeral procession. The roar of the multi-
tude never rose many degrees above anxious silence, save
at the very finish, when Pendil, with a too-late run, was
seen to be rapidly gaining on the leaders. None of the
other public fancies flattered their backers at any stage of
the proceedings, foralthough Golden Slipperand Artillerie
were in the first half-dozen half a mile from home, and
the former was running second at the turn info the
straight, neither of them seemed to impress the onlookers
as being likely to win.
The fact that the time —2min. 35sec.— nearly
equalled Poseidon’s record for a Caulfield Cup was due to
Mindful, who took up the running after a furlong had
been covered, and paced the field for a mile. Punters
knew nothing about Mindful, the mysterious mare from
old man Wilson’s stable, consequently her name was not
shouted. Following in close pursuit were two or three
other despised outsiders, and when the time came for a
very significant move to be made, the horse that dashed
to the front was Trelo Vouni, a 50 to 1 chance. The voice
of Ikey Mo could be heard inquiring “ What price Trelo
Vouni?” whilst the troubled public remained silent as
before. Then came feeble exclamations of “ Kyeadgerie ! ”
and “ Dhobi !” bursting- forth from a broad, comprehen-
sive murmur of disappointment as a brown and red jacket
was seen to be ahead of all the other colors. J. C. Wil-
liamson’s Blue Book had emerged unexpectedly from the
ruck, and was notv in a position to win. Then the colors
of Aborigine (white and red spots), unfamiliar to the
crowd as the colors of Blue Book, sw'ooped down on the
leader, and lastly the brown jacket and white cap, which
signified Pendil, came w’ith a belated rush. Pendil would
have been a comparatively popular winner—but Blue
Book and Aborigine ! ! All the people who had backed
either of the dead-heaters didn’t amount to a 20th of the
number who would have rejoiced over the victory of, say,
Post Town, not to mention the stronger favorites. Here
w'ere tw’o winners instead of the usual single cause for
exultation, but the exulters were so few that the noise
of them hardly sufficed to penetrate the gloom. Caul-
field racecourse had never looked more gay and crowded.
Everything in the garden was lovely except the faces of
the losers, whose name was legion.
Still there were chances of winnings to come.
A steeplechase following the Cup had seven starters, and
the public backed five of them at prices ranging from 9 to
4to Bto 1. One of the horses they didn’t back was
named Secret, and this neglected animal cantered in. For
the next race Irishman started at 6 to 4, and suffered the
ordinary fate of hot favorites. There remained one more
chance for punters, so they picked their fancies for the
Recovery Stakes and handed over the remnants of their
stuff to Paddy Mo. The fancies were distributed
among a dozen or 15 of the starters in a
field of 24. The winner turned up in Marcus.
Out of compliment to Ikey it was said to have started at
50 to 1 ; but, as a matter of fact, Marcus never had a
price put on him. This rankest of outsiders having
beaten the favorite in the last few strides, the public went
home full of bitterness.
Aborigine, of course, bad the support of a few
Maorilanders for the Cup, but the general public was
“ off ” him because his weight w ; as considerable, and his
recent “ form ” w'as discouraging, and he had been
scratched for the Melbourne Cup only a few days before
this race. It was reasonably argued that the stable
couldn’t be very sweet on Aborigine for the one Cup if he
had already been scratched for the other. Blue Book cer-
tainly represented a decent chance on “ paper,” but he had
never gone for a 12 furlongs race since he failed badly in
the-A.J.C. Derby, two years ago, and for the job on hand
his appearance had only one recommendation. A mighty
big horse is Blue Book, and leggy at that. A glorified
mount for a police trooper, or a modern majah-general,
he looked as he was being saddled up in the next stall to
Aborigine. The black Maorilander is much the hand-
somer animal of the two, and judging from their finish
last Saturday, he is the better stayer.
Blue Book has been backed for the Melbourne
Cup, seemingly, and even with a 101 b. penalty for the
Caulfield dead heat, his weight w'ill be only Bst. 101 b. It
is not the burden that will stop the big horse, provided
he is as good at two miles as, say, Pendil. Pendil gave
him 61b. last Saturday. At Flemington they will meet
on level terms—Bst„ 101 b. each. The big horse has none
the w’orst of the handicap argument—but can he stay?
The present writer can’t accept Blue 800k —high in the
leg and somewhat common-looking—as a first-class Cup
horse until he has done something better than his Caul-
field Cup performance, whereas Pendil last year won the
Australian Cup and Champion Stakes. He and Trafalgar
are of proved stamina and just now they look the most
dangerous opponents of Alawa among the top weights.
Alawa’ s victory in the Eclipse Stakes last w’eek
was a brilliant performance. Up to now this noble-
looking son of Malster has gone on improving with every
gallop, and is almost certain to start first favorite for the
Melbourne Cup if he gets through the last stages of his
preparation. Sent to the post for the Melbourne Stakes
next Saturday week he should win, for although the
apparent value of his recent form has been discounted
bv thb failure of Artillerie and others in the Caulfield
Cup, it is not on what he has lately done that Alawa is
judged. His manner of doing it and his jolly appearance
after his races were equally indicative of greatness in the
gee-gee ; and it is to be hoped that he will come up fit
and seasoned for the passage on Cup day.
The Westralian racing clubs don’t know how to
use a good thing when they get it. The totalisator is
legalised there —in fact, it is the only legalised mode of
betting—and yet, though the clubs draw the usual per-
centage from" the machine, no less than 39 Noses have
been licensed by the W.A.T.C. ; and there are plenty of
them who tout for five-shilling bets, and some get down
to half-a-crown. At the round of three Goldfields’ meet-
ings last month—Boulder, Kalgoorlie and Coolgardie—
£lol,B42 was put through the machine ; and it is reckoned
that at least £50,000 more would have gone that way if
the shadow of Aaron Noses hadn’t fallen between the
public and the tote. What the clubs squeeze out of Noses
in licensing fees is a small matter compared with what
they lose in percentage monev.
* * * *
“Snell”: Kid McCoy, a lad who may safely
be picked for featherweight champion of Australia in the
near future, provided Woodfull and Co. do not succeed
in having a law enacted making- it criminal for men to
hit each other with gloves, notched another victory at
Melbourne Sports Pavilion the other night, when he
whipped Soldier Rogers, a much more experienced
battler, very handsomely on points. Kid McCoy has
nothing the matter with him but his name (the folly of a
rising boxer taking on the name of a big man in the
game as a noin de plug becomes evident when he has
risen). He is quick, strong, shifty, with spry heels and a
cold head. He has gone over all his opponents to date
with scientific precision. He put a most workmanlike
finish on Mr. Rogers, and is now in a position to invite
one of the best to get into the ring with him.
* * * *
The Melbourne syndicate which is part B.
Jenkin (lessee of the Cyclorama, where the scrapping up-
roars are heard on Monday nights) and part certain Rich-
mond Bungs, struck trouble when it was some way
through the'erection of its stadium for the Lang-Squires
fight. The edifice was to have sprung up on a vacant
acre in the middle of Richmond, but the neighbors pro-
tested and the godly people objected, the property-
owners chipped in, and the Council refused to register
the premises. As the penalty for defying the Council was
only a £lO fine at most, it appeared that Jenkin and
co. would go ahead; but a deputation to Premier
Murray so impressed that tired politician that be
promised to bring in a Bill forbidding all such contests if
a cash reward was the inducement, the amateur being
still at liberty to spoil his fellow’s countenance if he were
able and willing.
* * * *
In accordance with his promise to the recent
deputation the Rev. John Murray, assisted by his curate,
the Rev. W. Watt, lost not a day in introducing his Bill
for the suppression of stoush in Victoria. The measure,
which is an amendment of the Local Government Act,
provides that no “ boxing contest ” shall be held in the
State except by permission of the municipal council of
the district where the service takes place, and that any
permit so granted may be withdrawn by the council or
cancelled by the Chief Secretary at any time ; the rules
governing such contest must be handed to the council
previously; and any constable or John may at any
time enter any premises where he has reason to believe
boxing is being carried on, and, failing correct names
and addresses heing given, may arrest all and
sundry. Penalties range up to £lOO for promoters and
participants, and £2O for spectators. The point of the
Bill lies in the definition of “boxing contest,” which is
“any boxing match, boxing competition, or boxing ex-
hibition, for admission to which a charge is made, or at
which a collection is made” (the Rev. Murray’s saving
clause), “ or at which those persons present are invited or
permitted to throw money into the ring, or otherwise
deposit it in the building or elsewhere.” It is a fairly noni-
prehensive definition, and the Bill, if administered without
bias, would speedilv put a head on all and sundry goes
in the Murray State, whether professional or amateur.
But it is fairly certain that uprisings of the cheerful
youths who paste each other annually at the Masonic
Ball in the presence of the law and the learned professions
will receive the enthusiastic permission of the City Coun-
cil, while the encounters of Messrs. Squires and Lang*,
and the budding pugs who meet at the Cyclorama will be
turned down in shocked disgust. Yet when the "V .M.C. A.
athletes hold a boxing contest the parsons and pietists
who witness it are permitted to “deposit money else-
where ” —in the savings bank, for instance, or in the plate
next Sunday—so it looks as if there was room for a heap
of argument over this measure. Anyhow, the Bill hasn t
passed yet, and there are good reasons for believing it
never will pass. There is a section in the Vic. Assembly
that objects to being rushed along by reverend gentle-
men who are professionally opposed to all shows
save their own. And the gross unfairness of the measure
will shock a lot of people. No boxing-match is to be
held without the permission of the local council ; but
when that is given and on the strength of it a match is
arranged at considerable expense, the permit may be
withdrawn at the last moment in deference to the row
made by a crowd of parsons, or it may be cancelled at the
11th hour and 59th minute by a preacher-bossed Govern-
ment. It would be much more honest for Murray to
propose the absolute prohibition of boxing matches
either for money or otherwise. And, while he is about ft,
he might as weil put down the Melbourne Cup.
* » • •
Judging by the cabled accounts, Stanley
Ketchell put up a good fight against his colored brndder,
chamoion of the world Jack Johnson. It was not till the
12th round that the coon managed to out the white man
completely, and during the interval between the start and
the finish, the little chap seems to have peppered his
bounding black opponent, fairly' hard at times. Ketchell
had learned something from Burns’ downfall, and de-
clined to allow Mr. Johnsing to use his most formidable
wallop, the right upper-cut; and the champion
managed to land it only about twice. It was Johnson s
superior weight and strength that settled Ketchell,
who is said to have shown remarkable skill and excellent
speed, and in the 11th round to have smitten the
champion so hard on the jaw that he was forced to clinch
till he recovered. Then the end came suddenly through
Ketchell doing as Burns had done—charging in to meet
the heavier mass of brown stoush. Johnson backed
away, stumbled and went down, but rose in a crouching
attitude. Then, with a sudden spring, he caught Ketchell
off his guard, and rained a shower of blows over the head
and face, finishing with a left to the body and a couple of
tremendous bashes to the head which sent the white man
down and out.

The first round of Melbourne pennant cricket
finished on Saturday'. East Melbourne, which had the
softest proposition in its battle with South, won
on the first hits by a margin of three wickets and 249
runs, M'Miehael, the well-known East bat, contributing a
fine total of 173. P’ran, last season's premiers, suffered
the degradation of being walloped by’ Richmond by’ 72 runs;
while University, after a pitiful exhibition of batting,
was defeated by Carlton by 217. Collingwood created a
trifle of excitement by raising its previous Saturday’s total
to 84, and just lost to Melbourne by the irreducible mini-
mum, viz., one run ; and Northcote lost to Essendon,
which was 106 runs in front. The debate between Fitzroy
and St. Kilda ended in a draw. ’Roy closed down with
seven for 264, but failed to demolish the whole of the
Saints in the time allowed, the sacred creatures being
eight for 148 at the gong.
. # * *
Sundry irreconcilables of the old Cricket-on-a-
Boodle-Basis party have been opening their heads con-
siderably’ of late. So far the Board of Control has made
no sign ; but it is said to have been taking voluminous
notes and collecting evidence, and in the near future it
may call upon sundry'indiscreet gentlemen to show cause
why they should not be booted quietly out of classic
cricket.
» ■* « *
When the Burwood cricketers dismissed Pad-
dington for 58 Noble’s club naturally followed on, and,
pulling itself together, it slogged up 217 for the loss of
five wickets. But, unfortunately’ for Paddington, this
made no difference. The game was decided on the first
innings, and Paddington went down by 208 runs. The
feature of the second innings was the 101 put up by
Garnsey before Reid bowled him. But the day’s excite-
ment was in the match between North Sy'dne.v and
Waverley'. North declared with eight wickets
down for 367 runs, and Waverley went in and
blew great guns. Mackenzie made 51, Gregory
slapped up 143, and Hewlett came along with 92,
so Waverley scored 389 for five wickets, and won the match
with 22 runs and five wickets to spare. Another century-
maker was Law, of Petersham, who notched 111 against
Central Cumberland, which had retired after making 261.
With the aid of Law’’s century', Petersham amassed 272,
and thus won out by 11 runs. VVhich was a nice close game.
For the rest, Sydney downed Gordon by 91 on the first go
and Middle Harbor accounted for University by 16 on the
innings.
* * • *
It was a mixed day, and although some batsmen
did well, the bowlers had something of a carnival. Smith
of Burwood took six Paddington men for 32 runs. Proud
of Gordon took five Sy’dney wickets for 27, and Reed
assisted him with four for 30 ; but on the other side,
Finneran of Sydney took five Gordon wickets for 63
runs. Stack of University’ downed five Middle Harbor
wickets for 57, and Finneran in a second innings between
Gordon and Sydney took four Gordon wickets for 46.
*‘* * *
The chief lesson of the National Rifle Associa-
tion’s meeting at Randwick (N.S.W.) is that the size of
the bull’s eye must be reduced. The way the bovine
optic was riddled became absolutely monotonous. Evi-
dently the modern sighting appliance has been brought to
a degree of perfection which makes the present size of the
mark equal to what a haystack was a few y’ears agone.
But it was not only at the fixed targets that the
shooting showed a marvellous improvement. The “ run-
ning man ” came in for a bad time. In the “Hutton,”
with 10 shots at 300yds., nine shooters banged the object
with every bullet ; and in the “ Philip Charley ” 10
shooters got home with every bullet every time the ob-
ject came into view; while in the “Finn” eight men
pinked it every time it bobbed up.
* * * ■*
“The King’s,” with a first prize of £lOO, was
the tit-bit of the Jubilee meeting. In the first stage, at
300yds. nine “possibles” were put up; at 500yds. 56
riflemen put all their shots into' the bull’s eye ; and at
600vds. 13 more “possibles” were added. The second
stage, at 600yds., saw seven “possibles” go up; but at
700yds. no rifleman found the bull's eye with all his
shots. The final stage—Booyds., 900yds, 1000yds.—
found the hole in the marksmen’s reputations, and
only’ in a few instances were the maximum points
scored. Out of all this bustle T. B. Roberts (S.A.) and
S. A. Kefford (N.S.W.) struggled through with 299 points
each but of a possible 325 ; but, in the shoot-off, Roberts
won the big money. The other riflemen were close
behind—fwo scores of 298, one of 297, two of 296; in
fact, 24 shooters were within nine points of the winner.
Young Roberts (who is only 20 years of age) comes from
a family which takes a delight in mercilessly slaughtering
the cow’s optic.
In the club matches, it was Parramatta (N.S.W.)
R.C.’s gala day. The crack team from the worn-out town
gathered in “ The Sargood,” “ Governor-General’s Cup,”
“The Harris Gold Cup,” “The Longfield Aggregate”
(which carries the A.N.A. £l5O trophy), besides narrowly
missing much other ornamental dross.

That Aviation Carnival at Rheims (France) was
the birth of a new sport. Now “ flying carnivals are
being organised all over the world. One is to take place
at Wembly Park, in England, where Blenot and Lieu-
tenant Latham will meet in a series of three i ma, ®hes,
the hope of demonstrating whether the mono P
is the superior air-horse. At Rheims there w ere
biplanes than single-decked machines in use, andl natu-
rally they carried off the majority of the prizes,
other hand, there seems to have been nothing that
the biplane did that the monoplane couW not also
accomplish. The great argument in favor of the mo ''°-
plane is that it is the nearest imitation of the bird, ihe
bird is monoplane every time. Looks as if, ini tnei near
future, these meetings will be as common as those de ot
to horse-racing, and that the bookmaker will lay the odds
i> the same old evil way, unless the tote supersedes him.
* * *,
As a long-distance traveller the aeroplane has
arrived, if that yarn of Forbes, the Yankee is true.
Forbes is said to have flown from St. Louis, in far Mis-
souri, to Richmond, in old Virginia, a distance of 731
miles, in 19J hours, or at an average speed of a trifle over
28 miles an hour. This is easily a world record.
The Arnsts, cyclists and scullers, are a mighty
family. No less than six of the brothers have nominated
for the Timaru to Christchurch (M.L.) road scurry,
booked (police permitting*) for October 30. Dick the boat-
pusher and Jack the pedaller will be on scratch, ana tne
other four will be strewn among* the front-markers.
* * * *
On Saturday last Vice-Commodore Norman
Murray and his merry men of the Sydney Prince Alfred
Yacht Club got to sea in a wild and headed
for where the white horses browse in Rose Bay. There
also was gathered together the Sydney Amateur Sailing*
Club. When the signal to dress ship blew aloft the
waters were transformed, and in a flash the bay was gay
with bunting. Even the snorting, puffing motor launches
were decorated to suit the occasion. As soon as the
water was bright with streamers the Commodore flew the
welcome signal “ Come on hoard ! ” They came. The
flagship was fairly hemmed in and its decks taken by
storm, and above the strains of music was heard the
fusillade of the marines. It was a great day.
* * * •
At the Prince Alfred Y. C.’s opening dinner,
Norman Murray proposed that the next season all the
sailing and rowing and motoring bodies should join to
make one vast . demonstration that would stagger
humanity ; and that, after humanity was staggered, the
whole force of men afloat in all capacities should be
formed into a naval reserve and do some training work
which w'ould make it of value when the day of invasion
and tribulation arrives.
* * * *
The Sydnev Roval Yacht Squadron has nomi-
nated the beautiful Awanui as challenger for the Sayon-
ara Cnp ; so Mr. Saxton’s craft will proceed to Port
Phillip and make an effort on behalf of Sydney to regain
the drinking vessel which, as everybody knows, was
originally subscribed for in the Ma capital. This will be
the Savonara’s third appearance as defender; and there
is an added interest this time as challenger and defender
were designed and built by the same man.
Hagney, who used to be a mighty wheeler
before the gods, is now a sculler of some fame, having
defeated big Ben Thoroughgood, and he is out after the
scalp of George Day—he who rowed the sensational race
w'ith Harry Pearce. Day has replied that. Barkis is
willin’ provided the race comes off at Woodburn, on the
Richmond River, on the 30th, and further states that he
can find £lOO for a side wager. If Thoroughgood is any-
thing of a judge, by the wav, Hagney is a coming cham-
pion who will one day give even the terrible Arnst all he
requires.
P. C. Ivens, who last week won the Victorian
sculling championship just howhe liked from A. Richards,
is a wonder and a phenomenon. This is the eighth time
he has demonstrated that he is the boss sculler of Vic-
toria, always winning with good margins. His latest
margin was 10 lengths.
The Touring Club of France has reached the
high-water mark in membership, with no less than
120,000 on its roll. The French Government recognisesit
as the representative of people who go about in
motor-cars, aeroplanes, or any other strange devices for
locomotion. Tt is.a name to'conjure with, and when a
member produces his badge, hotel-keepers fall down
and worship and the fellows who live on tips abase them-
selves before him, for to be written down as unsatisfac-
tory by the great club is to court extinction.
THE CUP AND THE CRUISER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
H.M.S. Cambrian left Sydney this morning: for South
Australia. She will make a short stay at Port Adelaide,
and then visit Melbourne. The Cumbrian will be the
only warship in Victorian waters during: Cup time. The
Powerful will be detained at Sydney refitting: a shaft, and
the Encounter is undergoing; an overhaul at Sydney.—
News item.
The lean, grey cruiser southward flies when hot November
comes,
And high noon finds her far at sea and steaming at
her top ;
The clear-voiced, lilting bugles blow —the rousing battle-
drums
At frequent intervals cheer up the floating lighting-
shop.
The Bulldog Boys trot round the decks with wide, ex-
pansive smiles
And polish up the hooded guns as if the angry foe
Was due to open fire within the next few hurried miles-
But, as they work, the whisper runs, “ Well, tell us
what yer know ! ” \
The grim , lieutenant on the bridge tramps ever up and
down ;
The silent helmsman spins the wheel and sternly looks
ahead ;
The thoughtful captain wears a dark and formidable
frown,
As though his warlike spirit were communing with the
dead ;
An atmosphere of strain and strife the peaceful spirit
feels
As southward on her lonely course the warship reels
and dives,
Till through the wind’s low crooning and the engines’
anthem steals
This little bit of comfort: “ Yes, I backed the thing at
fives ! ”
Deep down below the water-line among the sleek, grey
slaves
Whose journey song unto the stars by night and day
goes up,
The voices of the engineers who watch below the waves
Blend with the engines’ Song of Steel some notes about
the Cup ;
And deeper still, below the gleaming, steel-floored engine-
room,
Strange human murmurs still are heard around the
furnace doors.
And, like a prophet speaking in the shadows of a tomb,

at fours ! ”

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammatioa is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

ache, Neuralgia, Colds, Bronchitis, Asth-
ma. W rite for booklet free, or send

The Inky Way.
The cow leaves a very large, muddy hoof-print on the
journalism of the dairying districts, liere are some head-
ings, culled from one page of a paper which moos in a
cow locality: “The Profitable Cow—Statement by the
Dairy Expert,” “Cooked Food v. Raw Food for Stock,”
“ To Keep Clean Cows,” “Scours in Calves,” “ Increasing
Prevalence of Animal Tuberculosis,” “ A Test for Milk.”
And even after all that came “ The Story of Two Cows.”
It wasn’t a pretty little nursery tale, but a dry narrative,
full of fodder and facts, and it proved in a grim, statistical
way how the good, upright cow made a profit at the end
of the year; while the bad, idle cow owed several shillings
for board and lodging. The historic cow that jumped
over the moon was probably a frivolous beast whose per-
centage of butter-fat was disgracefully low.
*** " *
Hobart Daily Post, after running as “ Liberal ” for more
than a year, has now definitely gone over to Labor. The Tas-
manian Labor party, which is also the Opposition in the
Flyspeek’s Assembly, has acquired, or is acquiring, or is
advising its supporters to acquire, an interest in the paper
sufficient to control its policy and keep the Post firmly
planted in Labor principles. Jack Nettlefold, the founder
of the Daily Post, acting, so it is said, on the advice of
Attorney-General (now Judge) Nicholls, started out with
the intention of catering for and capturing the huge
crowd of “Democratic Liberal” voters that was alleged
to exist somewhere in Tasmania; but a year’s experience
and a general election has convinced the shrewd ex-tailor
that there is no crowd of “ Liberal Democrats ” in the
Island—that all the voters are either Laborites or Tories,
with nothing between but a barb-wire fence whereon no
man may roost in comfort. So Nettlefold wasted no time in
belaboring his “ Liberal Democratic League ” foes for
having failed him so miserably at the general elections ;
after which he flung the idol into the woodshed. Labor-
member Woods’ Clipper, the Labor weekly, is to be
absorbed in the new venture, and the Labor daily
promises to do its best to mortally offend the Tory
Mercury at least once during every 24 hours.
* * * *
Broken Hill—which runs the only union-owned daily
in Australia—will also be the first place in Australia,to
have a Journalists’ Association registered under the
Trades Union Act. The local inky-wayfarers formed the
usual non-committal association a few' months ago; but
after a while the members sat down and wondered what
>vas the use of it all. And after they had thought for a
while longer they resolved tp be a straight-out trades-
union and to affiliate with the Trades Hall.
* * * *
The hand of “Hamer” is rocking the cradle of The-
Clf.rk, the latest infant of the Sydney United Clerks’
Union. The child—a promising youngster—is to be
trained up mainly to “keep members of the union who
are employed outside Sydney in touch with its proceed-
ings ” ; and, for a start, it is going to have its little say on
the third, Saturday of every month. A child could talk
oftener than that without being sat upon by even the
sternest parent; but talk is money in this case, and just
at present the clerk hasn’t much money to spare. But
The Clerk (in larger letters) is going to try to improve
matters in that respect among others.

“X.P.D.”: While telling the clerks to “buck up”and
barrack for decent pay, as other working men have done,
Tiik Bulletin might put in a word for pressmen. Twenty-
five-bob-a-week reporters (fair stenographers, some of
them, arid considered good enough to do the rounds)
were common enough in Brisbane a few' years ago—and
are still, for aught I know. I knew an educated man who
edited a daily in a Queensland town for £2 a week. Three-
ten is about high-w’ater mark for editors in average
Queensland country towns. Divide these weekly rates
by the average number of hours per week that a pressman
works, and see whether it pays best to be a medium
journalist or a good shoveller.

“Sr”: I wish to claim the championship sash, wreath
or medal as the most rejected contributor to The Bul-
letin. Here is my latest performance : I have sent in 53
consecutive pars., and 53 times my copy has gone to its
wicker cemetery. I have spent 4s. 3Jd. in stationery and
2s. in stamps. I have spent 499 minutes w’riting my
brilliant MSS. and bought 10 Bulletins, costing 55.,
to see if any of it had reached print. I have
spent 120 minutes looking through the pages—mined
through the wild cat column, swagged through the
aboriginally column, investigated society notes, and
even inquired of the Red Page, sporting notes, ladies’
letters and sub-leaders for publication. lam prepared to
pay £37 to any contributor who can show a better record
of declines. I calculate my time with the above
exes, at £9 17s. s£d., and I demand that the
directors of The Bulletin, should take my case into
consideration, and, after taxing my Costs, remit payment.
If this par does not appear, I will send along 14 pars per
week for three years. If the staff refuses to w’ade through
these 2184 intended contributions, I will brief George
Reid—another reject—to sue the paper for £12,000
damages. I know' “Kodak’s” and “Hop’s” names by
sight, but not to speak to, and I ask their united votes
and influence in my interests.

“F.J.W.” : Brethren, keep off Maoriland country jour-
nalism. Sometime ago I “wrote” myself through the
North Island, and—W’ell, here’s a fair sample. 1 struck a
towm that boasted a daily, as most M.L. towns do, and
the editor, w'ho was ancient enough to draw an old-age
pension, told me there was absolutely no chance for a
line. The proprietary was a limited co., and I soon
found the chairman of directors. He was the local
butcher. I explained my case to him, but he told me
they w'ere cutting down expenses. They had dispensed
with their real editor some months ago, and the book-
keeper had had the editorial job tacked on to his duties,
which included everything barring delivery, and the
butcher’s dog did most of that. I offered to carve him out
enough leaders to do for two months, or three years, or
anything. Well, there was a meeting of directors that
afternoon, and the butcher would submit my offer. As a
result, I waited on the tripe man again, and was
informed that they regretted they had no use for
leaders. In fact, they were overstaffed, and thought
of sacking the dog. “ Still,” he continued, stroking a
suspicious-looking sausage, “ we would like to do some-
thing for a brother journalist, an’, as Mr. is address-
in’ the electors to-night, you can go an’ do his speech.”
I “ took” the candidate, and handed in two columns to
the sub. (who w'as everything barring the dog), and the
old chap passed me out two half-crowns. He seemed to
think they had treated me extra-well, too
THE WORLD-SYMPATHISER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sorrows of Peru,
Where Libel’s “imperilled,”
Draw tears as thick as glue
From Sydney’s Moaning ’Erald.
She weeps with woe sincere—
She scolds with vim and zest;
The brutal Cossacks hear
In far Sclavonia West !
Her righteous wrath hath wings
And flieth far away ;
She writeth down the things
Bad men do day by day !
Right boldly she attacks
The fiends who hold in thrall
The dear, delightful blacks
Of sunny Senegal !
And oh ! her heart is sore,
And ah ! her “ leader ”’s long
Upon the sinful war
In Timbuctoo or Bhong.
With pain her bosom throbs
As if ’twould break in two,
When she thinks on the jobs
Done in Kalamazoo 1
The treatment of the blacks
Or yellows, browns or reds,
Makes Grandma’s sorrow wax
Till o’er the world it spreads.
Her sympathy exudes—
Her wrath each “ w'rong ” excites
For nudes and semi-nudes,
BUT NOT AUSTRALIAN WHITES !
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

Women.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GOD be sorry for women, if there’s a God
who listens !
Sorry for rose-faced girls, the blossoms
that care makes sweet;
And for the evil women, the jaded, weary women,
Who drag their hopeless footsteps at night
through the flaring street.
God be sorry for women, for nobody else is sorry;
Themselves to blame if they falter from virtuous
paths of men.
If ’tis God who made them clinging and God
who made them loving,
Surely the One who made them must go griev-
ing because of them !
Think of the little babies, with eyes so clear and
trusting,
The tiny maids who are nestling so close to the
mother’s heart ;
And think of the awful trading, the beauty buy-
ing and selling,
The price men put on women in the crush of the
Marriage Mart!
Oh! if we could see our babies, our dear white,
dimpling daughters,
Setting their hopeful dancing feet the way of
the grievous end,
Would we not pray Death came early, and think
that the daisied sleeping,
The grave in the grassy hollow, was the
maiden’s truest friend !
But no mother’s love can shield them ; they lean
from their world’s wide window
Tip-toe, so gladly peering at the land of a
woman’s power,
And the Man comes strolling idly to see the bud-
face smiling,
And reach his hand to grasp it, the prize of the
full-blown flower !
God have mercy upon them, the drudge or the
fair Queen-woman
All red-mouthed with her laughter, a-sparkle
with gold and gem,
For only when brown earth covers the limbs that
were praised by lovers.
Should hearts that are wise in women be no
more sorry for them !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
One of Kidston’s Great Deeds.
Certain southern papers which support the Fused
Federal Government claim that one reason for
Kidston’s success (if it is a success to get back to
office with practically no followers of his own,
and to hold his job bv the grace of Philp)
was the agreement whereby the Common-
wealth is to pay the State Treasurers 255. per
inhabitant per annum for ever. Kidston, as one
of the gigantic intelligences which fixed up that
bargain, is said to have impressed Queensland,
and that country, it is alleged, has recognised in
him a true statesman. If these thing are so,
Queensland is the guileless purchaser of a gold
brick. Even if the agreement is ratified, that
State, like its neighbors, will one day get the
surprise of its life ; but, as it happens, there is
very little chance that the wretched thing will
be ratified. Already the Fused Administration
has been driven to hold caucus meetings, at which
Prime Minister Deakin has almost shed his
garment of affability in his efforts to coerce his
revolted followers, and a committee has even been
appointed to interview the insurgents individu-
ally, and grease them, and argue with them, and
talk them to death, and implore them not to do
this evil thing. And it has all been to no
purpose. The agreement, regarded as a per-
manent affair, is too hopeless. It is right enough
for the present; that is to say, that while Aus-
tralia is a thinly peopled country which lives
largely by growing wool and digging up gold—
while it manufactures little, borrows more money
per head than any other country on earth, and
imports more per head than any other
country on earth, paying for its imports
largely with its gold and its loans—its
tariff revenue is huge and out of all reason, and
the Commonwealth Government can pay the
State Treasurers 255. per inhabitant per annum,
and still be a vast deal better off than it was
under the Braddon clause. But when population
has doubled or trebled, the income from wool will
represent less wealth per head ; the gold will
represent less per head—much less, for the mines
will be wearing out; and the loans will represent
much less per head, for even if a State with
1.500,000 people can borrow £3,000,000 a year, the
same State, when it has 4,000,000 people, won’t
be able to borrow £12,000,000 a year—quite pos-
sibly it will owe so much that it won’t be able to
borrow anything. Therefore the country will
have to manufacture more and import far less,
and its tariff revenue will be so much less
per head that to pay 255. annually per in-
habitant to the States will be wholly impos-
sible. Germany, with all its wealth and
its high tariff, raises only about 15s. 2d. per
inhabitant by customs find excise; Denmark, a
very rich country, raises 21s. lOd. ; the United
States, 295. 6d. ; and heavily-taxed Great Britain,
31s. sd. With these examples in view it is quite
plain that when this country settles down to a
normal condition the payment of 255. per head
annually to the State Treasurers out of the tariff
revenue will be a mere whirling joke. The Fused
Government can’t but be aware of this, and there-
fore its proposed arrangement is a mere political
confidence trick. In return for some assistance
from the States to help its rocky finances in
the immediate present it is willing to promise
them something utterly impossible in the re-
mote future, when the Fused Government will
be dead and gone and someone else will have to
face the problem. The goods can’t be delivered.
The agreement may be put in the Constitution ;
but evqn if it is put in the Constitution 20
times over, and welded on and nailed down
and clamped and riveted, a time must come
when the States won’t get their 255. per
head, just because they cant —because there
is no blood in stones, and it is impossible to steal
the pantaloons of a Highlander who never had
any. So it. has come to pass that many of the
Fused Government’s own followers object to the
affable confidence dodge, and before the trouble is
over there may be a buried Fuse. If Kidston got
any votes at the recent Queensland election
because he helped to fix up the brilliant 25-shilling
agreement he had better hand these votes back,
for they were obtained under a misapprehension.
It’s only the very outermost rabbit that
hasn’t now been scared by some motor-car or other
in Western Queensland. Between Longreach and
Winton—the coach-stretch of 130 miles where
Cobb and Co. has had a sort of monopoly for years
—the chauffeur has entered into competition with
the ancient whip. The coach, however, hangs on,
and gives the passenger more bumps for less
money ; also it isn’t so particular about the size of
the wardrobe you carry. Private persons also
scatter the petrol’s fragrance on the Western
breeze. Kidstonite Cannon, Labor-member
Payne’s opponent for Mitchell, bombarded
an extra-large electorate of scattered shanties in a
car. Bunning, a Western squatter, has also been
travelling over the Mitchell and Gregory districts.
Starting at Longreach, he honked to Winton and
Buckingham Downs, and thence went to far-off
Boulia and back. Bets were made that he would
get lost in the sand ; but, though he often dis-
appeared axle deep he was able, with the use of
tree-branches, to manoeuvre his car over the loose
patches.
Canon Jones, of Indooroopilly (Brisbane),
who has been saying “Lastly, brethren.” for 50
years, mostly in Queensland, has been remin-
iscing. He and five other parsons came out from
the Cold Country with Bishop Tufnell, Bris-
bane’s first Bishop. The party arrived in Sydney
in August, 1860, and went on to Brisbane in the
Yarra Yarra. There were only six Other Anglican
reverends in Queensland when they arrived—
three in Brisbane, and the others at Drayton,
Ipswich and Maryborough. Among them was
A CANDID VICTIM.
MADAME: “ You will shortly be swindled out of a sum of money.”
VICTIM: “ Yes, I know — half-a-crown.”
Robert Creyke, who subsequently became
the first Registrar-General of Queensland,
and whose widow is still living at Cleveland,
about 20 miles from the Queensland capital.
The principal Anglican church in Brisbane was
St. John’s, which stood on the site now occupied
by the gardens adjoining the Executive Buildings
in William-street. The bells of St. John’s now
do duty at Sandgate, one of Brisbane’s marine
suburbs. There was a stone church in Leichliardt-
street, and another in South Brisbane not far
from where the Victoria Bridge now crosses the
river; also there was a slab tabernacle at
Kangaroo Point. In the country there were
preaching emporiums at Dalbv, Drayton (a slab
one, still standing), Warwick (pulled down this
year), Toowoomba (then called The Swamp),
Gayndah and Maryborough.
When the Queensland Government used to
employ private well-borers to tap land intended for
selectors, it struck so many difficulties that at last
it invested in four boring plants of its own. And,
of course, the S. M. Herald announces that the
thing has been the usual failure, because, as it has
pointed out in a thousand articles, Private Enter-
prise can always do the thing cheaper and better.
At least, it ought to have said that, but it didn’t;
in despair, it lias left off trying to make the yarns
it tells in its news columns square with those it
tells in its leaders. Instead, while it continued to
curse “Socialistic interference with Private Enter-
prise” on one page, it reported as follows on
another:—
It is computed that' the Government plant can work
more economically than the private plants ; at all events,
the Government has been getting its work done at
cheaper rates, and the plants have worked satisfactorily.
Then the Herald turned its editorial blind eye
on its own remark, and kept on writing furious
Anti-Sosh articles, as usual.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 16, 1909.
Mt Dear Thargomindah.—
You never know what angel you may be boiling
the billy for unawares. Still the angel sometimes
drops you a hint. The other day this sordid para-
graph appeared in the news columns of the Rock-
hampton (Q.) Bulletin
Herbert Lionel Henry Vane-Tempest, Marmor, laborer,
was adjudicated insolvent in forma pauperis yesterday.
The fees of Court were ordered to be remitted.
That was the billy-boiling. And this was the
hint (you had to look for this in the In Memoriam
advt. columns): —
VANE-TEMPEST.—Sacred to the memory of Mary
Vane-Tempest, the dearly beloved wife of the Right
Hon. H. L. H. Vane-Tempest, Wynyard Park,
daughter-in-law of the late Marquis of Londonderry.
R.I.P.
There is a good deal of mystery about these ex-
tracts. The only H. L. H. Vane-Tempest men-
tioned by Debrett is a courtesy lord with a house
in Grosvenor Place and an estate in Wales. He
doesn’t seem the sort to go laboring in Queens-
land, nor yet to go bankrupt there. And he has
no wife. However, the Rockhampton Bulletin
is doubtless right in its facts, in which case some
of the stately homes of England are in for a sur-
prising social find.
One of our very nicest bachelors has repented of
his evil single ways, and decided to take upon
himself the degree of benedict. This is Carl
Palmer, youngest son of the late knight of that
name. The lady who has had a hand in working
the reformation is Miss Minnie Lightollar, the
youngest child of the doctor of that ilk.
Sweet Kittynell Stewart and co. are having a
hilarious time of it as regards houses, and the folk
who “ go to the play ” nearly break themselves in
two with laughter. Apart from the Widow
Bellairs herself, Mistress Marjory Chard, who
speaks the prologue, is a vast success, and as to
her casual invite, “ O come with me to Bath
to-night”—well, I haven’t met a man to date who
isn’t ready and willing to go to Bath with her
then or any other time. On Saturday “Sweet
Nell ” and her oranges go up, and the house pro-
mises to be a crush.
A wail reaches me from a local lady whose
good-heartedness and avoirdupois are about
equal. She is at present visiting your part of the
world, and has been asked out to play bridge at
some score of houses perched, chalet-like, upon
the heights of Mosman. The usual climb of 90
steps seems likely to work a late reformation in
the matter of cards, unless she can buy an aero-
plane.
By last mail from Bangkok comes a cheery
screed from Bob Lowry, a Brisbane journalist
who, after a successful campaign upon the South
China Morning Post, seems to have dropped
into a snug billet under the Rajah of Bhong—P
mean the King of Siam. The Australian ink-
slinger has been appointed chief law-reporter at
the Bangkok Supreme Court, where he experiences
a pleasing change in working hours, for Siamese
Judges don’t work late.
Siam seems to have a fairly wide choice in the
matter of advisers to the throne. The chief
General Adviser hails from the land of the Stars
and stripes, while the Finance Expert comes
from England. The Lord Chief Bobby is British
also, i’hfere is a German secretary of some sort,
and the Admiral (Siam has 22 small vessels) is a
Dane. The Judges are Siamese, but even they
have European advisers to help them fine the
small brown drunk.
A catty story is going round re the very slight
response made to some 100 invitations which were
lately issued to a wedding. It seems that the
family does not hold a high place for hospitality,
and when a previous offspring was wed it asked a
heap of folks to a very saving breakfast, and
scooped in a fine collection of house furnishings
from the invitees. It was considered that the
thing had been overdone, and this time the
attendance was small and the house cat is very ill
through trying to overtake all the superfluous food.
Speaking of weddings, a second orange-blossom
event came off in the Murray Prior family on
Monday, when Phylis Claudia gave her hand
and heart to Edgar McConnell, son of the Laird
of Cressbrooke. ' The Arch-Anglican Bishop tied
the knot, and there was much rejoicing. When
the victims had fled to the south, the groomsmen
and their merry maids put in the evening with
Kitty Bellairs.
The Newcastle bowlers, who are just finishing up
a visit here, are great sports in a social way, and
have been, on an average, to three teas a week
ever since they arrived. This excessive drinking
has evidently ruined their nerves, and not one of
them can now get within a mile of the jack.
Fourteen quarts of hot tea would upset anybody’s
aim.
The Boy Scouts are out, and it is as much as
Angelina’s life is worth to occupy a seat with
Algernon in the park. These small fiends infest
the land, and have already upset two promising
matches.
Tooth-doctor Moran and Mrs. Moran are home-
ward bound from Europe upon the Canadian liner
Aorangi due here at the end of the month.
Another pair about to arrive home are the
Hartigans—now frequently alluded to as the
Outagains.
The most startled, surprised and angry man
this week is ex-Minister for Mines Jackson. He
took things in an election way with refreshing
calmness, and everyone said, “Oh, he must be
safe.” One Minister’s wife asked Mrs. Jackson
if she wasn’t going up to give her husband a hand,
but that lady explained that there was no need.
And now Mr. Jackson may seek for his lost job
amid the ruins of Nineveh or in some such place.
Next time Pa seeks the electors’ favor the Jackson
family will take no chances.
Jackson’s defeat recalls the fact that he himself
got into politics by knocking out old Isidor Liss-
ner, the then Minister for Mines. Jackson now
sings quite a song about the folly of electors who
do themselves out of the honor of being repre-
sented by a Minister, but I can’t recall any such
sentiment expressed by him when Isidor the elder
walked the plank.
Touching that matter of the Agent-General-
ship, which office, the Kidston person announces,
was offered during the recent election to a pro-
minent business man. The offer seems to have
been made right enough, and to Mr. E. V. Reid,
of Walter Reid and Co. ; but then Kilp must have
known that E. V.R. was safe to say No. Now if
he can stall off the claims of Joey Bell, he will
probably slip into the job himself with a “no-one-
else-would-take-it,-old-chap” sort of air.
The annual banquet of the Pioneer women was
well and truly spread at the Eschenhagen Pie-
shop on Saturday evening. There were some
speeches, and from what one could hear of them
between bursts of melody arising from a stone-
masons’ social held near by, it was evident that
optimism was in the air. There were lashings of
roses on the table, and many pretty gowns
around the festive board, and a half-dozen guests
(one from your State). Mrs. Joey Bell had Mrs.
Clifton and Mrs. Harrington Morris alongside,
and dames Cullen, Bond, Pagan and other
inveterate clubbers were near by. The mention
of absent friends brought up the name of the
former secretary, Miss Edith Dickson, just now
sojourning in Brussels, and everyone wished her
back and called her a jolly good fellow.
Sincerely yours,
MAEIO.
George Hall, a youth attending the Towns-
ville (N.Q.) Grammar School, is something above
the ordinary as an athlete. At the recent school
sports he won almost the entire programme. He
threw the cricket ball 101yds., covered 20ft. 3in.
in a broad jump, cleared sft. s|in. in a high jump,
got over 100yds. in llsec., 440yds. in 57sec.. and
880yds. in 2min. 21sec.; won the mile handicap
from scratch (time not taken), won the 120yds.
hurdles in 19sec., and was third in putting the
weight. Most of these things he did on the same
day. Hall is a native of Charters Towers, and
has lived all his life in the North, where, accord-
ing to the Stinking Fish gospel, the “whiteman
can’t live,” and the youth grow up like weeds.


CANE SUGAR AND WHITE FARMERS.
Dr Maxwell, one of the world’s authorities on the
sugar industry, wrote :—
“7n other sugar-growing countries the
estates are in the hands of extensive
owners, or controlled by large corpora-
tions, which state carries with it the
consequence that a minimum number
of white men are located on the land.
“In Queensland this ownership or occu-
pancy embraces a vast number of
STRONG, PROGRESSIVE WHITE
MEN, who are planted all over the
sugar-growing areas.”
The late W. H. Traill (formerly editor of The
Bulletin) wrote
“ The unmistakable fact is that the sugar
industry in Queensland is only in its
infancy. The pioneers have warred
with difficulties innumerable. Many
brave men fell in the conflict. But,
reinforced, others fought on, and
stormed one after another the entrench-
ments of inexperience, plant diseases,
labor troubles and other minor obstruc-
tions by scores.”
The Queensland Government has advanced
£589,000 to start central mills, which
give the small cane-grower a chance to
make a competency on land in the
vicinity of the mill.
These mills may in time become the co-
operatively owned property of the cane-
growers themselves, on repayment of the
interest and principal advanced by the
Government, out of the share which
they now receive as shareholders in
the communal working of the sugar in-
dustry.
