No. XLII.
FORTUNES IN SUGAR—IV.
By J. D. FITZGERALD, Barrister-at-Law.
Before dealing with the vast possibilities of the
industry by a comparison with other countries, or
treating of the central mill system and its conse-
quences, a word might bo said here —which will
conclude this article —upon the by-products of the
cane. In the annual report of Mr. E. G. E.
Scriven, Under-Secretary of the Queensland
Department of Agriculture and Stock, speaking
of molasses, the principal by-product of sugar-
cane, he says :
Although molasses is of value for a variety of purposes,
its storage, especially at a busy time, and its transport
offer difficulties against its being systematically put to
profit. There was less molasses entered on the returns as
produced in 1907 than in 1906 ; 7,272,905 gallons against
8,373,581 gallons, perhaps due to the better quality of the
cane-juice handled in the former year. Of the output
for last year 520,000 gallons went to distilleries, and were
converted into rum ; 223,573 gallons of that spirit being
the outcome. Approximately, 536,000 gallons were burnt
in the furnaces as fuel; 960,000 gallons employed as fer-
tiliser for the land ; whilst about 2,000,000 gallons were
otherwise utilised, mostly for feeding stock ; the balance
in hand of the year’s production at the end of 1907 was
734,000 gallons ; all the remainder being unaccounted for
— i.e., run to waste.
Enough has been said to show the “ small man ”
what his chances are in the cane-fields. This
mode of settlement is especially suitable for
enterprising immigrants whose families are capable
of rendering such assistance as will obviate the
necessity of hired labor, which is difficult to be
got—difficult because every man of nous in
Queensland is seeking with all his might to
become his own master by selecting land. Sugar
offers these attractions :
1. A small area can be worked profitably.
2. Small capital is required.
3. Land is available in the best spots.
4. Climate and soil are admirably adapted to
the industry.
5. The central mill system gives a ready mar-
ket and a prompt cheque to the planter.
6. The State services are benevolently active.
The crux of the problem of making the sugar
industry a success in Queensland was the legisla-
tion by which the difficulties of the planters were
met. Twenty-five years of industrial and legisla-
tive experiment is to-day at the service of the
cane farmer, and of the immigrant who embarks
his capital and labor in sugar. Upon the decline
of the system copied from the United States—the
big plantation and the colored proletariat—and
the advance of the small occupier, who, with his
family, could grow, treat, cut and deliver the cane,
the necessity for handy mills became imperative.
The principle which is everywhere coming into
play in Australia (a good social and communal sign
—the principle of closer settlement) has been de-
veloped in connection with the sugar industry.
The Sugar Works Guarantee Act, passed in 1893,
and its Amending Acts, provide that the settlers
working cane are enabled to register as a company
to carry on sugar works. They can raise funds by
the issue of debentures, the Government guaran-
teeing the payment of interest and principal, and
securing itself by a mortgage over the cane lands
and the sugar mills.
Up to date the Government has advanced
£589,400 towards the establishment of these central
mills by settler groups. The result has been that
the industry has gone ahead by leaps and bounds.
Queensland to-day not only supplies her own
wants, but exports to the other States of the
Commonwealth, and oversea. In 1906 she sent
2,832,118 cw t. of sugar to
the other States of
Australia of the value
of £1,615,338, and still
the Australian market
is unexhausted, for in
that year £439,000
worth of sugar was
imported into the
Commonwealth from
Java, Fiji and Mau-
ritius. Then there are
the markets of the
world, which have
hardly yet been touched
by the Australian
sugar industry. The
export overseas in 1906
only amounted to
185,000 cwt., valued at
£140,000.
Comparisons of the
area and also the
product of the United
States and Queens-
land, which have
already been given,
may be applied to
sugar-cane. The great-
est amount of sugar
produced in the United
States is from beet,
and not from cane. In
1906 the sugar products
in that country were:—
1906. lb.
Beet sugar .. 967,223,000
Cane sugar .. 544,320,000
Maplesugar.. 11,928,000
And the values, re-
spectively, were :
Beet prod uct.. £6,000,000
Cane product.. 7,200,000
Maple product.. 270,000
• £13,470,000
Thus, without taking
into account the by-
products, we can get at a glance the value of the
industry to the great Republic. We should have
a splendid market for Queensland sugar, after the
wants of the Commonwealth are supplied, and
there is a surplus for export. Britain in 1907
imported 21,500,000 cwt. of raw and refined sugar,
valued at £19,143,000, and if the “preference”
idea takes root in England as is expected, an
exclusive market for colonial products will give a
splendid opportunity to bur producers, and
amongst them no one will benefit more than the
cane planters.
CLEARING SCRUB AT QUEENSLAND AGRICULTURAL COLLEGE.
Coming back to the central mill system, a few
figures will show the position of the State with
regard to the financing of the new policv.
During the period from 1893 to 1907, 14 mills had
Government advances to the amount of £589,482.
A SPLENDID COLLECTION OF A FEW OF THE NORTHERN STATE S PRODUCTS,
INCLUDING CHEESE AND PRESERVES, EXHIBITED BY QUEENSLAND
AGRICULTURAL COLLEGE.
Upon this sum £193,700 has been paid for
interest, and £210,326 on account of principal. The
amount owing in June, 1907, was approximately
£428,500. The Government, in pursuance of the
new policy, exercised the strictest supervision
over the mills. There is a regular official in-
spection of plant, and reports are furnished to the
Minister at intervals. To the “ small men ” it is
of interest to learn that they have been safe-
guarded so far as participation in the profits of
the mill itself are concerned, and they are let into
the co-operative principle of mill ownership.
Thus, where the mills had been taken over bv the
Treasurer as mortgagee, a speedy recovery of the
prosperity of the venture and a change of manage-
ment led to the increase of the number of growers
of cane. The growers were divided into two
classes, the ‘‘shareholding” growers and the
“ non-shareholding ” growers. The former used
to receive in some cases a higher price for their
cane than the latter, and, in addition, had the
advantage of becoming co-operative owners of
the mill if they succeeded in paying off their obli-
gations to the Queensland Treasurer as mortgagee
in possession.
It was soon observed that the increase* of the
number of non-shareholding cane-growers was
the chief factor in the success of the mill as a
commercial speculation, and. therefore, the chief
cause of the release of the mill from its financial
embarrassments. The Government saw that, as
things worked out, those growers who were
guaranteeing the success of the mills would not be
participators in the benefits created by their
presence and enterprise, and that a substantial
injustice would be done them. Certain proposals
were made and, after much negotiation and some
litigation to determine the scope of the Govern-
ment's powers, agreements were signed which
place the “ non-shareholding ’’ growers in a better
position. The intending immigrant should care-
fully note this settlement, for the interests which
await him, ready to spring into existence the
moment he arrives in Queensland and goes into
sugar-growing, are carefully safeguarded. The
compromise was on these lines :
The absolute ownership by the State of the mills of
which the Treasurer is in possession until such time at
least that a mill has repaid all moneys owing to the
Treasury.
The execution, by canegrowing mortgagors, of agree-
ments to grow cane, and the releasing of the mortgagi s
over their lands.
LOADING BANANAS AT CAIRNS, NORTH
QUEENSLAND.
The organisation of new companies after the Treasury
moneys have been totally repaid, which new companies
shall include all cane suppliers, and none other than cane
suppliers; and each grower’s interest and share owner-
ship in the mill shall" be proportional to his guaranteed
supply of cane, according to conditions that shall be de-
termined upon.
The earnings of the mill will be applied (1) to
pay working expenses and upkeep ; (2) in paying
arrears of interest and principal; (3) the balance
will be divided equally between the suppliers of
cane on the one hand and the Treasurer on the
other hand ; the amount due to the suppliers of
cane being distributed amongst them pro raid
according to the deliveries of cane made by them
respectively ; and the amount due to the Treasu-
rer carried to a reserve fund for the benefit of the
business, but will be available at any time towards
the payment of capital or other moneys owing to
the Treasurer in respect of the undertaking.
Here will be found a true co-operation between
the growers themselves on the one hand, and
between them and the Government on the other,
for mutual advantage. The immigrant can see
the advantage of this all round.
The Government is in the position of the trustee
of a valuable estate; in this case, responsible to
the whole people of Queensland, now and to come,
as cestuis qui trustcnt , for a diligent trusteeship ;
or like directors of a joint stock company. They
know that the more they develop the properties
the better it will be for themselves and for every
one of the shareholders. They have this huge
territory of 670,000 square miles, full of natural
wealth, requiring only the application of human
labor and capital to bring forth the marvellous
riches now lying in the fallow territories.
The access of population aids those who are
already in the country —who are represented
by the Government. The Government acts
as agents for the present inhabitants in inviting
the suitable and ambitious settler in Europe, or
elsewhere, to come along to a country which awaits
him with a hearty welcome; which offers him,
not a renewal of the struggle with
and harsh climates from which he escaped, but a
share in the system which places the land and
the money and the resources of the State at his
disposal, to assist him to become an independent
producer of wealth ; which supplies education for
bis children, from the elementary schools to
Oxford University; and which admits him into
its political system as a citizen and a voter under
the freest conditions in any country in the world.

GOVERNMENT SAVINGS BANK OF NEW SOUTH WALES, Sydney.
The Commissioners are prepared to pay Interest on deposits up to £5OO.
ADVANCE. DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending settlers on the land, a few of
the important features being:
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO.
(b) Low AND FIXED rate of interest during:
currency of loan.
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to
31 years.
(d) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
are payable half-yearly.
(e) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
on any instalment date; interest to that
date only being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are:—(l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or w.thout associated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Crant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases.
SAVINGS BANK DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department makes advances up to £25,000 upon city and suburban freeholds and on country lands well
A situated and of good quality at moderate rates of interest.

“Locust ” writes :
Anions' the delegates to the recent Chamber of Com-
merce Conference were several municipal enthusiasts
from small places like London and Manchester, and large
places (municipally) like Bristol. When the Greater ]
Sydney question was broached to them, the Bristol man j
grew enthusiastic, and informed the writer that there ]
had been four Greater Bristol movements, in each of I
which the civic boundaries had been stretched, new popu- |
lation added to the city’s total, and the organised city
services extended to a larger radius. The City Council
of Bristol had put £5,250,000 into docks, wharves, com-
mercial stores, etc., for the benefit of the city, besides
owning art galleries, great parks, gas and water supply,
electricity, etc. The Manchester man proudly said that
he also had been associated with Greater City movements,
which had led to more scientifically-conducted civic
government. His council had put £5,000,000 into a
canal which it managed, and which gave Man-
chester many of the advantages of a seaport town. His
Council also had art galleries, libraries, bands, parks, gas,
water, electrical trains, etc., all held for the benefit of the
citizens, and the profits of some of which went in re-
duction of rates to the ratepayers. When these British
civic men heard that Sydney had 41 councils and five
shires for under 600,000 citizens, they smiled a wicked
smile which might have been heard in at least 40 munici-
palities, and which would almost have drowned the tur-
moil in the Leichhardt and Waverley Council meetings ;
and this explosion was followed by a silence so dead that
you could plainly hear the dropped “ haitch ” of Haider-
man ’Enley rebounding on the hasphalt.

17 Long Years of Indigestion Cured after
all Other Treatments Fail.

AN interesting personality is Mr. Janies
Siddons, Master Mariner, Soldier, Ex=
plorer and Octogenarian, who gives a
graphic description of his terrible
sufferings from Indigestion, his vain
endeavors to obtain relief, and his
remarkable restoration to health by
Bile Beans, for the benefit of potential
sufferers among “ Bulletin ” readers,
as follows :
The Bile Bean Co., Veterans’ Home,
Sydney. Mt. Roskiil, Auckland.
Dear Sirs,—For seventeen long years I was a
great sufferer from indigestion, brought on by
hardship during many years of seafaring life. At
times 1 was completely prostrated with dizziness,
pains InThe side and back, attacKS of recching, sTcic
headache, and other distressing symptoms, and was
often utterly incapacitated for work. I tried many so-
called remed!is in the hope of ridding myself of the
Indigestion, but my efforts were in vain. I sought the
advice of doctors, chemists and other medical men, but
their treatment, too, proved utterly futile, and I continued
suffering.
The curative properties of Bile Beans were
brought to my notice by a friend in Gisborne, who strongly advised me to give them a trial. And
a sound recommendation it proved. lat once procured a box of the Beans, and started on them.
Even from the first few doses i began to feei their beneficial virtues ; the pains in my back and side
disappeared ; my appetite, which previously was so poor, began to return ; vomiting became a
thing of the past; my head no longer ached as though it were under a heavy weight; and my eyes
became clearer and brighter. I continued taking Bile Beans—gave them a fair trial and underwent
a thorough course—and was soon cured of the indigestion, after having been a martyr for seventeen
long years. In fact, I honestly believe that Bile Beans were the means of saving my life. 1 served
for three years in the Crimean War, and am well on in years now, having passed my 79th birthday i
but still very active for my age, and I attribute my health and activity to Bile Beans. If at any
time I feel run down, a Bile Bean puts new life and energy into me. 1 cannot speak too highly of
this great family medicine, and urge all sufferers to give them a trial.
Yours faithfully, (Signed) JAMES SIDDONS, Master Mariner.

South of Gabo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE young gales hatch below the Snares ;
As fledglings wild, uncouth,
Their fierce Antarctic dam prepares
Their flight of fear and ruth.
From icy nests on crags forlorn,
And bergs and glaciers bold,
They flutter forth, for aye to mourn
Their birthplace lorn and cold.
Full-pinioned, at the Tasman Sea,
They leave along the crests,
In shrieking, loud, witch revelry,
White feathers from their breasts.
They scream around the lonely isles
Like sad-voiced restless things
That sweep perforce the darkened miles
Witli strong, far-spreading wings.
From Wilson’s up to cloud-capped Howe
Their giant playground lies,
When on each sprav-drenched harbor brow
The “ Stand-off ” signal flies.
Then South of Gabo watch and ware
The sliipmen as they go ;
For o’er the hummocks, whitely bare,
The cutting sand-drifts blow ;
And cruel rock-knives, hidden, wait
With edges sharp as steel,
Along a coast of Evil Fate,
Each doomed shore-driven keel.
Here lie the dead ships one by one ;
Out here the surges croon
The Federal to her rest-place gone,
The sunken Ly-ee-moon.
Long kelp and seaweed, through the curl
Of combers all agleam,
The floating hair of some drowned girl
In waving tresses seem.
Here, graved beneath the golden sands
And iridescent shell,
Lost sailors out of distant lands,
Unsought, are sleeping well.
But South of Gabo, when those strong
And wayward winds are done,
’Tis all a deep, harmonious song
Of Sea and Land and Sun.
The little cutters spread their wings,
From Eden to Cape Sehanck.
The coaster’s rusty framework rings
The hymn of rod and crank.
The ketches, leaving in their wake
An odor of benzine,
With quick explosions noisy take
Their way across the green.
With wattle-bark and fish and maize,
From five to twenty tons,
The midget fleet goes down the bays,
And seaward daring runs,
With seasoned crews, of twos and threes,
To handle wheel and sheet,
Steal up and down the changing seas,
The fathers of our fleet.
Hard-fisted, lean Australians these
Who know the fickle bars,
The soundings and the mysteries
Of clouds and tides and stars.
When South of Gabo roars the brood
Of all the gales of Hell,
They—long before—for shelter Stood
And anchored safe and well.
But here and there along the coast,
Sea-worn and.salt with foam,
Old wreckage gives the brood to boast
Of ships that came not home.
Oh, South of Gabo—where the Heel
Of All Australia stands.
Their hearts are like the tested steel,
And iron are their hands.
And South of Gabo—where no eale
Of Capricorn they ken,
Is bred by rougher shores and seas,
A stronger race of men.
From South of Gabo yet may track
By sea-trail sternly forth,
The men who’ll hurl Invasion back,
Defeated, from the North.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.

The Little Minister.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A MAN of grit and forbearance, known
at many a bush christening and to the
earth-weary plodders on the outside
tracks. Here and there on the Hobs of the Edge,
where the lean, dry sheep seek the spindly shade,
his name held good among men who faced fire and
blood with both hands.
In after years the scent of wattle often mingled
with his voice when he preached in his little
weatherboard church. To-day the church is
empty, the mountain wind has torn asunder eave
and shingle, and there are times when an inquisi-
tive king parrot or magpie flutters in and perches
on the communion rail.
The Rev. Caryl Ford came to Taranbar long
before the railway had changed the face of the
glen and zig-zagged its way to the heights of
Mount Victoria. The people had a fine regard for
their little preacher ; they came, like the Coven-
anters of old, through sleet and rain to hear him.
His cottage adjoined the church. No one in
the district remembered his wife. She died, it
was rumored, after the birth of their only son.
Only one man ever heard him mention her name.
But even when a woman has been dead for 20
j'cars a man may speak of her with passionate
regret. No one questioned Caryl Ford’s past, not
even his son James. Now and then a wagging
tongue explained how he had shouldered his cross
a year after the birth of his son. The road had
been strewn with thorns and pain that was borne
in silence.
His study was scantily furnished—a few books,
a shelf for his pipes and a chair for his son. On
the east wall, where the sun often stayed like a
burning shield, was the picture of Helen Ford.
It was there so that James might not ask questions
in after years.
The little minister’s son grew into a limber
giant with the eyes of a woman and a wrist of
iron. He flung himself into sport with the
fervor of an athlete, proving himself a born
fighter and a captain of many games. Yet he
often marvelled, in his robust way, on the loneli-
ness of his father’s past life.
One day, while turning over an old portmanteau,
he came upon a faded letter, written in a woman’s
hand :
Dear Caryl,—l left Bourke with Malcolm Stair on
Friday last. Life among sheep and cattle and the way-
back schools was to me inconeievably dreary. .. . This is
no leap in the dark, Caryl. I was not born to perish,
even as a minister’s wife, in the wilderness. There were
days in that heated, iron-roofed bush hospital when I
cried for a flower, not for myself but for the little sick
children. . . . Nothing to see or hear save the bleating
of sheep and the hungry tramps. Caryl. . . blot out
for me if you can the memory of the rainless days and
nights that put dreams of Hell into my sick, lonely
mind. . . . When I have drunk my fill of life I shail
turn again to my son James. .. . And Caryl, dear,
have mercy on my name when he is near.
Helen Ford.
That night, with a shame-flushed face, James
Ford waited his father’s home-coming. The little
minister heard his son’s passionate outbreak in
silence. Then it seemed as though unending
deserts leaped between them ere he spoke.
“ What was there left for me to do? Malcolm
Stair was my friend and schoolfellow. He was
not a villain in all things. His sin was David s
sin. .. . Yet... he tore her from me. That is
the way of such men. It was not good for me, a
minister of God, to crave an unholy revenge. I
was strong enough to forgive and forget.”
“ Forgive. . . . Father, have you forgotten
that man? Can you forget?”
“ Aye, James, as I hope to be forgiven at the
last call. But it was years before I could stifle the
thing that was crying like a child in my heart.
The end came two years after she left me. She
died in Paris. I never heard what became of
Malcolm Stair. He may be wandering over the
earth hiding himself from a dead woman’s face.”
Autumn passed; the first signs of winter
appeared on the cloud-wrapped heights of Mount
Victoria. The shock came one Sunday in July,
when the hail was beating on the panes of the
little mountain church.
It was packed as usual. At the moment when
Caryl Ford ascended the pulpit an elderly man,
with a young girl on his arm, entered and sat
midway between altar and vestibule.
Something in the air, something sharp as steel,
caused the minister to glance at the newcomers.
For a moment he remained staring down as
though each feature of the man’s face was part of
his life tragedy.
The visitor sat beside his young companion
with head half bent. His face held a certain
rugged charm that one sometimes sees in old men
who have lived the open-air life for half a century.
The girl beside him wore a veil of gossamer stuff,
and the beauty of her form was like a flame in the
cold house of God.
At the end of the service, Caryl Ford passed
into his cottage with the man’s face fixed immov-
ably in his mind. The years had not altered it
much or furrowed it with the eagle talons of
remorse.
“Malcolm Stair, Malcolm Stair!” The name
dripped through his brain like an old blood drop.
Was it possible that he had entered the church
without knowing the preacher’s name? Or had
he come to ask forgiveness for an irreparable sin?
When James Ford entered the study he found
his father asleep with his hands over his face.
■»■*■**
It was a bleak August day. Grey rain-clouds
clung to the peaked summits or floated in foam-
like sheets from valley to valley.
Malcolm Stair had failed to recognise the white-
haired, white-bearded little preacher; even the
name had escaped him. His thoughts were
absorbed in the young girl he had married only a
few weeks before. They were on their way to
Bourke, where Malcolm Stair, owner of Glen-
dower and Airlie stations, intended to settle in
peace for the rest of his days. They had merely
intended to break their journey at first, but the
weird beauty of the hills and the invigorating air
prompted a longer stay.
Their apartments at the hotel overlooked a blue
expanse of valley and mountain summits. A coal
fire blazed in their bedroom. She was standing
near the window as he entered, her eyes fixed on
the wet white sky. Health sang in the wine-like
flush of her cheeks. He noticed how the fire glow
danced in amber tongues through her hair.
He placed his hand on her shoulder gently, and
in a flash saw how shrivelled and old it appeared,
lying so close to her beautiful flesh.
“ What are you afraid of, Malcolm ?” Her eyes
caught him so suddenly that he flinched and grew
pale. “ What are you afraid of ? ” she repeated.
“Of to-morrow,” he said, huskily. “Of the
despair that strips men naked. This aged love of
mine is a shallow thing, eh, Blanche ? Tell me !
His pale eyes looked into hers and his lips grew
dry as the centuries.
She laughed lightly and pushed him half play-
fully into a chair.
“ But there is something in your eyes, Blanche,’
he half whispered.
“When did you first notice it?” she asked,
steadily.
“ Yesterday,” he answered.
• * * *
The news flew about the township like a wind-
blown straw. Women caught their breaths, while
the young men laughed. It was a day of rain and
squalling wind. The distant peaks seemed to
pose craftily in the mountain’s silence, as though
a bolt had been flung among the scattered dwellers
along the ridge.
The little minister sat in his study, his slip-
pered feet resting on the bright fender-top. The
shaded lamp at his elbow flickered when the
draught from the open fanlight caught it. The
outer door shook once as though a dingo had
rushed against it. He rose as it flew open sud-
denly and faced Malcolm Stair, half crouching in
the passage.
“ Where is James Ford ? They said I would
find the Hell-cub here. Eh, God .. . and he a
preacher’s son ! ”
Mountain mud fell from his knees and hands.
It was as though he had fought his way through
racing torrents and boulder-strewn gullies. Hat-
less, wild-eyed, he blinked and nodded while the
rain drops fell from his matted hair.
The minister remained alert, as though a hunted
beast had come in from the storm. “James Ford
is my son, sir. And ... your face is familiar.
Pray be seated.”
Malcolm Stair stood against the door, his long
fingers curling like a garrotter’s at his side. “I
will not sit,” he said hoarsely. “I will not eat
or pray until ” He threw out his hands as
though tearing at the throat of an unseen enemy.
“ Preacher, where is your son ? ”
Hand on hip, the little minister nodded and
then returned to his seat at the fire. “I can
almost guess your errand, Malcolm,” he said
quietly. “ Please do not state it—yet.”
Malcolm Stair flinched as he stooped forward.
It was as though a cold breath had blown the
light of murder from his eyes. His face grew
bleak, his jaw hung.
“You have come to tell me,” continued the
minister, “that my son has stolen your wife. For
me the news had lost flavor an hour ago. Calm
yourself, Malcolm. I too have felt the loneliness
of my home, the motherless child crying in my
arms. Ah, man . . . look up, look up. Tell me
if you remember the day ye came like a thief into
my life, stripping me of honor and wife, running
like a wolf witli my love on your shoulder. Do
you remember?”
Each word cut bullet-like. The man in the
passage gaped and stammered as though white
steel had touched his spine.
“In a Paris churchyard the name of Helen Ford
is written over an insignificant grave—unremem-
bered.” Caryl Ford half-turned suddenly and
held out his right hand. “As a minister of God
I offer you my sympathies. Malcolm. lam not
responsible for my son’s act.”
He drew a photograph from a near shelf and
tossed it across the table. “ That is my son. A
fine lad, eh, Stair ? A sportsman, an athlete, a
gentleman. Look at him well.”
Malcolm Stair threw aside the photograph ; his
pale eyes could not see the erect young j o ’
the clear-cut handsome face of the minister sso .
“ Your wife will follow him. Malcolm, until sno
dies. Still there is no reason why you ana
should hate each other.” .
There was no response from the passage, on y >
whining of breath as though from a hurt beast,
Caryl Ford stole to a sideboard and reacnea
down a bottle with an unbroken seal.
“Stair!” His voice was clear as the middle
note of an organ. “A glass of wine with me,
because we stand on the threshold of comedy anc
hate. Let us be friends.” .
The wine-filled tumblers glinted redly m the
fireglow. With his long white hair flung P !l^
from his brow, the minister drank until the light
of youth leaped to his eyes and his face shone with
good humor.
“Drink. Malcolm, drink !” ,
In a flash the other’s glass lay shattered on the
hearth. A moment later Malcolm Stair passed
out into the mountain rain.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
The Pearl Diver.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHERE the sun’s shimmer on the sea
Duns to a shaft of tawny light;
A hidden world lies but for me—
A world of beauty and affright.
Sea shell and red anemone —
Starfish and coral, cup and crown,
Upon the ribbed floor of the sea,
Deep down ! Deep down !
Eager to find the weed-grown shell
That guards its splendid treasure well ;
I search through many a coral town,
Deep down !
Round me the weight of oceans press,
But I am armored by the air ;
The silky seas give warm caress
To tempt me down to groves more fair ;
By copse of Sponge, and madrepore,
And sea flowers red and blue and brown —
I sink unto the sandy floor
Deep down ! Deep down !
Where the dead shells lie, white and stark ;
And pearls from pain grow in the dark ;
Where the sea-cresses writhe and drown ;
Deep down !
By coral lanes and spongy brakes,
Through sap-green waters to the sand ;
Where, in his pain, the blind shell makes
The pearl to grace my lady’s hand.
The shark, the sea-snake, and the ray—
The horrors that the dun depths crown,
I meet by reef and sandy cay !
Deep down ! Deep down !
Yet unafraid —below —ashore —
For death, once come, can come no more—
I seek the jewel for the crown
Deep down !
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
